Allah's Existence

6 min
The Peripatetic Doctrine of an Eternal World
Al-Shahrastani enumerates the Peripatetic philosophers and states their core claim about the world's eternity.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the core claim of the Peripatetic philosophers that the world is eternal, not temporally created, but ontologically dependent on the Necessary Being.

Core Idea
The Peripatetics affirm God but deny temporal creation, saying the world is eternally emanated.

Al-Shahrastani begins by listing the major Peripatetic philosophers, from Aristotle to al-Farabi and Ibn Sina. He then presents their shared doctrine: the world has a Maker who is necessary in Himself, and the world is possible in itself but necessary through Him. Crucially, they reinterpret 'origination' as ontological dependence, not a temporal beginning.

Guided Path
1
Identify the philosophers listed
Read the opening lines and note the names: Aristotle, Proclus, Alexander, Themistius, al-Farabi, Ibn Sina. These are the key figures in the Peripatetic tradition.
The inclusion of Muslim philosophers shows the continuity of this school.
2
Understand the necessary vs possible distinction
The passage distinguishes between the Necessary Being (wajib bi-dhatihi) and the world which is possible in itself (mumkin bi-dhatihi) but necessary through another (wajib bi-ghayrihi). This is the foundation of their metaphysical framework.
3
Grasp the redefined meaning of origination
Take note that they do not mean temporal beginning when they say huduth. Instead, they mean ontological dependence: the world's existence is caused by and dependent on God.
This reinterpretation is key to their compatibility with the eternity thesis.
4
Observe the conclusion about endurance
The passage ends this section by stating the world is perpetual (da'im al-wujud), having no beginning or end. This is the direct conclusion of their premises.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ومذهب أرسطاطاليس ومن شايعه مثل برقلس والإسكندر الإفروديسي وثامسطيوس، ومن نصر مذهبه من المتأخرين مثل: أبي نصر الفارابي، وأبي علي الله الحسين بن عبد بن سينا، وغيرهما من فلاسفة الإسلام، أن للعالم صافعًا مبدعاً وهو واجب بذاته، أو العالم ممكن الوجود بذاته، واجب الوجود بالواجب بذاته، غير محدث حدوثا يسبقه عدم، بل معنى حدوثه وجوبه به وصدوره عنه واحتياجه إليه، فهو دائم الوجود لم يزل ولا يزال فالباري تعالى أوجب بذاته عقلا، وهو جوهر مجرد قائم بذاته مجرد عن المادة، ويتوسط ذلك أوجب عقلا آخر ونفسا وجرما سماويا، ويتوسطهما وجدت العناصر والمركبات، وليس يجوز أن يصدر عن الواحد إلا واحد ومعنى الصدور عنه وجوبه به ولا يتصور موجب بغير موجب فالعالم سرمدي وحركات الأفلاك سرمدية لا أول لها تنتهي إليه، فلا تكون حركة إلا وحركة قبلها فهي لا تتناهى مدة وعدة
The doctrine of Aristotle and those who followed him, such as Proclus, Alexander of Aphrodisias, and Themistius, and those who supported his doctrine among the later ones, such as Abu Nasr al-Farabi and Abu Ali al-Husayn ibn 'Abd ibn Sina (Avicenna) and other philosophers of Islam, is that the world has a Maker and Originator, and He is Necessary in His essence (wajib bi-dhatihi), and the world is possible in its essence (mumkin al-wujud bi-dhatihi), necessary in existence through the Necessary in His essence (wajib al-wujud bi al-wajib bi-dhatihi), not originated an origination preceded by non-existence. Rather, the meaning of its origination (huduth) is its necessity through Him, its emanation from Him, and its need of Him. So it is perpetual in existence (da'im al-wujud), has not ceased and will not cease. The Creator Almighty necessitated by His essence an intellect ('aql), and He is a separate substance (jawhar mujarrad) subsisting by itself, abstract from matter. Through that intellect, He necessitated another intellect, a soul, and a celestial body. Through these two, the elements and composites came into existence. And it is not permissible that from the One (al-Wahid) should proceed except one. The meaning of proceeding from Him is its necessity through Him. And no necessitator (mujib) can be conceived without something necessitated. Thus the world is eternal (sarmadi) and the movements of the spheres are eternal, having no beginning that ends at it. So there is no movement except that there is a movement before it; they are infinite in duration and number.
Source pages p. 6

Commentary
This passage presents the view of the Peripatetic philosophers, led by Aristotle, and including later Muslim followers like al-Farabi and Ibn Sina. The author first lists the figures: Aristotle, Proclus, Alexander, Themistius, and among Muslims, al-Farabi and Ibn Sina. Their doctrine is that the world has a Maker (sani') who is necessary in Himself. The world is possible in itself but necessary through the Necessary Being. They do not mean by 'origination' (huduth) a temporal beginning after non-existence; rather they mean ontological dependence: the world's existence is necessitated by God. God is one and simple (jawhar mujarrad) and from Him proceeds one intellect (the First Intellect). Then from that intellect proceeds another intellect, a soul, and a celestial sphere. Then from these proceed the elements and composites. The principle 'from the One only one proceeds' (al-wahid la yasdur 'anhu illa wahid) is central. They conclude that the world is eternal (sarmadi) and the movements of the spheres are endless, each movement preceded by another, thus infinite in time and number.

Key Terms
واجب بذاته
ممكن الوجود
الحدوث

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The Peripatetics deny God's existence.
Correction: They affirm God as Necessary Being but deny temporal creation.

Checkpoint
According to the Peripatetics, what does 'origination' (huduth) mean?
A) temporal beginning after non-existence
B) ontological dependence on God
C) creation ex nihilo
D) accidental property

Key Takeaway
The Peripatetic philosophers view the world as eternally dependent on God, not temporally created.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
You just read that the Peripatetics believe the world is eternal. Does your heart find comfort in the idea of a world that has always existed alongside God? Or does it feel unsettling? Examine why. Are you willing to submit to what Allah has revealed about His creation of the world in time, even if a philosophical system seems more logical to you?
?
The philosophers redefined 'origination' to mean mere dependence. Have you ever redefined a clear Islamic concept to fit your own reasoning? What would it cost you to accept Allah's words as they are, without reinterpretation?
?
If you were to live as if the world were eternal and independent (as the Peripatetics say), how would your worship of Allah change? Now reflect: In what ways do you still act as if parts of your life are 'eternal' or independent of Allah?

The Emanation Cascade from the One
Al-Shahrastani explains the emanation process and the principle of unity, concluding the world and its motions are eternal.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the Peripatetic emanation process from the First Intellect to the material world and their argument for eternal celestial motion.

Core Idea
From the One proceeds only one intellect, which becomes the source of a cascade leading to the universe.

This passage continues the Peripatetic doctrine by describing how the universe emanates from the Necessary Being. The key principle is 'from the One only one proceeds', leading to a chain of intellects, souls, and celestial bodies, finally resulting in the elements and composites. The argument concludes that celestial movements are eternal and infinite.

Guided Path
1
Understand the emanation of the first intellect
Read that the Creator necessitated an intellect (al-'aql) which is a separate substance, abstract from matter. This is the first emanation from the One.
This intellect is often called the First Intellect in Islamic philosophy.
2
Trace the cascade to subsequent entities
From that first intellect, another intellect, a soul, and a celestial body are necessitated. Then through these two (the intellect and soul), the elements and composites come into existence.
3
Grasp the principle of unity
The passage asserts the famous principle: 'from the One only one proceeds'. This means only the first intellect can proceed directly from God; everything else comes through intermediaries.
This principle is a cornerstone of Peripatetic metaphysics.
4
See the conclusion about eternal motion
The passage concludes that the world is eternal (sarmadi) and the movements of the spheres are infinite, each movement preceded by another, thus no beginning.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ومذهب أرسطاطاليس ومن شايعه مثل برقلس والإسكندر الإفروديسي وثامسطيوس، ومن نصر مذهبه من المتأخرين مثل: أبي نصر الفارابي، وأبي علي الله الحسين بن عبد بن سينا، وغيرهما من فلاسفة الإسلام، أن للعالم صافعًا مبدعاً وهو واجب بذاته، أو العالم ممكن الوجود بذاته، واجب الوجود بالواجب بذاته، غير محدث حدوثا يسبقه عدم، بل معنى حدوثه وجوبه به وصدوره عنه واحتياجه إليه، فهو دائم الوجود لم يزل ولا يزال فالباري تعالى أوجب بذاته عقلا، وهو جوهر مجرد قائم بذاته مجرد عن المادة، ويتوسط ذلك أوجب عقلا آخر ونفسا وجرما سماويا، ويتوسطهما وجدت العناصر والمركبات، وليس يجوز أن يصدر عن الواحد إلا واحد ومعنى الصدور عنه وجوبه به ولا يتصور موجب بغير موجب فالعالم سرمدي وحركات الأفلاك سرمدية لا أول لها تنتهي إليه، فلا تكون حركة إلا وحركة قبلها فهي لا تتناهى مدة وعدة
The doctrine of Aristotle and those who followed him, such as Proclus, Alexander of Aphrodisias, and Themistius, and those who supported his doctrine among the later ones, such as Abu Nasr al-Farabi and Abu Ali al-Husayn ibn 'Abd ibn Sina (Avicenna) and other philosophers of Islam, is that the world has a Maker and Originator, and He is Necessary in His essence (wajib bi-dhatihi), and the world is possible in its essence (mumkin al-wujud bi-dhatihi), necessary in existence through the Necessary in His essence (wajib al-wujud bi al-wajib bi-dhatihi), not originated an origination preceded by non-existence. Rather, the meaning of its origination (huduth) is its necessity through Him, its emanation from Him, and its need of Him. So it is perpetual in existence (da'im al-wujud), has not ceased and will not cease. The Creator Almighty necessitated by His essence an intellect ('aql), and He is a separate substance (jawhar mujarrad) subsisting by itself, abstract from matter. Through that intellect, He necessitated another intellect, a soul, and a celestial body. Through these two, the elements and composites came into existence. And it is not permissible that from the One (al-Wahid) should proceed except one. The meaning of proceeding from Him is its necessity through Him. And no necessitator (mujib) can be conceived without something necessitated. Thus the world is eternal (sarmadi) and the movements of the spheres are eternal, having no beginning that ends at it. So there is no movement except that there is a movement before it; they are infinite in duration and number.
Source pages p. 6

Commentary
This passage presents the view of the Peripatetic philosophers, led by Aristotle, and including later Muslim followers like al-Farabi and Ibn Sina. The author first lists the figures: Aristotle, Proclus, Alexander, Themistius, and among Muslims, al-Farabi and Ibn Sina. Their doctrine is that the world has a Maker (sani') who is necessary in Himself. The world is possible in itself but necessary through the Necessary Being. They do not mean by 'origination' (huduth) a temporal beginning after non-existence; rather they mean ontological dependence: the world's existence is necessitated by God. God is one and simple (jawhar mujarrad) and from Him proceeds one intellect (the First Intellect). Then from that intellect proceeds another intellect, a soul, and a celestial sphere. Then from these proceed the elements and composites. The principle 'from the One only one proceeds' (al-wahid la yasdur 'anhu illa wahid) is central. They conclude that the world is eternal (sarmadi) and the movements of the spheres are endless, each movement preceded by another, thus infinite in time and number.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The Peripatetics believe God directly creates each thing.
Correction: They believe everything emanates through intermediaries, with only the first intellect proceeding directly from God.

Checkpoint
What is the principle that governs emanation from the One?
A) From the One proceeds multiple things
B) From the One only one proceeds
C) From the One proceeds nothing
D) From the One proceeds everything

Key Takeaway
The Peripatetic emanation cascade begins with the First Intellect and ends with an infinite series of celestial movements.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Do you live as though your existence is necessary—that you could not have been otherwise—or as though it is a gratuitous gift from a free Creator? What would change if you truly believed the latter?
?
The Peripatetics say from the One only one proceeds. What does your heart say about the distance between you and God? Does the idea of intermediaries comfort or disturb you?
?
If the world's motions are eternal and necessary, where does that leave your personal accountability before God? Are you content with a God who must create, or do you long for a God who freely loves?

The Impossibility of Infinite Bodies and Motions and God's Transcendence of Space and Time
Author argues that infinite bodies and motions are impossible and do not imply God's coexistence in space or time.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why infinite bodies and motions are impossible and why, even if they were supposed, they would not imply God's coexistence in space or time.

Core Idea
Infinite extension and duration are impossible, and God is beyond space and time entirely.

Sanusi tackles the objection that positing other worlds or infinite motions would place God in relation to space or time. He first demonstrates the impossibility of infinite bodies and motions, then argues that even if they were possible, they would not entail God's coexistence with them. The key is that God is not receptive of spatial or temporal categories. This lesson unpacks that argument.

Guided Path
1
Read the Arabic passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without trying to analyse it. Then read the translation. Let the author's own rhythm come through before you interpret.
Notice the author uses a very compressed style with conditional clauses.
2
Identify the two impossibilities
The passage states two impossibilities: infinite spatial dimension (in plenum or void) and infinite temporal duration (infinite number of motions). Find where each is stated and note the reasoning.
The author says these are proven by demonstration (bi al-burhan).
3
Understand the opponent's concession
The opponent concedes priority and posteriority in the concept of 'withness' (ma'iyya). This sets up the next step. Notice that the author uses this concession to build his argument.
Concession means the opponent agrees that some things are prior and others posterior.
4
Follow the argument about coexistence
Even if we suppose an infinite body or infinite motions, it does not follow that God coexists with them in place or time, because God is not subject to place or time. There was God and nothing with Him.
The argument uses the opponent's own concession to show that infinite things do not imply divine spatial or temporal relation.
5
Note the negation of all 'withness'
The author concludes that all forms of 'withness' – by essence, by existence, by spatial/temporal rank – are negated of God. This includes any implications from the attributes of Creator or Necessitator.
This is a key theological point: God's creative act does not entail coexistence with creatures.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
العالم لم يؤد ذلك تجويز عوالم هي أجسام لا تتناهى؛ إذ قد تبين بالبرهان استحالة بعدٍ لا يتناهى في الملا والخلا وكذلك لو قدر تقديرًا عالم آخر قبل هذا العالم لم يؤد ذلك إلى تجويز عوالم هي متحركات لا تتناهى؛ إذ تبين بالبرهان استحالة مدة وعدة لا تتناهى، فرجع الخلاف إذا إلى استحالة وجود جسم لا يتناهى بعداً، وبيان استحالة وجود أجسام لا تتناهى زمانًا، فإن الخصم يسلم التقدم والتأخر في المعية، فإذا سلم التقدم نقول : لا يلزم من تقدير جسم لا يتناهى بعداً وإن كان مستحيلا أن يكون مع الباري تعالى بالمكان، كذلك لم يلزم من تقدير حركات لا تتناهى زمانًا أن تكون مع الباري تعالى بالزمان؛ فإنه تعالى غير قابل للزمان والمكان وكان الله ولم يكن معه شيء؛ إذ لم تكن معية بالذات ولا بالوجود، ولا بالرتبة المكانية والزمانية، ولم يلزم من إطلاق كونه موجدا أن يكون في الوجود، أولا لزم من إطلاق كونه موجبًا أن يكون الموجب معه في الوجود، فإن الوجود المستفاد لا يكون مع الوجود المفيد، وكان المعية من كل وجه، وعلى كل معنى من الأقسام منفيا عنه، سواء أطلقناها في قسم واحد أو في قسمين مركبين، ونحن نبدأ بطرق المتكلمين ثم نعود إلى ما ذكرناه من الكلام على محل النزاع.
The universe not leading to that [would necessitate] allowing worlds that are bodies without end; because it has been proven by demonstration the impossibility of a dimension without end in the plenum and void. And likewise if one were to suppose another world before this world, that would not lead to allowing worlds that are moving bodies without end; because it has been proven by demonstration the impossibility of a duration and a number without end. So the dispute returns to the impossibility of the existence of a body infinite in dimension, and the demonstration of the impossibility of the existence of bodies infinite in time. For the opponent concedes priority and posteriority in conjunction (ma'iyya). So if he concedes priority, we say: it does not follow from supposing a body infinite in dimension, even if it is impossible, that it would be with the Most High God in place; likewise it does not follow from supposing motions infinite in time that they would be with the Most High God in time; for He, Most High, is not receptive of time and place, and there was God and there was nothing with Him; for there was no conjunction (ma'iyya) by essence nor by existence, nor by spatial or temporal rank. And it does not follow from applying [the attribute] 'Creator' (muwjid) to Him that there must be an existence [elsewhere], nor does it follow from applying [the attribute] 'Necessitator' (mujib) to Him that the necessitator must be with Him in existence; for the existence that is derived (al-wujud al-mustafad) is not together with the existence that is productive (al-wujud al-mufid). And conjunction (ma'iyya) from every respect and in every meaning of the categories is negated of Him, whether we apply it in one category or in two combined categories. And we begin with the methods of the mutakallimun, then we return to what we mentioned of the discussion on the point of dispute.
Source pages p. 11

Commentary
This passage presents an argument about the impossibility of infinite bodies in extension and infinite motions in time, and draws conclusions about God's relation to space and time. The author begins by stating that if we suppose another world before this one, it does not lead to infinite bodies because infinite extension is impossible. Similarly, infinite motions are impossible. Thus the dispute reduces to the impossibility of infinite bodies in space and infinite bodies in time. The opponent concedes priority and posteriority relative to a state of 'withness' (ma'iyya). Once he concedes priority, the author argues that it does not follow that a body infinite in extension would be with God in place, nor that infinite motions would be with God in time, because God is not subject to time or place. There was God and nothing with Him, neither by essence nor by existence nor by spatial or temporal rank. The attributes of being a creator (muwjid) or necessitator (mujib) do not imply that the effect must coexist with the cause in existence. The derived existence is not together with the productive existence. Thus all forms of 'withness' are negated of God. The author then announces that they will first present the methods of the mutakallimun and then return to this discussion.

Key Terms
معية
بعد
مدة
جسم
موجِد
موجِب

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Positing infinite bodies or motions implies God coexists with them in space or time.
Correction: The author clarifies that infinite bodies/motions are impossible, and even if supposed, God is not subject to space/time so no coexistence.

Checkpoint
What is the author's conclusion about the possibility of infinite bodies or motions in relation to God?
A) They can exist but are irrelevant to God's attributes.
B) They are impossible, and even if supposed, they do not imply God's coexistence in space or time.
C) They are possible but only in the void.
D) They are necessary for God's creative power.

Key Takeaway
God is not subject to space or time; infinite bodies and motions are impossible, and even if supposed, they do not necessitate any divine relation.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Do you ever subconsciously imagine God as existing within time, like a being that 'waits' for your prayers? How does this passage challenge that mental image? Will you actively train your heart to conceive of God as beyond time today?
?
When you think of God's presence with you, do you primarily think of a spatial closeness? This lesson says God is not receptive of place. How can you cultivate a consciousness of God's presence without spatial imagery?
?
The author argues that derived existence is not together with productive existence. Does your fear or hope of God reduce to a transactional relationship where you expect Him to 'be with you' in a temporal sense? How can you deepen your reliance on God without demanding His coexistence in your frame of time?

Imam al-Haramayn's Proof from Specification
Juwayni argues from the need for a specificator for possible existents

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand Juwayni's argument that the specification of possible entities to a particular state necessitates a specificator (mukhaṣṣiṣ), and how this applies even if the world is considered eternal in time.

Core Idea
When equal possibilities exist, actualization of one requires a specificator.

Juwayni presents a compelling argument for the existence of a specificator based on the observable world. He points out that the elements and celestial bodies could have been arranged differently, yet they are in specific states. This specification, given the equal possibility of alternatives, rationally demands a cause that selects one outcome. He also addresses potential objections regarding the world's finitude and eternity.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without trying to analyse it. Then read the translation. Let the author's own argument come through before you interpret.
Notice that Juwayni uses concrete physical examples to ground the argument.
2
Identify the components of the world
Juwayni lists earth, water, air, fire, and celestial spheres. Understand that these are all bodies occupying space and are subject to variation.
3
Understand the key premise: equality of possibilities
The passage states that for each body, it could be to the right or left, larger or smaller, etc., and all these possibilities are equally possible. Yet each body is actual in one specific way.
This premise is crucial: 'مع استواء الجايزات وتماثل الممكنات'.
4
Follow the reasoning to the conclusion
Given equal possibilities, the actual specification to one particular state requires a specificator (mukhaṣṣiṣ) by rational necessity.
This is the core of the argument.
5
Consider the refinement and objection
Juwayni separates spatial and temporal finitude into distinct issues, and then addresses the objection that possibility must apply to the whole world. He replies that pure reason judges possibility in every part, and the composite inherits possibility.
This shows the argument's robustness even if the world is considered eternal.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
واعتمد إمام الحرمين رضي الله عنه طريقة أخرى فقال : الأرض عند خصومنا محفوفة بالماء، والماء بالهواء، والهواء بالنار، والنار بالأفلاك وهي أجرام متحيزة شاغلة جوا وحيزاً، وبالاضطرار نعلم أن فرض هذه الأجسام متيامنة عن مقرها أو متياسرة أو أكبر مما وجدت شكلا وعظماً أو أصغر من ذلك ليس من المستحيلات، وكل مختص بوجه من وجوه الجواز دون ساير الوجوه، مع استواء الجايزات وتماثل الممكنات احتاج إلى مخصص بضرورة العقل، وستعلم فيما بعد أن العالم ممكن الوجود باعتبار ذاته ؛ سواء قدر كونه متناهيا في ذاته مكانًا أو زمانًا أو غير متناه، فإن الخصم يقضي عليه بالإمكان على أنه في ذاته غير متناهي الزمان وهو متناهي المكان، فالأولى أن يجعل للتناهي المكاني مسألة ، ويجعل للتناهي الزماني من حيث الحركات والمتحركات مسألة أخرى، ونأخذ احتياجه إلى المخصص كالمسلم أو كالضروري أو كالقريب من الضروري. فإن قيل : فما الدليل على تطرق وجود الجايزات إلى العالم بأسره؟ قلنا : العقل الصريح يقضي بالإمكان في كل واحد من أجزاء العالم، والمجموع إذا كان مركباً من الأجزاء كان الإمكان واجبا له ضرورة.
And Imam al-Haramayn, may Allah be pleased with him, relied on another method and said: the earth according to our opponents is surrounded by water, and water by air, and air by fire, and fire by the celestial spheres, and they are bodies occupying space, occupying space and location. And by necessity we know that supposing these bodies as being to the right or left of their place, or larger in shape and size than they are found, or smaller than that, is not impossible. And each is specified with one aspect of possibility to the exclusion of other aspects, despite the equality of possibilities and the similarity of possible things, so it needs a specificator by necessity of reason. And you will know later that the world is possible in existence with respect to its essence, whether it is finite in its essence in place or time or infinite, for the opponent judges it as possible, while it is infinite in time and finite in place. So it is better to make finitude in place one issue, and finitude in time from the perspective of motions and moving things another issue. And we take its need for the specificator as accepted, or as necessary, or as near to necessary. If it is said: What is the proof that possibility extends to the whole world? We say: Pure reason judges possibility in every part of the world, and the whole, if it is composed of parts, possibility is necessarily incumbent upon it.
Source pages p. 13

Commentary
This passage presents a proof for the existence of a specificator (mukhaṣṣiṣ) based on the method of Imam al-Haramayn al-Juwayni. The argument begins by describing the physical universe as composed of layered elements (earth, water, air, fire, celestial spheres), all of which are bodies occupying space. It then asserts that for each of these bodies, it is conceivable that they could be different—shifted in direction, size, etc.—yet they are actualized in a specific state. This specification, given the equal possibility of alternatives, necessitates a specificator by rational necessity. Juwayni anticipates a refinement: the world is possible in itself, whether considered finite or infinite, and even if the opponent holds it to be eternal in time but finite in space, the need for a specificator remains. He then reframes the issue by separating spatial and temporal finitude into distinct questions. The passage closes with a standard objection and reply: the objector asks for the evidence that possibility applies to the entire world; the reply is that pure reason affirms possibility for every part, and a composite of possible parts is necessarily possible as a whole.

Key Terms
مخصص
جواز
إمكان
ضرورة العقل

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The need for a specificator relies on the world being created in time.
Correction: Juwayni's argument works even if the opponent believes the world is eternal in time, as long as the world is composed of possible parts that are specified in particular states.

Checkpoint
According to Juwayni's argument, what necessitates the need for a specificator (mukhaṣṣiṣ)?
A) The world's composition from elements
B) The equality of possibilities and similarity of possible things, with each actualized in one specific way
C) The finitude of the world in space
D) The impossibility of infinite regress

Key Takeaway
Juwayni's argument shows that the specification of possible entities to concrete states requires a specificator, regardless of whether the world is created or eternal.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Do you live as if the specific state of your own existence—your time, place, abilities—is a result of blind chance or the deliberate choice of a Specificator? How does that belief shape your gratitude and purpose today?
?
Juwayni argues that the need for a specificator is 'near to necessary' by reason. Is your belief in Allah based on such rational necessity, or is it merely cultural or inherited? If you faced a skeptic, would your conviction remain anchored in reason?
?
If you truly internalize that every aspect of your life—your direction, size, shape—is specified by Allah, what one area of your life would you immediately surrender to His will that you have been trying to control yourself?

The Proof That Bodies Are Finite
Reductio proof that bodies cannot be infinite

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the classical kalām proof that bodies cannot be infinite, using a logical reductio argument.

Core Idea
An infinite body leads to logical absurdity.

Before we can speak of a Creator, we must first establish that the world (bodies) is finite. This lesson presents the hallmark kalām proof that an infinite body is impossible: it would require a shorter infinite line to equal a longer one, an evident contradiction. Follow along as the reasoning unfolds.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage
Read the Arabic passage once and then the translation. Notice how the argument begins with a hypothetical and proceeds through a reductio ad absurdum.
The proof is purely logical, not empirical.
2
Identify the two lines
The argument posits two infinite lines, one shorter than the other. Understand why both being infinite leads to equality, which contradicts the assumption.
This is the core of the reductio.
3
Consider the alternative
If the shorter line is finite, then the longer line, exceeding it by a finite amount, must also be finite. Thus, both are finite — no infinity.
4
Extend to numbers
The same proof applies to infinite numbers or individuals. Reflect on why this universalizes the argument.
Shows the argument's breadth.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فإن قيل : فما الدليل على أن الأجسام متناهية من حيث الذات؟ قلنا : لو قدرنا جسماً لا يتناهى، أو بعداً لا يتناهى، فإما أن يكون ذلك الجسم غير متناه من جميع الجهات أو من جهة واحدة، وعلى أي وجه قدر فيمكن أن يفرض فيه نقطة متناهية يتصل بها خط لا يتناهى ويمكن أن تفرض نقطة أخرى على خط هو أنقص من الأول بذراع، ونصل بين النقطتين بحيث يطبق الخط الأصغر على الخط الأطول، فإن كان كل واحد من الخطين تمادى إلى غير نهاية، فيكون الخط الأصغر مساويًا للخط الأطول، وهو محال، وإن قصر الأقصر عن الأطول بمتناه فقد تناهى الأقصر وتناهى الأطول ؛ إذ قصر عنه الأقصر بمتناه وزاد الأطول عليه بمتناه، وما زاد على الشيء بمتناه كان متناهيا. وعلى كل حال فإن كان أحدهما أكبر من الثاني كان فيما لا يتناهى ما هو أصغر وأكبر وأكثر وأقل وذلك محال، فعلم أن جسماً لا يتناهى محال وجوده، وإن بعدا لا يتناهى في خلا أو ملا محال، ويمكن أن ينقل هذا البرهان بعينه إلى أعداد وأشخاص لا تتناهى حتى يتبين أن فرض ذلك محال، فإذا قضينا على الجسم بالتناهي وجاز أن يكون أكبر منه وأصغر، وإذا تخصص أحد الجايزين احتاج إلى المخصص، وكما يمكن فرض الجواز في الصغر والكبر، يمكن فرضه في التيامن والتياسر، فإن الجواز العقلي لا يقف في الجايزات، ثم المخصص لا يخلو إما أن يكون موجبًا بالذات مقتضيا بالطبع، وإما أن يكون موجدًا بالقدرة والاختيار، والأول باطل ؛ فإن الموجب بالذات لا يخصص مثلا عن مثل ؛ إذ الإحياز والجهات والأقدار والأشكال وساير الصفات بالنسبة إليه واحدة وهي في ذواتها متماثلة ؛ إذ لا طريق لنا إلى إثبات الصانع إلا بهذه الأفعال، وقد ظهر فيها آثار الاختيار لتخصيصها ببعض الجايزات دون البعض، فعلمنا قطعا ويقينا أن الصانع ليس ذاتا موجبًا بل موجدًا عالمًا قادرًا.
If it is said: What is the proof that bodies are finite in essence? We say: If we suppose a body without end, or a dimension without end, then either that body is infinite from all directions or from one direction. And whichever way it is supposed, it is possible to posit a finite point in it connected to a line without end, and it is possible to posit another point on a line that is shorter by a cubit, and we connect the two points such that the smaller line overlaps the longer line. If each of the two lines extends without end, then the smaller line equals the longer line, which is impossible. And if the shorter falls short of the longer by a finite amount, then the shorter is finite and the longer is finite, because the shorter falls short of it by a finite amount and the longer exceeds it by a finite amount, and what exceeds a thing by a finite amount is finite. In any case, if one is greater than the other, then in what is infinite there is something smaller and larger, more and less, and that is impossible. So it is known that a body without end is impossible in existence, and a dimension without end in void or plenum is impossible. This same proof can be transferred to infinite numbers and individuals until it becomes clear that supposing that is impossible. So when we judge the body to be finite, and it is possible for it to be larger and smaller, and when one of the possibilities becomes specific, it needs a specificator. And just as possibility can be supposed in smallness and largeness, so it can be supposed in being to the right or left, for rational possibility does not stop at possibilities. Then the specificator cannot but be either a necessary agent by essence, an exigent by nature, or a voluntary creator with power and choice. The first is false, for the necessary agent by essence does not specify one similar thing over another, since positions, directions, sizes, shapes, and all other attributes are equal relative to it and are similar in themselves, because we have no way to prove the Maker except through these acts, and the effects of choice have appeared in them, specifying some possibilities over others. So we know with certainty and conviction that the Maker is not a necessary essence but a creator, knowing, powerful.
Source pages p. 13

Commentary
This passage contains a detailed proof for the finitude of bodies and then uses that to demonstrate that the specificator (the Creator) is a free agent. It begins with an objection: what is the proof that bodies are finite? The answer provides a classical kalam argument: suppose an infinite body or dimension; then one can conceive two lines, one shorter than the other, both extending infinitely. If both are infinite, they must be equal, which contradicts the assumption of one being shorter. If the shorter is finite, then the longer, exceeding it by a finite amount, is also finite. This reductio ad absurdum shows that an infinite body is impossible. The argument extends to numbers and individuals as well. With finitude established, the text then argues that since a finite body could be larger or smaller, its actual size requires a specificator. Similarly, direction and other properties require specification. The specificator must be either a necessary agent (mūjib bi al-dhāt) or a voluntary agent (mūjid bi al-qudra wa al-ikhtiyār). The former is rejected because a necessary agent cannot choose among equals; all possibilities are alike to it. But the observed variety in the world shows choice, so the Creator must be knowing and powerful, acting by will.

Key Terms
تناهي الأجسام

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The proof of finitude relies on empirical observation of the world.
Correction: This is a purely logical, a priori argument that uses a thought experiment, not empirical data.

Checkpoint
What is the key step that leads to contradiction in the proof of finitude?
A) The two lines are both finite
B) The two infinite lines must be equal
C) The shorter line is longer than the longer line
D) The lines are perpendicular

Key Takeaway
The impossibility of an infinite body is established through a logical reductio, not empirical observation.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
If the universe cannot be infinite, then it must have a beginning. How does that confront your daily sense of self-sufficiency? Do you live as if the world around you is eternal, or do you acknowledge its Creator every moment?
?
This proof uses logical reasoning to demonstrate finitude. Are you willing to let reason lead you to faith, or do you resist conclusions that demand your submission?
?
The proof shows that infinite numbers are impossible in reality. When you contemplate the vastness of space, do you feel small and turn to the One who is truly infinite, or do you remain lost in wonder without drawing nearer to Him?

Why the Creator Must Be a Free Agent
Deriving that the specificator is a free agent

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why the specificator cannot be a necessary agent and must be a voluntary creator with knowledge and power.

Core Idea
A necessary agent cannot choose among equals, but the world shows deliberate choice.

Having proven that bodies are finite, the text now asks: why does a finite body have a particular size and direction? The answer requires a specificator. But what kind of specificator? It cannot be a necessary agent because that would not choose among equal possibilities. The observable specification points to a Creator who acts by knowledge, power, and will.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage
Read from 'فإذا قضينا' to the end. Notice the logical steps from finitude to specification to the nature of the specificator.
2
Understand the need for a specificator
A finite body could be larger or smaller, to the right or left. Its actual state requires something that selected that particular possibility.
This is the argument from contingency.
3
Examine the two options
The specificator is either a necessary agent (mūjib bi al-dhāt) or a voluntary agent (mūjid bi al-qudra wa al-ikhtiyār). The text explains why the first is impossible.
4
Reject the necessary agent
A necessary agent acts by nature, not choice. It cannot prefer one equal possibility over another. But we see clear preferences — different sizes, shapes, positions — so it cannot be necessary.
5
Conclude the Creator's attributes
Therefore, the Maker must be a voluntary creator: knowing, powerful, and acting by will. This aligns with the observed effects of choice.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فإن قيل : فما الدليل على أن الأجسام متناهية من حيث الذات؟ قلنا : لو قدرنا جسماً لا يتناهى، أو بعداً لا يتناهى، فإما أن يكون ذلك الجسم غير متناه من جميع الجهات أو من جهة واحدة، وعلى أي وجه قدر فيمكن أن يفرض فيه نقطة متناهية يتصل بها خط لا يتناهى ويمكن أن تفرض نقطة أخرى على خط هو أنقص من الأول بذراع، ونصل بين النقطتين بحيث يطبق الخط الأصغر على الخط الأطول، فإن كان كل واحد من الخطين تمادى إلى غير نهاية، فيكون الخط الأصغر مساويًا للخط الأطول، وهو محال، وإن قصر الأقصر عن الأطول بمتناه فقد تناهى الأقصر وتناهى الأطول ؛ إذ قصر عنه الأقصر بمتناه وزاد الأطول عليه بمتناه، وما زاد على الشيء بمتناه كان متناهيا. وعلى كل حال فإن كان أحدهما أكبر من الثاني كان فيما لا يتناهى ما هو أصغر وأكبر وأكثر وأقل وذلك محال، فعلم أن جسماً لا يتناهى محال وجوده، وإن بعدا لا يتناهى في خلا أو ملا محال، ويمكن أن ينقل هذا البرهان بعينه إلى أعداد وأشخاص لا تتناهى حتى يتبين أن فرض ذلك محال، فإذا قضينا على الجسم بالتناهي وجاز أن يكون أكبر منه وأصغر، وإذا تخصص أحد الجايزين احتاج إلى المخصص، وكما يمكن فرض الجواز في الصغر والكبر، يمكن فرضه في التيامن والتياسر، فإن الجواز العقلي لا يقف في الجايزات، ثم المخصص لا يخلو إما أن يكون موجبًا بالذات مقتضيا بالطبع، وإما أن يكون موجدًا بالقدرة والاختيار، والأول باطل ؛ فإن الموجب بالذات لا يخصص مثلا عن مثل ؛ إذ الإحياز والجهات والأقدار والأشكال وساير الصفات بالنسبة إليه واحدة وهي في ذواتها متماثلة ؛ إذ لا طريق لنا إلى إثبات الصانع إلا بهذه الأفعال، وقد ظهر فيها آثار الاختيار لتخصيصها ببعض الجايزات دون البعض، فعلمنا قطعا ويقينا أن الصانع ليس ذاتا موجبًا بل موجدًا عالمًا قادرًا.
If it is said: What is the proof that bodies are finite in essence? We say: If we suppose a body without end, or a dimension without end, then either that body is infinite from all directions or from one direction. And whichever way it is supposed, it is possible to posit a finite point in it connected to a line without end, and it is possible to posit another point on a line that is shorter by a cubit, and we connect the two points such that the smaller line overlaps the longer line. If each of the two lines extends without end, then the smaller line equals the longer line, which is impossible. And if the shorter falls short of the longer by a finite amount, then the shorter is finite and the longer is finite, because the shorter falls short of it by a finite amount and the longer exceeds it by a finite amount, and what exceeds a thing by a finite amount is finite. In any case, if one is greater than the other, then in what is infinite there is something smaller and larger, more and less, and that is impossible. So it is known that a body without end is impossible in existence, and a dimension without end in void or plenum is impossible. This same proof can be transferred to infinite numbers and individuals until it becomes clear that supposing that is impossible. So when we judge the body to be finite, and it is possible for it to be larger and smaller, and when one of the possibilities becomes specific, it needs a specificator. And just as possibility can be supposed in smallness and largeness, so it can be supposed in being to the right or left, for rational possibility does not stop at possibilities. Then the specificator cannot but be either a necessary agent by essence, an exigent by nature, or a voluntary creator with power and choice. The first is false, for the necessary agent by essence does not specify one similar thing over another, since positions, directions, sizes, shapes, and all other attributes are equal relative to it and are similar in themselves, because we have no way to prove the Maker except through these acts, and the effects of choice have appeared in them, specifying some possibilities over others. So we know with certainty and conviction that the Maker is not a necessary essence but a creator, knowing, powerful.

This passage contains a detailed proof for the finitude of bodies and then uses that to demonstrate that the specificator (the Creator) is a free agent. How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
You now know that every detail of creation—including your own existence—was deliberately chosen by a free Creator. Are you living as if your life is a thoughtful selection from Allah or as if it happened by accident? What will you do today to acknowledge His specific choices in your life?
?
If the Creator were a necessary agent, your obedience or disobedience would be meaningless. Since He is a voluntary agent who chooses, your response matters. Are you trying to please Him by choosing His commands, or are you acting out of routine?
?
The proof shows that Allah's knowledge and power are evident in every specific detail of creation. Look at one specific gift in your life—health, time, or ability—and ask yourself: Did Allah have to give it to me in this exact way? How will I use this chosen blessing to serve the One who chose it for me?

From Finitude to a Voluntary Creator
Argument that specification of finite bodies requires a voluntary agent

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how the finitude of bodies leads to the conclusion that the Creator is a voluntary agent with knowledge and power, not a necessary cause.

Core Idea
A necessary cause cannot choose between equals; only a voluntary creator can specify possibilities.

The argument so far has shown that bodies are finite. Now the author takes the next step: since finite bodies could have been otherwise, their actual state requires a cause that selects one possibility. This cause cannot be a necessary agent because such an agent cannot discriminate between identical alternatives. Therefore, the Creator must be a voluntary agent.

Guided Path
1
Understand the premise
Bodies are finite and could have been larger, smaller, or positioned differently. All these possibilities are equally rational.
2
Identify the two types of specifier
The cause that specifies one possibility over others is either a necessary cause (by essence) or a voluntary agent (by power and choice).
Pay attention to the Arabic terms: موجب بالذات vs. موجد بالقدرة والاختيار.
3
Refute the necessary cause
A necessary cause treats all similar possibilities equally and cannot choose one. Since the world shows specification, the necessary cause is impossible.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فإذا قضينا على الجسم بالتناهي وجاز أن يكون أكبر منه وأصغر، وإذا تخصص أحد الجايزين احتاج إلى المخصص، وكما يمكن فرض الجواز في الصغر والكبر، يمكن فرضه في التيامن والتياسر، فإن الجواز العقلي لا يقف في الجايزات، ثم المخصص لا يخلو إما أن يكون موجبا بالذات مقتضيا بالطبع، وإما أن يكون موجدا بالقدرة والاختيار، والأول باطل؛ فإن الموجب بالذات لا يخصص مثلا عن مثل؛ إذ الإحياز والجهات والأقدار والأشكال وساير الصفات بالنسبة إليه واحدة وهي في ذواتها متماثلة؛ إذ لا طريق لنا إلى إثبات الصانع إلا بهذه الأفعال، وقد ظهر فيها آثار الاختيار لتخصيصها ببعض الجايزات دون البعض، فعلمنا قطعا ويقينا أن الصانع ليس ذاتا موجبا بل موجدا عالما قادرا.
When we pass judgment that the body is finite and it is possible for it to be larger or smaller, and when one of the two possibilities becomes specific, it needs a specific. Just as permission can be supposed in smallness and bigness, it can be supposed in right and left, for rational permission does not stop at possibilities. Then the specific is either a necessary cause by essence, requiring by nature, or an efficient cause by power and choice. The first is false, because the necessary cause by essence does not specify one similar from another, since positions, directions, sizes, shapes, and all attributes are equal relative to it and are identical in their essences. And we have no way to prove the Maker except through these acts, and in them appear the marks of choice, as they specify some possibilities over others. So we know with certainty that the Maker is not a necessary essence but an efficient, knowing, powerful cause.
Source pages p. 14

Commentary
This passage builds on the previous proof of finitude. Since bodies are finite, they could have been bigger, smaller, or in different positions. Their actual specific features require a cause that selects one possibility. This cause is either a necessary agent (mūjib bi l-dhāt) or a voluntary agent (mūjid bi l-qudra wa l-ikhtiyār). The former is impossible because a necessary cause would not choose between equals; it would produce all alike. Since we observe that only some possibilities are realized, we deduce a voluntary creator with knowledge and power.

Key Terms
مخصص
موجب بالذات
موجد بالقدرة والاختيار
جواز عقلي
تيامن
تياسر

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: A necessary cause (like natural causality) can still produce particular effects by specifying them inherently.
Correction: A necessary cause cannot differentiate between identical possibilities; if it were the specifier, all possibilities would be realized equally, which is not the case.

Checkpoint
Why is the first option (necessary cause by essence) impossible?
A) It would not create anything.
B) It cannot specify between similar possibilities.
C) It is not intelligent.
D) It requires a body.

Key Takeaway
The Creator is a voluntary agent because only such an agent can specify between equally possible alternatives.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
If your existence in this precise moment—with your specific traits, circumstances, and opportunities—is not a random outcome but a deliberate choice by a voluntary Creator, what does that demand from you right now? In what area of your life are you acting as if your situation is a brute fact rather than a meaningful selection?
?
The argument shows that a mindless necessity cannot produce the particular details of creation. When you pray, do you truly address a free, personal God who could have done otherwise, or do you unconsciously treat Him like a mechanical force? What would change in your prayer if you fully internalized that you are speaking to a voluntary agent?
?
If God's voluntary specification includes not only your physical form but also your moral and spiritual capacities, how should that affect your responsibility to use them? What specific possibility that God has actualized in you are you neglecting or misusing?

Refuting Simultaneity Between Contingent and Necessary
Juwayni refutes any form of simultaneity between the contingent and the Necessary Existent.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the three ways in which the contingent cannot be simultaneous with the Necessary Existent, and the reasoning behind each refutation.

Core Idea
The contingent can never share existence, time, or rank with the Necessary.

Juwayni now tackles the critical theological question: Could the contingent (the world) ever be coexistent with God? He systematically rejects three forms of simultaneity—in essence and existence, in time, and in rank—using logical arguments that safeguard God's transcendence and absolute priority. This passage is a masterclass in applying rational principles to defend tawḥīd.

Guided Path
1
Read the Arabic passage
Read the Arabic text slowly, noting the three 'lā yajūzu' (it is not permissible) phrases that mark the three refutations.
The structure is clear: the author repeats 'lā yajūzu' for each case.
2
Understand the first refutation (essence and existence)
Focus on the argument that 'before' and 'with' in essence cannot coexist in one thing. This directly implies the contingent is posterior.
3
Analyze the second refutation (time)
Notice the use of correlatives (al-mutaḍāyifāt) to show that temporal simultaneity would require mutual dependence, making God temporal—an impossibility.
Think about relationships like brotherhood: if A is brother of B, then B is brother of A.
4
Examine the third refutation (rank and virtue)
See the distinction between necessary in itself and necessary through another: their ranks are inherently different.
5
Grasp the conclusion
The final sentence confirms the absolute negation of any form of coexistence, and ends with the statement of God's priority: 'kāna llāhu wa lam yakun maʿahū shayʾ'.
This is a well-known ḥadīth or theological dictum.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ولا يجوز أن يكون وجوده مع واجب الوجود بالذات والوجود جميعا؛ لأن قبل ومع بالذات والوجود لا يجتمعان في شيء واحد، فهو إذا متأخر الوجود، ولا يجوز أن يكون مع واجب الوجود بالزمان؛ لأنه يوجب أن يكون واجب الوجود زمانيا؛ لأن قولنا: مع من جملة المتضايفات؛ كالأخوة والأبوة، فأحد الشيئين إذا كان مع الثاني بالزمان كان الثاني معه أيضا بالزمان، وبكل اعتبار أثبت المعية في أحد الشيئين وجب عليك أن تثبتها في الشيء الثاني، ولا يجوز أن يكون وجوده مع واجب الوجود بالرتبة والفضيلة؛ لأن رتبة الواجب بذاته لا يكون كرتبة الواجب بغيره، وظهر أن جايز الوجود وواجب الوجود لا يكونان معا بوجه من الوجوه واعتبار من الاعتبارات، وصح القول كان الله ولم يكن معه شيء.
And it is not permissible that its existence be with the Necessary Existent in essence and existence together, because 'before' and 'with' in essence and existence do not combine in one thing; so it is posterior in existence. And it is not permissible that it be with the Necessary Existent in time, because that would necessitate that the Necessary Existent be temporal; for our saying 'with' is among the correlatives (al-mutaḍāyifāt), like brotherhood and parenthood; so if one of the two things is 'with' the second in time, then the second is also 'with' it in time; and by whatever consideration you establish coexistence in one of the two things, you must also establish it in the second thing. And it is not permissible that its existence be with the Necessary Existent in rank and virtue, because the rank of the Necessary in itself is not like the rank of the necessary through another. And it became clear that the possible of existence and the Necessary Existent cannot be together in any aspect or any consideration. And the statement became sound: 'God was and nothing was with Him'.
Source pages p. 19

Commentary
This passage is a refutation of any form of simultaneity between the contingent (the world) and the Necessary Existent (God). The author rejects three possibilities: (1) coexistence in essence and existence, because then the world would be necessary; (2) coexistence in time, because that would make God temporal and because 'with' in time is a correlative concept requiring mutual qualification; (3) coexistence in rank and virtue, because God's rank is intrinsic while the world's rank is derivative. Therefore, the only correct statement is that God was and nothing was with Him.

بالذات والوجود (in essence and existence)
بالزمان (in time)
بالرتبة والفضيلة (in rank and virtue)
جايز الوجود (possible of existence)
واجب الوجود (Necessary Existent)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: That the world could be co-eternal with God in some sense, e.g., in time or rank.
Correction: The passage shows that any form of simultaneity would either make God temporal or equate His rank with creation. Since God is Necessary in Himself and the world is possible, they cannot coexist in any aspect.

Checkpoint
Why does Juwayni reject temporal simultaneity between the contingent and the Necessary?
A) Because time does not exist
B) Because it would make God temporal and because 'with' in time is a correlative requiring mutual qualification
C) Because God is not in time
D) Both B and C

Key Takeaway
The contingent and the Necessary Existent cannot be simultaneous in any way; God exists prior to all creation, and nothing coexists with Him.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
If God was and nothing was with Him, what then is your life? Your achievements? Your problems? How does this truth reshape the weight you give to your own plans and fears?
?
You say God is the Creator, but do you mentally reserve a corner of the universe where your own efforts exist independently? Where in your heart have you smuggled a 'with' alongside God?
?
The possible of existence is nothing without the Necessary. When you feel self-sufficient, which verse or proof from this passage confronts your pride? Write one concrete way you will remind yourself today that you are contingent.

Why God and the World Cannot Coexist: The Two Eternities
Proof that God and the world cannot coexist and refutation of shared eternity.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why the Necessary Being and contingent beings cannot coexist in any respect, and how the term 'eternity' applies differently to God and the world.

Core Idea
True eternity belongs to God alone; the world's temporal eternity is fundamentally different.

This passage tackles a critical point: if God and the world both 'exist forever', doesn't that make them co-eternal? The author argues that the word 'eternity' is equivocal—it means something completely different for God and for the world. By analyzing what it means for something to be necessary versus contingent, we see that coexistence in any sense is impossible.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without analyzing it. Then read the translation. Let the author's own rhythm come through before you interpret.
Notice how the author uses conditional statements and rhetorical questions.
2
Identify the logical argument
The passage begins with a conditional: if the contingent coexists with the Necessary in time, then the Necessary would also be temporal. Find this and understand why co-existence in rank is also impossible.
The Necessary's rank is essential, not derived.
3
Recognize the equivocal use of 'eternity'
The author accuses his opponents of using the word 'dawam' equivocally. What are the two distinct meanings he distinguishes?
One is temporal, the other essential/non-temporal.
4
Contrast the two eternities
God's eternity means necessary existence without beginning or end, non-temporal. The world's eternity is temporal, having a beginning and subject to change. Contrast them clearly.
5
Understand the absurdity
If both eternities were the same, it would lead to absurdity: either God's existence becomes temporal or the world's becomes essential. Both are false, so the claim fails.
This refutes the idea that the world is eternal by or with God.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
كان مع الثاني بالزمان كان الثاني معه أيضًا بالزمان، وبكل اعتبار أثبت المعية في أحد الشيئين وجب عليك أن تثبتها في الشيء الثاني، ولا يجوز أن يكون وجوده مع واجب الوجود بالرتبة والفضيلة؛ لأن رتبة الواجب بذاته لا يكون كرتبة الواجب بغيره، وظهر أن جايز الوجود وواجب الوجود لا يكونان معا بوجه من الوجوه واعتبار من الاعتبارات، وصح القول كان الله ولم يكن معه شيء، فما معنى قولكم: الجايز دايم الوجود بالواجب أو مع الواجب، أو قدرتم دوام وجود الباري تعالى زمانيا ممتداً مع الأزمنة غير المتناهية، كما توهمتم وجود العالم زمانيا ممتداً في أزمنة لا تتناهى وبئس الوهم وهمكم في الدوامين، فكأنكم أخذتم لفظ الدوام بالاشتراك المحض؟ أما دوام وجود الباري تعالى فمعناه أنه واجب لذاته وبذاته، ولا يتطرق إليه جواز ولا عدم بوجه من الوجوه، فهو الأول بلا أول كان قبله، والآخر بلا آخر كان بعده، وأوله آخره، وآخره أوله؛ أي ليس وجوده زمانيا. وأما العالم فله أول ودوامه دوام زماني يتطرق إليه الجواز والعدم والقلة والكثرة، والاستمرار والانقطاع فلو كان دايم الوجود بدوام الباري كان الباري دايم الوجود بدوام العالم، فلو كان الدوامان بمعنى واحد فيلزم أن يكون وجود الباري تعالى زمانيا، أو وجود العالم ذاتيا، وكلا الوجهين باطل، فبطل قولكم: إن العالم دايم الوجود بالواجب، وإنما يتضح هذا البطلان كل الوضوح إذا أثبتنا استحالة حوادث لا أول لها ووجود موجودات لا تتناهى.
If it (the contingent) was with the second (the Necessary) in time, then the second was also with it in time. And by any consideration you establish coexistence in one of two things, you must establish it in the second thing. And it is not permissible that its existence with the Necessary Being be in rank and virtue, because the rank of the Necessary in itself is not like the rank of the necessary by another. And it became clear that the contingent being and the Necessary Being are not together in any respect or consideration. And the statement 'There was God and there was nothing with Him' is correct. So what is the meaning of your saying: 'The contingent is eternal in existence by the Necessary or with the Necessary,' or you imagined the eternity of the existence of the Most High as temporal, extended with endless times, just as you imagined the existence of the world as temporal, extended in endless times? How evil is your imagination regarding the two eternities! It is as if you took the word 'eternity' (al-dawām) by pure equivocation. As for the eternity of the existence of the Most High, its meaning is that He is necessary in essence and by essence, and possibility or non-existence does not pertain to Him in any respect. So He is the First without a first before Him, and the Last without a last after Him. His first is His last and His last is His first, meaning that His existence is not temporal. As for the world, it has a beginning, and its eternity is temporal eternity, to which possibility, non-existence, paucity, multiplicity, continuity, and discontinuity pertain. For if it were eternal in existence by the eternity of the Necessary, then the Necessary would be eternal in existence by the eternity of the world. For if the two eternities were one meaning, then it would follow that the existence of the Most High is temporal, or the existence of the world is essential. Both aspects are false. Thus your statement that the world is eternal in existence by the Necessary is false. And this falsehood becomes completely clear when we establish the impossibility of events without a beginning and the existence of existents without end.
Source pages p. 19

Commentary
This passage is a proof establishing that the Necessary Being (wājib al-wujūd) and contingent beings (jā'iz al-wujūd) cannot coexist in any respect, and it refutes the claim that the world is eternal in the same sense as God. The author begins with a logical conditional: if the contingent coexists with the Necessary in time, then the Necessary would also be temporal with it. Furthermore, any respect of coexistence applied to one must apply to the other, but the Necessary's rank is essential and unique, so coexistence in rank is impossible. Therefore, the contingent and Necessary cannot be together in any way. This supports the statement 'There was God and nothing with Him.' The author then questions the meaning of saying the contingent is eternal by or with the Necessary, and criticizes the notion of God's eternity being temporal and extended. He distinguishes God's eternity—necessary existence without beginning or end, non-temporal—from the world's temporal eternity, which has a beginning and is subject to change. If both were eternal in the same sense, it would force an absurdity: either God's existence becomes temporal, or the world's existence becomes essential. Both are false, so the world cannot be eternal through the Necessary. The passage concludes by noting that this falsehood becomes fully clear when the impossibility of an infinite regress of events and existents is established, setting up a subsequent argument.

Correction: God's eternity is essential and non-temporal; the world's eternity is temporal and contingent. They are fundamentally different.

The World's Beginning and Finitude Are Necessary
Author answers a question about God creating the world earlier.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why the world's beginning and finitude are necessary aspects of its conception, and how this answers the objection about God's ability to create the world earlier.

Core Idea
The world must have a beginning; it cannot be conceived otherwise.

This passage addresses a natural question: Could God have created the world earlier? The answer affirms that the world's beginning (awwaliyya) and finitude (tanahi) are necessary to conceive. Let's explore this reasoning and see why it is crucial for a proper understanding of creation.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once without analyzing. Then read the literal translation. Notice that this is a short objection-and-answer format.
The answer appears fragmentary but is complete in its core claim.
2
Identify the objection and the answer
Find the question asked and the answer given. The objector wonders if God could have created the world at an earlier time, implying a longer duration. The answer asserts that the world's beginning and finitude are necessary to conceive.
The answer does not elaborate why it is necessary, but it affirms the necessity.
3
Reflect on the necessity
Think about what the answer means: Why must the world have a beginning? Consider that if the world were eternal, it would not be created. The beginning is a necessary aspect of its being a creature.
This connects to broader theological discussions about temporality and creation.

It remains to be said: Was it permissible for Him to create the world before He created it, such that the proportion of its beginning to our time would be more time? It is answered: That establishing the beginning and finitude of the world is necessary to conceive.

Why God Created the World When He Did
Answers objections about timing and size of creation via rational demonstration.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why the questions 'Could God have created the world earlier?' and 'Could He have made it larger?' do not refute the rational proof that the world has a beginning and is finite.

Core Idea
Imaginary possibilities do not disprove demonstrated realities.

This passage addresses common objections to the world's temporality and finitude. Critics ask: Why did God create at this moment and not earlier? Why this size and not larger? The author responds by distinguishing between what reason has proven—that the world has a beginning and is finite—and imaginary estimations that go beyond proof. Such estimations, though mentally possible, are infinite and do not invalidate the demonstration.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the full Arabic and English to grasp the overall argument. Notice the two parallel questions about time and space.
The passage uses a question-and-answer format.
2
Identify the two objections and responses
The author first addresses the timing objection, then the size objection. Note that the answer is structurally identical for both.
3
Understand the key distinction
The author distinguishes between what is proven by demonstration (burhān) and what is mere mental possibility (tajwīz ʿaqlī). This is the core of the refutation.
The term 'taqdīr wahmī' refers to imaginary estimations.
4
Reflect on why imaginary possibilities do not refute proofs
Consider that such possibilities are infinite and cannot be exhausted, so they do not constitute valid objections to a conclusive rational proof.
5
Apply the final summary
The author concludes that combining something with a beginning and something without a beginning is impossible, and this is understood necessarily from the concept of origination.
This summary ties the temporal and spatial finitude together.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
يبقى أن يُقال: هل كان يجوز أن يخلق العالم قبل ما خلقه بحيث يكون نسبة بدوه إلى وقتنا أكثر زمانًا؟ فيجاب عنه: أن إثبات الأولية والتناهي للعالم واجب تصوره عقلا؛ إذ البرهان قد دل عليه، وما وراء ذلك تقدير وهمي يسمى تجويزا عقليا، والتقديرات والتجويزات لا تقف ولا تتناهى، وهو كما إذا سألتم: هل كان يجوز أن يحدث العالم أكبر مما خلقه بحيث تكون نسبة نهايته إلى مكاننا أكبر مسافة؟ فيجاب عنه: إن إثبات الحد والتناهي للعالم واجب تصوره عقلا؛ إذ البرهان قد دل عليه وما وراء ذلك فتقدير ذهني يسمى تجويزا عقليا، والتقديرات والتجويزات لا تتناهى، فتقدير مكان وراء العالم مكانًا كتقدير زمان وراء العالم زمانًا، وبالجملة حدث العالم حيث يتصور الحدوث والحادث ما له أول والقديم ما لا أول له والجمع بين ما له أول وبين ما لا أول له فحال هذا ما نعقله من الحدوث ضرورة، وهو كتناهي العالم من الحجمية والجسمية حذو القدة بالقدة.
It remains to be said: Could it have been possible for God to create the world before He created it, such that the ratio of its beginning to our time be greater in duration? The answer: That establishing the beginning and finitude (al-awwaliyya wa al-tanāhī) of the world is necessarily conceivable by reason, because the demonstration (al-burhān) has proven it. And beyond that is an imaginary estimation (taqdīr wahmī) called mental possibility (tajwīz ʿaqlī). Estimations and possibilities do not stop and are not infinite. And this is like if you ask: Could it have been possible for God to create the world larger than He created it, such that the ratio of its end to our place be greater in distance? The answer: That establishing limit and finitude (al-ḥadd wa al-tanāhī) of the world is necessarily conceivable by reason, because the demonstration has proven it, and beyond that is a mental estimation called mental possibility. Estimations and possibilities are not infinite. So estimating a place beyond the world is like estimating a time beyond the world. In sum, the world originated (ḥadatha) where origination is conceived; the originated (al-ḥādith) is that which has a beginning (mā lahu awwal), and the eternal (al-qadīm) is that which has no beginning. The combination between that which has a beginning and that which has no beginning is a state that we understand necessarily from origination. And it is like the finitude of the world in magnitude and corporeality, measure for measure.
Source pages p. 23

Commentary
This passage answers the common objection: Why did God create the world at this particular time and not earlier? And similarly, why this particular size and not larger? The author states that rational demonstration (al-burhān) has already established that the world has a beginning (awwaliyya) and is finite (mutanāhī). Any question beyond that—like 'Could it have been earlier?'—is merely an imaginary estimation (taqdīr wahmī) called mental possibility (tajwīz ʿaqli). Such possibilities are infinite and have no stopping point; they do not constitute a valid objection. The same reasoning applies to spatial size. The author concludes that the combination of something with a beginning and something without a beginning is impossible, as we understand necessarily from the concept of origination. The finitude of the world in time and space is equally established by demonstration.

Key Terms
أولية
تناهي
برهان
تقدير وهمي
تجويز عقلي
قديم
حادث

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The question 'Could God have created the world earlier?' disproves the world's temporality.
Correction: The author argues that such a question is based on an imaginary possibility beyond rational demonstration; it does not invalidate the proof because imaginary possibilities are infinite and do not undermine demonstrated truths.

Checkpoint
How does the author respond to the objection that God could have created the world earlier?
A) By arguing that time itself began with the world.
B) By saying the question is based on an imaginary possibility beyond rational demonstration.
C) By stating that God's will is arbitrary and cannot be questioned.
D) By proving that an earlier creation would be contradictory.

Key Takeaway
The world's beginning and finitude are proven by rational demonstration; imaginary possibilities of 'earlier' or 'larger' do not refute that proof.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?

The passage says that possibilities (tajwīzāt) are infinite and do not stop. In your own life, do you ever get lost in 'what if' scenarios about your past decisions or future paths? How can the principle of resting on what has been proven (burhān) free you to act with clarity today?
?
Consider that you, like the world, are a creature with a beginning (ḥādith). How does facing your own temporality directly challenge you to prioritize eternal realities over fleeting comforts? What one change will you make today because you truly understand that you have a beginning and an end?
