Refutation of the Philosophers' Emanation Scheme
Refutation of the emanation scheme with probing questions.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the key flaws in the philosophers' emanation theory and the rational necessity of a free Creator.

Core Idea
The emanation scheme is arbitrary; only a free Creator explains existence.

The philosophers claimed that the universe necessarily emanates from a first intellect. But their scheme is riddled with arbitrary stops, inconsistent numbers, and unexplained transitions. In this passage, the author dismantles their system with penetrating questions and affirms that only a free Creator—who creates by will—can account for the order we see.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without trying to analyse. Then read the translation. Let the author's questioning style come through.
Notice the author builds up to a series of questions.
2
Identify the series of questions
The author poses several objections to the emanation scheme. Try to list them as you read: about the four considerations, the number of intellects, the change from celestial to elemental, etc.
Each question targets an arbitrary assumption.
3
Observe the conclusion
After showing the philosophers' confusion, the author states that the existence of things depends on a Maker who is all-knowing, powerful, and free. See how the refutation leads to this positive affirmation.
4
Reflect on the method
The author does not just reject the scheme; he reveals its internal inconsistencies. Ask yourself: Why is this method effective?
This connects to the learning objective about rational necessity.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فعرف بهذه الاعتراضات أن الواسطة التي أوجبوا وجودها حتى توجب الموجودات لا يحتاج إليها. ثم نقول: من أين أثبتم أربع اعتبارات في الصادر الأول حتى صدرت عنه أربعة جواهر؟ فما قولكم في الصادر الثاني والثالث أفيصدر عن كل واحد على مثال ما صدر من الأول، أو يتغير الترتيب؟ فإن كان على مثال الأول فيجب أن يصدر عن كل واحد من الأربعة أربعة جواهر فيتضاعف الأعداد أربعة في أربعة وذلك على خلاف الوجود، أو يصدر عن العقل الثاني أربعة أخرى، فما الموجب للوقوف على تسعة من العقول أو تسع من النفوس وتسعة من الأفلاك وأربعة من العناصر، فهلا زاد إلى ما لا يتناهى، أو هلا زاد بعدد معلوم، أو هلا نقص، فهل الانتهاء إلى عدد معلوم ثم الوقوف عليه إلا تحكم محض، ثم ما الموجب حتى يتغير التأثير من السماوي المتركب تركيبا لا ينحل قط، المتحرك تحركا على الاستدارة لا يسكن قط إلى العناصر والمركبات تركيبا لا يدوم قط، ولا يثبت على حال قط المتحركة تحركا على الاستقامة، ثم الانتهاء إلى النفس الإنسانية والوقوف عليها آخراً؟ وما الموجب لتقدير النجوم والشمس والقمر بأقدارها المعلومة حتى صار منها ما هو أكبر ومنها ما هو أصغر؟ وما الموجب لتعيين القطبين بالموضع المعلوم مع أن كل كرة واحدة وليس بعض الأجزاء بتعين القطب فيه أولى من البعض؟ فإن كنتم شرعتم في طلب العلة لكل شيء، وادعيتم أن كمال نفس الإنسان في أن تتجلى لها حقائق الموجودات، وتصرفتم بالفكر العقلي في الهيئات وكيفية الإبداع. فأفيدونا جواب هذه الأسئلة، وإذ رأينا انحصاركم وتحيركم، ورأينا الوجود على خلاف الترتيب الذي وضعتم ولم تستمر قاعدتكم على ما مهدتم؛ عرف بطلان مذهبكم من كل وجه وعلم بالضرورة استناد الموجودات إلى صانع عالم قادر مختار ابتدع الخلق بقدرته وإرادته ابتداعًا، واخترعهم على مشيئته اختراعا، ثم سلكهم في طرق إرادته أو بعثهم على سبيل محبته لا يملكون تأخراً عما قدمهم إليه، ولا يستطيعون تقدمًا إلى ما أخرهم عنه، وهذا القدر يكفي في هذه المسألة من الرد على الفلاسفة المتفقين على رأي أرسطاطاليس، وسنرد الباقين عند تعقبنا آراء أرباب المقالات إن شاء الله تعالى.
So it was known by these objections that the intermediary which they necessitated to exist in order to necessitate the existing things is not needed. Then we say: From where did you prove four considerations in the first emanation so that four jewels proceeded from it? What do you say about the second and third emanations? Do they proceed from each one in the manner of what proceeded from the first, or does the order change? If it is on the pattern of the first, then it must proceed from each one of the four four jewels, so the numbers multiply four by four, and that is contrary to existence, or from the second intellect another four issues. So what is the cause for stopping at nine intellects, or nine souls, and nine spheres, and four elements? Why did it not increase to infinity, or why did it not increase by a known number, or why did it not decrease? Is the termination at a known number and then stopping there anything but pure arbitrariness? Then what is the cause that the influence changes from the celestial [body] composed with a composition that never dissolves, moving with a circular motion that never rests, to the elements and compounds with a composition that never lasts, never remaining in any state, moving with rectilinear motion? Then the termination at the human soul and stopping there at last? What is the cause for the determination of the stars, the sun, and the moon in their known magnitudes, so that among them are larger and smaller? What is the cause for the assignment of the two poles to a known position, when every sphere is one, and no part of it is more deserving of being the pole than another? If you have embarked on seeking the cause for everything, and claimed that the perfection of the human soul is that the realities of existing things become unveiled to it, and you have exercised rational thought regarding the configurations and the manner of origination, then inform us of the answers to these questions. And when we saw your consternation and confusion, and we saw existence contrary to the order you posited, and your principle did not continue on what you established, the invalidity of your doctrine became known from every aspect, and it was known necessarily that existing things depend upon a Maker, omniscient, omnipotent, free, who originated creation by His power and will as an origination, and invented them according to His will as an invention, then led them in the paths of His will or sent them on the way of His love. They do not possess delay from what He has brought forward for them, nor can they advance to what He has delayed for them. And this suffices in this issue as a refutation of the philosophers who agree on the opinion of Aristotle. And we will refute the rest when we examine the views of the proponents of doctrines, God willing.
Source pages p. 61

Commentary
This passage is a refutation of the philosophers' (falasifa) emanation theory, particularly the scheme of the Peripatetics following Aristotle. The author first states that the objections raised earlier show that the intermediary they posited (the First Intellect) is unnecessary. He then launches a series of questions exposing the arbitrariness of their system. The questions are: (1) Why did they posit four considerations in the First Intellect to produce four things? (2) What about subsequent emanations—do they follow the same pattern or change? If same, numbers would multiply inconsistently with reality; if different, why stop at nine intellects, nine souls, nine spheres, and four elements? Why not more or less? (3) Why does the influence change from the celestial to the elemental, despite the celestial being eternal and the elemental perishable? (4) Why stop at the human soul? (5) Why are the stars, sun, and moon in specific sizes? (6) Why are the poles located where they are, when every point on a sphere is equivalent? The author then points out that the philosophers claim to seek causes for everything and to perfect the soul through knowledge, but they cannot answer these questions. Their confusion and the fact that reality contradicts their order prove their doctrine false. Instead, existence necessarily depends on a Creator who is omniscient, omnipotent, free, and creates by will. The author concludes that this suffices for refuting the Aristotelian philosophers, and he will refute others later.

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The philosophers' emanation scheme is a necessary logical deduction.
Correction: The author shows it contains arbitrary stops and inconsistencies, so it is not necessary but man-made.

Checkpoint

A) The scheme contradicts scripture.
B) The scheme contains arbitrary stops and inconsistent numbers.
C) The scheme denies the existence of God.

Key Takeaway
The philosophers' emanation scheme is arbitrary; the order of existence ultimately points to a free Creator.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Have you ever placed your trust in a human system of thought—science, philosophy, or cultural norms—as if it were a sufficient explanation for your existence and purpose? When you read the author's relentless questioning of the philosophers' arbitrary assumptions, do you feel the same demand to examine the foundations of your own worldview? Will you commit this week to identify one assumption you hold that may be as arbitrary as the emanation scheme, and test it against revealed truth?
?
The philosophers claimed that the universe emanated necessarily from the First Cause, removing any need for a personal will. In your daily life, do you live as if your existence is a matter of blind necessity—as if you have no real choice, no personal responsibility to your Creator? The author concludes that all things depend on a Maker who is all-powerful and free, and who controls your every moment. Do you truly believe that, moment to moment, your life is guided by His will? What would change if you acted on that belief today?
?
The author's final words remind us that we 'do not possess delay from what He has brought forward for them, nor can they advance to what He has delayed for them.' How does this truth confront your own sense of control over your life? Are you trying to advance something that God has delayed, or resisting something He has brought forward? Will you surrender that area to Him today?

Refutation of Aristotelian Philosophy on the Sphere
The author refutes the philosophers' theory of a single sphere and demonstrates their confusion.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the logical contradiction in the Aristotelian cosmology and the author's argument that all existence depends on a free and powerful Creator.

Core Idea
The philosophers' own model is self-contradictory; existence demands a free Creator.

The author confronts the Aristotelian philosophers with a logical dilemma regarding the celestial sphere: if it is a single body, no part is more deserving to be the pole, yet they posit a fixed pole. Using this and their own claims about causality, he demonstrates their confusion and concludes that existence necessarily depends on a Creator who is knowing, powerful, and free. This lesson unpacks that argument.

Guided Path
1
Read the Passage
Read the Arabic passage once, then the translation. Pay attention to the author's challenge and the conclusion.
The passage is a continuous argument without breaks.
2
Identify the Contradiction
Find where the author points out the inconsistency: if the sphere is one body, no part is more fit to be the pole, yet the philosophers claim a fixed pole.
This is the core logical move.
3
Understand the Philosophers' Claims
The philosophers claim to seek causes for everything and that human perfection is knowledge of realities. See how the author uses their own standards against them.
4
See the Conclusion
The author concludes that the philosophers are perplexed and their doctrine is false. Instead, existence depends on a Creator who is all-knowing, powerful, and free.
This conclusion is presented as necessary knowledge.

One single sphere, no part of which is more deserving to be the pole than another? For you have embarked on seeking the cause for everything, and you claimed that the perfection of the human soul is that the realities of existent things be disclosed to it, and you conducted yourselves with rational thought concerning the configurations and the modality of origination. And when we saw your perplexity and confusion, and we saw existence contrary to the order you posited, and your foundation did not persist on what you established, the falsity of your doctrine became known from every aspect, and it was known necessarily that existent things are dependent upon a Maker, Knowing, Powerful, Free, who originated creation with His power and will as an origination, and invented them according to His will as an invention. Then He made them traverse the paths of His will or sent them forth on the way of His love; they cannot delay what He has advanced, nor can they advance what He has delayed. And this suffices in this issue as a refutation of the philosophers who agree with the opinion of Aristotle. And we will refute the rest when we follow up the opinions of the proponents of doctrines, God willing.
Source pages p. 62

Commentary
This passage is a refutation of the Aristotelian philosophers. The argument begins by posing a dilemma: if the celestial sphere is a single body, then no part of it has greater claim to be the pole (axis) than another, yet they posit a fixed pole. This contradiction shows the inconsistency of their model. The author then addresses the philosophers' claim that they seek causes for everything and that human perfection is to know the realities of existents through rational thought. He challenges them to answer the questions raised. Since they are seen to be confused and their system does not match actual existence, their doctrine is false. Instead, it is known necessarily that all existents depend on a Creator who is knowing, powerful, and free, who originates creation by His power and will. He controls all things; nothing can outrun His decree. This refutation is sufficient against followers of Aristotle, and the author promises to refute others later.

The Need for God's Help in Obligations
The author clarifies the answer by citing Quranic verses of seeking help, proving the servant's need for God.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how the Quranic command to seek help from God proves that the servant is dependent on God for his actions.

Core Idea
Seeking help from God shows the servant's dependence, not independence.

In this passage, the author presents a powerful argument from the very structure of Islamic obligations. The commands to 'do' and 'do not do' are paired with instructions to seek God's help. This reveals that the servant cannot accomplish even the commanded acts without divine assistance, undermining any claim of human independence.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once, then the translation. Notice the Quranic verses cited and the logical flow.
The verses are from al-Fatiha and Al Imran.
2
Analyze the two supplications
Identify what is being asked: guidance itself or steadfastness upon it. Understand that both imply need.
3
Grasp the logical implication
The author argues that if the servant were independent, he would not need to seek help. Since the Quran commands seeking help, the servant must be dependent.
This is the core logical move.
4
Consider the favor (minna) argument
God's bestowal of guidance as a favor would be meaningless if the servant had independent power. The opponent's interpretation nullifies this.
See verse from al-Hujurat.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ومما يوضح الجواب غاية الإيضاح أن التكليف) بافعل ولا تفعل(1) ورد بالاستعانة بالله سبحانه وتعالى في نفس المكلف به كقوله تعالى : ( اهْدِنَا الصِّرَاطَ الْمُسْتَقِيمَ [الفاتحة: ٥]، وكقوله تعالى : ( رَبَّنَا لَا تُزِغْ قُلُوبَنَا بَعْدَ إِذْ هَدَيْتَنَا لَآل 10، عمران : ١٦، وسواء كانت الهداية نفسها هي المسئولة بالدعاء أو الثبات عليها هو المسئول، ولا شك أن العبد لو كان مستقلا بإنشائها بقدرته (۷) مستبدا بالثبات عليها كان مستغنيا عن هذه الاستعانة ، ثم الله سبحانه (۸) يمن على من يشاء من عباده بأن (۹) هداهم للإيمان، وعند الخصم هو محمول على خلق القدرة وهي صالحة للضدين جميعًا على السواء، وذلك يبطل قضية الامتنان بالهداية، قال الله سبحانه وتعالى : ( بَلِ اللَّهُ يَمُنُّ عَلَيْكُمْ أَنْ هَدَنَكُمْ لِلإِيمَنِ [الحجرات : ۱۷] (۱۰) (۱).
And what clarifies the answer to the utmost clarity is that the ruling of obligation 'Do' and 'Do not do' came with seeking help from God, exalted, in the very act commanded, as His statement: 'Guide us to the straight path' (al-Fatiha 5), and His statement: 'Our Lord, do not cause our hearts to deviate after You have guided us' (Al Imran 8/16). And whether the guidance itself is what is asked for in supplication or the steadfastness upon it is what is asked for, there is no doubt that if the servant were independent in originating it by his power (7), exclusive in being steadfast upon it, he would be needless of this seeking of help. Then God, exalted, bestows favor upon whom He wills of His servants by that He has guided them to faith. According to the opponent, it is interpreted as the creation of power, which is equally suitable for both opposites. That destroys the case of bestowal of favor through guidance. God, exalted, says: 'But God bestows favor upon you that He has guided you to faith' (al-Hujurat 17) (10) (1).
Source pages p. 85

Commentary
This passage provides a clarification (taḍwīḥ) of the overall answer by presenting an argument from the Quranic command to seek help (isti'āna) from God in performing obligations. The author points out that the commands 'do' and 'do not do' in Islamic law are accompanied by the injunction to ask God's assistance in the very actions being commanded. The author cites two Quranic verses: 'Guide us to the straight path' (Fatiha 5) and 'Our Lord, do not cause our hearts to deviate after You have guided us' (Al Imran 8/16). These supplications ask for either the guidance itself or steadfastness upon it. If the servant were independent in creating these actions by his own power and exclusive in maintaining them, he would have no need to ask God for help. Moreover, God's bestowal of favor (minna) by guiding people to faith is cited: 'But God bestows favor upon you that He has guided you to faith' (Hujurat 17). According to the opposing view (the Mu'tazila), such verses are interpreted as referring to God creating the power which is equally suitable for both belief and unbelief, thus nullifying the very meaning of divine grace (imtinān) through guidance. The author argues that if the servant were self-sufficient, the sense of favor would be lost. This passage reinforces the Ash'ari position that all actions are ultimately from God and the servant is dependent on Him.

Key Terms
الاستعانة
الهداية
الامتنان
التكليف

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: That the need for God's help implies the servant is completely passive and has no role in his actions.
Correction: The servant still acts, but his power and guidance come from God; he is not independent. The Quranic command to seek help assumes the servant's effort, but that effort is enabled by God.

Checkpoint
What is the main point the author makes by citing the verses about seeking help?
A) The servant is completely passive and does not act.
B) The servant must call upon God because he is independent.
C) The servant's need for God's help in the very act commanded proves he is not independent.
D) Seeking help is merely recommended, not required.

Key Takeaway
The Quran's command to seek help in performing obligations proves that all actions are dependent on God.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you recite 'Guide us to the straight path' in every rak'ah, do you truly sense your desperate need for God's guidance at that very moment, or is it a rote phrase? If you knew that without His constant help you would instantly stray, how would your prayer change starting today?
?
The opponent's view strips God of His favor, making guidance a neutral event. Have you ever, even subtly, felt that your faith is the result of your own superior choice or effort? In what specific area of your life do you need to repent of this hidden pride and acknowledge that He is the One who favored you?
?
If you were left to your own power for even a second, you could not remain steadfast. What is one concrete step you will take today to remind yourself of your absolute dependence on God—such as a specific du'a or a moment of reflection—so that you live in constant isti'ana?

The Meaning of Divine Oneness
Defines 'al-wāḥid' and refutes dualists and philosophers.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the Islamic theological definition of divine unity (tawḥīd) as the absolute indivisibility and uniqueness of God in essence, attributes, and acts, and how this refutes dualism and philosophical views that compromise it.

Core Idea
Divine unity means God is indivisible in essence, without likeness in attributes, and without partner in actions.

This passage from Ashʿarī theologian al-Shahrastānī sets out the third foundation of tawḥīd: the meaning of 'the One' (al-wāḥid). It not only defines the term but also contrasts the Ashʿarī view with that of the philosophers and the Muʿtazila, showing why no true rival deity can exist. By the end, you'll see how this definition guards against both explicit dualism and subtle philosophical denials of divine attributes.

Guided Path
1
Read the Ashʿarī definition
Read the definition of al-wāḥid given by aṣḥābinā (our scholars). Note its key features: no division, no association.
This definition is the foundation of the entire discussion.
2
Examine the three aspects of oneness
The definition expands to three respects: essence (no parts), attributes (no likeness), and actions (no partner). Recognize how each aspect reinforces absolute unity.
3
Contrast with the philosophers
The philosophers define unity by negation of parts (quantitative, definitional, essential). Understand why this leads them to deny attributes.
Philosophers' view: wājib al-wujūd must be one from every aspect.
4
Note the Muʿtazila position
The Muʿtazila agree with the philosophers in denying attributes, though with differences. Recognize that this is a major theological fault line.
The author promises a separate discussion on attributes.
5
Understand the refutation of dualism
The author notes that no sect truly affirms two fully equal gods. The dualists affirm two eternal beings but do not equate them in all respects. This clarifies the polemical target.
The passage ends mid-sentence, but the point is clear: true divine oneness is absolute.

⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
القاعدة الثالثة في التوحيد وفيها الرد على الثنوية، وتستدعي هذه المسألة سبق ذكر الوحدانية ومعنى الواحد. قال أصحابنا: الواحد هو الشيء الذي لا يصح انقسامه، إذ لا تقبل ذاته القسمة بوجه، ولا تقبل الشركة بوجه، فالباري تعالى واحد في ذاته لا قسم له، وواحد في صفاته لا شبيه له، وواحد في أفعاله لا شريك له، وقد أقمنا الدلالة على انفراده بأفعاله، فلنقم الدلالة على انفراده بذاته وصفاته. وقالت الفلاسفة: واجب الوجود بذاته لا يجوز أن يكون أجزاء كمية، ولا أجزاء حد قولا، ولا أجزاء ذات فعلا ووجودا، وواجب الوجود لن يتصور إلا واحدا من كل وجه، فلا يتصور ولا يتحقق موجودان كل واحد منهما واجب بذاته، وعن هذا نفوا الصفات، وإن أطلقوها عليه فبمعنى آخر كما سنذكره. ووافقهم المعتزلة على ذلك، غير أنهم مختلفون في التفصيل، وسنفرد لإثبات الصفات مسألة ونذكر المذهبين فيها، وهذه المسألة مقصورة على استحالة وجود الإلهين، يثبت لكل واحد منهما من خصائص الإلهية ما يثبت للثاني، ولست أعرف صاحب مقالة صار إلى هذا المذهب؛ لأن الثنوية وإن صارت إلى إثبات قديمين لم تثبت لأحدهما ما ثبت للثاني من كل وجه، والفلاسفة وإن قضوا بكون
The third foundation on Tawḥīd (divine unity). It contains the refutation of the dualists. And this issue requires prior mention of Tawḥīd and the meaning of 'the One' (al-wāḥid). Our scholars (aṣḥābinā) said: 'The One is the thing whose division is not valid, for its essence does not accept division in any respect and does not accept association in any respect. Thus the Creator (al-Bārī), exalted is He, is One in His essence, having no division; One in His attributes, having no likeness; and One in His actions, having no partner. And we have established the proof of His uniqueness (infirād) in His actions, so let us establish the proof of His uniqueness in His essence and attributes.' The philosophers (falāsifa) said: 'The Necessary of Existence (wājib al-wujūd) by virtue of itself cannot have quantitative parts, nor parts of definition in speech, nor parts of essence as act and existence. And the Necessary of Existence cannot be conceived except as one from every aspect; thus it is not conceivable nor can it be actualized that there exist two beings each of which is necessary by virtue of itself. And because of this they negated the attributes (ṣifāt), even if they apply them to Him, it is in another sense, as we will mention.' The Muʿtazila agreed with them on that, except that they differ in detail. And we will devote a separate issue for the proof of the attributes and mention the two schools therein. And this issue is confined to the impossibility of the existence of two gods (ilāhayn), each of which would have established for it from the characteristics of divinity what is established for the other. And I do not know any proponent of a doctrine who has gone to this [dualist] school; because the dualists (thunawiyya), although they went to affirming two eternal beings, did not establish for one of them what is established for the other from every aspect. And even if the philosophers judged that...
Source pages p. 87

Commentary
This passage is a combined definition, proof, and refutation concerning the oneness of God. It begins by presenting the third foundation in the study of tawḥīd, which includes a refutation of dualists. The author states that this issue requires understanding the term 'al-wāḥid' (the One).

The author first quotes the Ashʿarī scholars (aṣḥābinā) defining 'al-wāḥid': it is a thing that cannot be divided, because its essence does not accept division or participation in any way. This definition is immediately expanded into three aspects: the Creator (al-Bārī) is One in His essence (no parts), One in His attributes (no likeness), and One in His actions (no partner). The author then mentions that they have already proven uniqueness in actions, so now they will establish uniqueness in essence and attributes.

Next, the author presents the philosophers' view: the Necessary Existence (wājib al-wujūd) cannot have any kind of parts—quantitative, definitional, or essential—and can only be one from every angle. Therefore, it is impossible for two necessary beings to exist. Because of this, they deny the attributes, though some may use the term in a different sense. The Muʿtazila are said to agree with the philosophers on this negation of attributes, though with differences in detail; the author promises a separate discussion on proving the attributes.

Finally, the author clarifies that the current discussion is limited to the impossibility of two gods existing such that each would have all the properties of divinity equally. He notes that no known sect actually holds this extreme view: the dualists (thunawiyya) affirm two ancient beings but do not fully equate them, and the philosophers likewise do not claim two equal necessary beings. The passage ends mid-sentence, but the argument is clear: the oneness of God is absolute and admits no rival.

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Divine unity means only that God is one in number, allowing for complexity or partners.
Correction: The kalam definition of al-wāḥid emphasizes indivisibility and absence of association in essence, attributes, and acts—true oneness is qualitative and absolute.

Checkpoint
According to the scholars (aṣḥābinā) as quoted in the passage, what does 'al-wāḥid' (the One) mean?
A) The thing that is singular in number and has no equal.
B) The thing whose division is not valid, for its essence does not accept division or association in any respect.
C) The thing that is simple and cannot be conceived by the mind.
D) The thing that exists necessarily and has no parts.

Key Takeaway
True divine unity means God is indivisible in essence, has no likeness in attributes, and no partner in actions—no being can share any of these.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you call upon God in your prayers, do you ever mentally divide His attributes—focusing on His mercy as if it were separate from His justice? How does the definition of al-wāḥid (indivisible in essence and attributes) challenge you to worship Him as a unified whole? What specific thought or action will you change today to reflect this?
?
The text states that God has 'no likeness in attributes' (لا شبيه له). Have you ever imagined God in human terms—attributing to Him human emotions or limitations? What image from your heart do you need to repent of, and how will you actively replace it with the tawḥīd taught here?
?
If God's actions have 'no partner' (لا شريك له), are there areas in your life where you rely on created means more than on God? Ask yourself: in your career, health, or relationships, where have you given a 'partner' to God in your trust? Write one practical commitment to restore tawḥīd in that area beginning this week.

The Definition of al-Wahid and Threefold Unity
Sanusi defines al-Wahid and explains the threefold unity of God.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the definition of al-Wahid (the One) in Islamic theology and its threefold application to God's essence, attributes, and actions.

Core Idea
God is One in essence, attributes, and actions—nothing divides or partners with Him.

In this passage, the author establishes the foundational definition of divine oneness. The term 'al-Wahid' is not merely a numerical count but a comprehensive unity that excludes any division or partnership. This sets the stage for refuting all forms of polytheism and dualism.

Guided Path
1
Read the Arabic definition
Read carefully how the author defines 'al-Wahid' and note the negation of division and partnership.
2
Identify the three aspects of oneness
The author states that God is One in essence, attributes, and actions. Write down each one and its negation (no division, no similarity, no partner).
This threefold distinction is crucial.
3
Connect to the broader argument
Realize that this definition is the basis for proving God's uniqueness. The author says proofs for actions are established; now attention turns to essence and attributes.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
القاعدة الثالثة في التوحيد الواحد. وفيها الرد على الثنوية وتستدعي هذه المسألة سبق ذكر الوحدانية ومعنى. قال أصحابنا : الواحد هو الشيء الذي لا يصح انقسامه، إذ لا تقبل ذاته القسمة بوجه ولا تقبل الشركة بوجه فالباري تعالى واحد في ذاته لا قسم له، وواحد في صفاته لا شبيه له وواحد في أفعاله لا شريك له، وقد أقمنا الدلالة على انفراده بأفعاله، فلنقم الدلالة على انفرادها بذاته وصفاته. وقالت الفلاسفة : واجب الوجود بذاته لا يجوز أن يكون أجزاء كمية ولا أجزاء حد قولا، ولا أجزاء ذات فعلا ووجودًا، وواجب الوجود لن يتصور إلا واحدًا من كل وجه ، فلا يتصور ولا يتحقق موجودان كل واحد منهما واجب بذاته، وعن هذا نفوا الصفات وإن أطلقوها عليه فبمعنى آخر كما سنذكره. ووافقهم المعتزلة على ذلك، غير أنهم مختلفون في التفصيل، وسنفرد لإثبات الصفات مسألة ونذكر المذهبين فيها، وهذه المسألة مقصورة على استحالة وجود الإلهين، يثبت لكل واحد منهما من خصائص الإلهية ما يثبت للثاني، ولست أعرف صاحب مقالة صار إلى هذا المذهب؛ لأن الثنوية وإن صارت إلى إثبات قديمين لم تثبت لأحدهما ما ثبت للثاني من كل وجه والفلاسفة وإن قضوا بكون العقل والنفس أزليين، وقضوا بكون الحركات سرمدية لم يثبتوا للمعلول خصائص العلة كيف وأحدهما علة والثاني معلول والصابية وإن أثبتوا كون الروحانيين والهياكل أزلية سرمدية مدبرة لهذا العالم وسموها أربابا وآلهة، فلم يثبتوا فيها خصايص رب الأرباب، ودلالة التمانع في القرآن مسرودة على من يثبت خالقا دون الله سبحانه وتعالى، قال الله تعالى : ( إِذًا لَّذَهَبَ كُلُّ إِلَٰهٍ بِمَا خَلَقَ المؤمنون : ٩١، وعن هذا صار أبو الحسن رحمه الله إلى أن أخص وصف الإله هو على الاختراع فلا يشاركه فيه غيره، ومن أثبت فيه شركة فقد أثبت إلهين.
The third principle on tawheed: The One. And in it is the refutation of the dualists. This issue requires prior mention of tawheed and its meaning. Our associates said: The One is the thing whose division is not valid, for His essence does not accept division in any way nor does it accept partnership in any way. So the Exalted Creator is One in His essence with no division, One in His attributes with no similarity, and One in His actions with no partner. We have established the proof of His uniqueness in His actions, so let us establish the proof of His uniqueness in His essence and attributes. The philosophers said: The Necessary Existence by itself cannot have quantitative parts, nor parts of definition in terms of genus and differentia, nor parts of essence in actuality and existence. The Necessary Existence cannot be conceived except as one in every respect, so it cannot be conceived or realized that two existents each of which is necessary by itself exist. And because of this they denied the attributes, and if they applied them to Him, it is in another sense as we will mention. The Mu'tazila agreed with them on that, except they differ in detail. We will dedicate an issue to affirming the attributes and mention the two schools on it. And this issue is confined to the impossibility of the existence of two gods, each of whom would have the specificities of divinity ascribed to him as the other. I do not know any proponent of a doctrine who holds this belief, because the dualists, though they affirm two eternals, do not affirm for one what is affirmed for the other in every respect. The philosophers, though they judge that the Intellect and the Soul are eternal, and they judge that movements are beginningless, they do not affirm for the effect the characteristics of the cause, how (can they) when one is cause and the other effect? The Sabians, though they affirm that spiritual beings and temples are eternal, beginningless, managing this world, and call them lords and gods, they do not affirm in them the characteristics of the Lord of lords. And the argument of mutual hindrance (dālālāt al-tamānu') in the Qur'an is presented against one who affirms a creator besides Allah. Allah said: 'Then each god would have taken away what he created' (Q 23:91). And because of this, Abu al-Hasan (al-Ash'ari) came to the view that the most specific attribute of God is creation (al-ikhtirā'), so no one else shares it with Him. And whoever affirms partnership in it has affirmed two gods.
Source pages p. 88

Commentary
This passage defines 'the One' (al-wahid) in the context of divine oneness (tawheed). The author begins by stating that this principle involves refuting dualists. He first cites the definition of our scholars (Ahl al-Sunnah): the One is that which cannot be divided, because His essence does not accept division or partnership. Then he explains that God is One in essence (no division), One in attributes (no similarity), and One in actions (no partner). He then presents the view of philosophers, who define the Necessary Existence as having no parts, and conclude that there cannot be two necessary existents; this leads them to deny attributes. The Mu'tazila agree but differ in details. The author clarifies that the exact issue is the impossibility of two gods each possessing full divine properties. He notes that no known sect holds this precisely: dualists affirm two eternals but not equal in all respects; philosophers affirm eternal intellect and soul but one is cause and the other effect; Sabians affirm many lords but not with the full attributes of the Lord of lords. The Quranic verse (23:91) is presented as the argument of mutual hindrance (tamānu'). Finally, Abu al-Hasan al-Ash'ari concluded that the most specific attribute of God is creation (ikhtirā'), so sharing it implies two gods.

Misconception: Some think 'one' simply means numerically one, but the definition includes unity in essence, attributes, and actions.
Correction: The passage emphasizes that God is One in a comprehensive sense, not just numerically.

Checkpoint

A) Numerically one
B) The thing whose division is not valid
C) The first in a sequence
D) The only creator

Key Takeaway
The One (al-Wahid) is defined as that which cannot be divided or shared, and God is One in essence, attributes, and actions.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
If God is One in essence without division, then every part of your being is owned by Him. What part of your life are you still claiming for yourself as if it were not His? Will you surrender that to Him today?
?
God is One in actions with no partner. In what area of your life do you act as if you are the one in control, forgetting that all power belongs to Him alone? How can you remind yourself of His sole sovereignty in that area starting now?
?
God is One in attributes with no similarity. Have you ever imagined God in human terms, limiting His majesty to your understanding? What steps will you take to ensure your conception of God is based solely on how He describes Himself, not on your imagination?

Philosophers and Mu'tazila on Divine Oneness
Sanusi presents the philosophical and Mu'tazilite perspectives on divine oneness and attributes.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the philosophers' and Mu'tazila's view on divine oneness, including their denial of attributes and the argument against two necessary existents.

Core Idea
The philosophers argue that the Necessary Existence must be one in every respect, leading them to deny separate attributes.

The author presents the philosophical perspective on tawheed, which arrives at a similar conclusion of absolute oneness but through a different reasoning. This view denies that God has distinct attributes, a position that the Mu'tazila also adopt, though with nuances. This lesson clarifies that reasoning and its consequences.

Guided Path
1
Read the philosophers' position
Note their definition of wājib al-wujūd as having no parts of any kind.
2
Understand the denial of attributes
Because God must be one in every respect, philosophers deny distinct attributes. See why this follows.
They argue that attributes would imply multiplicity.
3
Note the Mu'tazila agreement
The Mu'tazila agreed on this denial but differ in detail. Recognize that the author will later address the affirmation of attributes.
This sets up a later debate.

Refuting the Possibility of Two Gods and the Argument of Mutual Hindrance
Sanusi refutes the possibility of two equal gods and presents the argument of mutual hindrance.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the refutation of the possibility of two gods, the arguments from mutual hindrance, and Ash'ari's conclusion that creation is the exclusive attribute of God.

Core Idea
No sect truly affirms two equal gods, and the Quranic argument of mutual hindrance shows the impossibility of two creators.

This lesson completes the discussion on divine oneness by addressing the hypothetical case of two gods. The author surveys various sects—dualists, philosophers, Sabians—to show that none actually ascribe full divine attributes to two beings. He then invokes the Quranic argument of mutual hindrance and concludes with al-Ash'ari's identification of creation (ikhtirā') as God's exclusive attribute.

Guided Path
1
Read the survey of sects
The author shows that dualists, philosophers, and Sabians do not affirm two equal gods. Note the distinctions.
2
Analyze the Quranic argument
The verse 23:91 is cited as dālālāt al-tamānu'. Understand its logic: if there were two gods, each would take away what the other created, leading to chaos.
This is a proof of tawheed.
3
Learn al-Ash'ari's conclusion
Abu al-Hasan al-Ash'ari states that the most specific attribute of God is creation (al-ikhtirā'), so sharing it implies polytheism.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
القاعدة الثالثة في التوحيد الواحد. وفيها الرد على الثنوية وتستدعي هذه المسألة سبق ذكر الوحدانية ومعنى. قال أصحابنا : الواحد هو الشيء الذي لا يصح انقسامه، إذ لا تقبل ذاته القسمة بوجه ولا تقبل الشركة بوجه فالباري تعالى واحد في ذاته لا قسم له، وواحد في صفاته لا شبيه له وواحد في أفعاله لا شريك له، وقد أقمنا الدلالة على انفراده بأفعاله، فلنقم الدلالة على انفرادها بذاته وصفاته. وقالت الفلاسفة : واجب الوجود بذاته لا يجوز أن يكون أجزاء كمية ولا أجزاء حد قولا، ولا أجزاء ذات فعلا ووجودًا، وواجب الوجود لن يتصور إلا واحدًا من كل وجه ، فلا يتصور ولا يتحقق موجودان كل واحد منهما واجب بذاته، وعن هذا نفوا الصفات وإن أطلقوها عليه فبمعنى آخر كما سنذكره. ووافقهم المعتزلة على ذلك، غير أنهم مختلفون في التفصيل، وسنفرد لإثبات الصفات مسألة ونذكر المذهبين فيها، وهذه المسألة مقصورة على استحالة وجود الإلهين، يثبت لكل واحد منهما من خصائص الإلهية ما يثبت للثاني، ولست أعرف صاحب مقالة صار إلى هذا المذهب؛ لأن الثنوية وإن صارت إلى إثبات قديمين لم تثبت لأحدهما ما ثبت للثاني من كل وجه والفلاسفة وإن قضوا بكون العقل والنفس أزليين، وقضوا بكون الحركات سرمدية لم يثبتوا للمعلول خصائص العلة كيف وأحدهما علة والثاني معلول والصابية وإن أثبتوا كون الروحانيين والهياكل أزلية سرمدية مدبرة لهذا العالم وسموها أربابا وآلهة، فلم يثبتوا فيها خصايص رب الأرباب، ودلالة التمانع في القرآن مسرودة على من يثبت خالقا دون الله سبحانه وتعالى، قال الله تعالى : ( إِذًا لَّذَهَبَ كُلُّ إِلَٰهٍ بِمَا خَلَقَ المؤمنون : ٩١، وعن هذا صار أبو الحسن رحمه الله إلى أن أخص وصف الإله هو على الاختراع فلا يشاركه فيه غيره، ومن أثبت فيه شركة فقد أثبت إلهين.
The third principle on tawheed: The One. And in it is the refutation of the dualists. This issue requires prior mention of tawheed and its meaning. Our associates said: The One is the thing whose division is not valid, for His essence does not accept division in any way nor does it accept partnership in any way. So the Exalted Creator is One in His essence with no division, One in His attributes with no similarity, and One in His actions with no partner. We have established the proof of His uniqueness in His actions, so let us establish the proof of His uniqueness in His essence and attributes. The philosophers said: The Necessary Existence by itself cannot have quantitative parts, nor parts of definition in terms of genus and differentia, nor parts of essence in actuality and existence. The Necessary Existence cannot be conceived except as one in every respect, so it cannot be conceived or realized that two existents each of which is necessary by itself exist. And because of this they denied the attributes, and if they applied them to Him, it is in another sense as we will mention. The Mu'tazila agreed with them on that, except they differ in detail. We will dedicate an issue to affirming the attributes and mention the two schools on it. And this issue is confined to the impossibility of the existence of two gods, each of whom would have the specificities of divinity ascribed to him as the other. I do not know any proponent of a doctrine who holds this belief, because the dualists, though they affirm two eternals, do not affirm for one what is affirmed for the other in every respect. The philosophers, though they judge that the Intellect and the Soul are eternal, and they judge that movements are beginningless, they do not affirm for the effect the characteristics of the cause, how (can they) when one is cause and the other effect? The Sabians, though they affirm that spiritual beings and temples are eternal, beginningless, managing this world, and call them lords and gods, they do not affirm in them the characteristics of the Lord of lords. And the argument of mutual hindrance (dālālāt al-tamānu') in the Qur'an is presented against one who affirms a creator besides Allah. Allah said: 'Then each god would have taken away what he created' (Q 23:91). And because of this, Abu al-Hasan (al-Ash'ari) came to the view that the most specific attribute of God is creation (al-ikhtirā'), so no one else shares it with Him. And whoever affirms partnership in it has affirmed two gods.
Source pages p. 88

Commentary
This passage defines 'the One' (al-wahid) in the context of divine oneness (tawheed). The author begins by stating that this principle involves refuting dualists. He first cites the definition of our scholars (Ahl al-Sunnah): the One is that which cannot be divided, because His essence does not accept division or partnership. Then he explains that God is One in essence (no division), One in attributes (no similarity), and One in actions (no partner). He then presents the view of philosophers, who define the Necessary Existence as having no parts, and conclude that there cannot be two necessary existents; this leads them to deny attributes. The Mu'tazila agree but differ in details. The author clarifies that the exact issue is the impossibility of two gods each possessing full divine properties. He notes that no known sect holds this precisely: dualists affirm two eternals but not equal in all respects; philosophers affirm eternal intellect and soul but one is cause and the other effect; Sabians affirm many lords but not with the full attributes of the Lord of lords. The Quranic verse (23:91) is presented as the argument of mutual hindrance (tamānu'). Finally, Abu al-Hasan al-Ash'ari concluded that the most specific attribute of God is creation (ikhtirā'), so sharing it implies two gods.

Key Terms
الثنوية
الفلاسفة
الصابئة
دلالة التمانع
الاختراع

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some think that dualists believe in two equal gods, but they do not ascribe full divine properties to both.
Correction: The text clarifies that dualists only affirm two eternals, not equal in all aspects.

Checkpoint
What is the argument of mutual hindrance (dālālāt al-tamānu') based on?
A) The impossibility of two creators
B) The need for a cause
C) The diversity of religions
D) The unity of the universe

Key Takeaway
The impossibility of two equal gods is proven by the Quranic argument of mutual hindrance, and creation is God's unique attribute.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?

Do you claim ownership over your achievements, forgetting that every outcome is created by Him alone? How can you humble yourself today and attribute every good in your life back to its true Source?

The Sabians had many lords but not with full divine attributes. Is there any 'lord' in your life—a person, a system, a habit—that you treat as if it has independent authority over you, while not overtly calling it God? What step will you take to dethrone it and submit only to the Rabb al-Arbab?

Proof of Impossibility of Two Gods (Tamānu')
Proof for impossibility of two gods via three possibilities.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the logical proof against the possibility of two gods through the argument of mutual hindrance (tamānu').

Core Idea
Two gods cannot coexist because their wills would contradict.

This lesson presents a rational proof from Islamic theology demonstrating that the existence of two gods is impossible. The argument uses a simple hypothetical scenario involving a single body and two conflicting wills. By examining three logical possibilities, the proof shows that each leads to an impossibility, thereby establishing tawhid.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage and its translation to grasp the overall argument about two gods willing opposite actions on one body.
Notice the three possibilities laid out: both wills succeed, both fail, or one succeeds.
2
Analyze each possibility
Examine each of the three logical outcomes. Why does each one lead to an impossibility? Relate them to the attributes of divinity (will, power, independence).
3
Connect to the Quranic verse
Read the verse quoted (Q 23:91) and see how it supports the rational conclusion that two gods would lead to conflict and superiority.
The verse reinforces the logical argument.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فدليلنا على استحالة وجود إلهين أنا فرضنا الكلام في جسم، وقدرنا من أحدهما إرادة تحريكه، ومن الثاني إرادة تسكينه في وقت واحد لم يخل الحال من أحد الثلاثة أمور، إما أن تنفذ إرادتهما فيؤدي إلى اجتماع الحركة والسكون في محل واحد في حالة واحدة وذلك بين الاستحالة، وإما أن لا تنفذ إرادتهما فيؤدي إلى عجز وقصور في إلهية كل واحد منهما وخلو المحل عن الضدين وذلك أيضا بين الاستحالة وإما أن تنفرد إرادة أحدهما دون الثاني فيصير الثاني مغلوبًا على إرادته، ممنوعًا من فعله مضطرًا في إمساكه، وذلك ينافي الإلهية قال الله تعالى : ( وَلَعَلَا بَعْضُهُمْ عَلَى بَعْض المؤمنون: ٩١).
Our proof for the impossibility of the existence of two gods is that we suppose the discussion concerning a body, and we assume from one of them the will to move it, and from the second the will to still it at the same time. The situation could not be other than one of three matters: either both wills are executed, leading to the combination of motion and rest in one place at one time, and that is clearly impossible; or both wills are not executed, leading to incapacity and deficiency in the divinity of each of them and the locus being free of both opposites, and that is also clearly impossible; or the will of one of them is carried out to the exclusion of the second, and then the second becomes overcome in his will, prevented from his action, constrained in his withholding, and that contradicts divinity. Allah said: 'And some of them would have exalted themselves over others' (Q 23:91).
Source pages p. 89

Commentary
This passage provides a rational proof (dalīl) for the impossibility of two gods using the argument of mutual hindrance (tamānu'). The author sets up a hypothetical: suppose two gods will opposite actions on the same body at the same time—one wants to move it, the other to stop it. Three possibilities are examined. First, if both wills succeed, motion and rest would coexist in the same place simultaneously, which is impossible. Second, if neither succeeds, both gods are incapable, negating divinity. Third, if only one succeeds, the other is overcome, which also negates divinity. The conclusion is that two gods cannot exist. The verse (23:91) is cited in support.

Key Terms
الإلهين
التمانع
الإرادة
القدرة
المحل
الإلهية

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some think that the third possibility (one god overcomes the other) could be acceptable, because one god still acts.
Correction: The author shows that an overcome god is not truly divine, as divinity requires absolute power and independence. Being constrained negates divinity.

Checkpoint
In the proof against two gods, what happens if neither will is executed?
A) Both wills combine
B) Incapacity and deficiency in divinity
C) One god overcomes the other
D) The body moves

Key Takeaway
The proof of tamānu' shows that any scenario with two gods leads to an impossibility, establishing that only one God can exist.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
If two gods cannot coexist because of contradiction in will, what does that say about the absolute unity of the One God who has no rival? How does this truth affect your trust in His control over every detail of your life?
?
The proof shows that a god whose will can be overcome is not truly God. Have you ever, in moments of hardship, doubted that Allah's will is always supreme? What would it take for you to surrender every desire that opposes His decree?
?
The impossibility of two gods means that every event in the universe—from the movement of an atom to the rise of a nation—is under the single, uninterrupted will of Allah. If you truly believed this with certainty, what worry or anxiety would you abandon today, and why?

The Proof from Simultaneous Contradictory Wills
Proof of impossibility of two gods via contradictory wills on a single body

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how the proof of mutual hindrance (tamānuʿ) demonstrates the impossibility of two gods by analyzing the logical outcomes of contradictory wills.

Core Idea
Two gods cannot coexist because their wills would clash, leading to inevitable absurdities.

This lesson presents a classical Kalam argument known as the proof of mutual hindrance (dalīl al-tamānuʿ). It uses a concrete example of a body and two contradictory intentions to demonstrate that the existence of two gods leads to unavoidable absurdities. Let's examine the three possible scenarios and their implications.

Guided Path
1
Read the source passage carefully
Begin by reading the Arabic passage and its English translation. Pay attention to the logical structure: a scenario is set up, then three possibilities are examined.
The proof is concise; each possibility is stated and then dismissed as impossible.
2
Understand the scenario
The proof assumes a body and two gods: one wills to move it, the other wills to keep it still at the same time. This sets up a conflict of wills.
The body is just an example; the logic applies to any contingent act.
3
Analyze the three possibilities
The author lists three outcomes: both wills succeed, both fail, or only one succeeds. Each leads to an impossibility: contradiction, impotence, or subjugation.
The third possibility (one succeeds) makes the other god less than divine.
4
Reflect on the Quranic support
The verse (23:91) is cited to reinforce the argument. Consider how the logical proof aligns with the revealed text.
The verse speaks of each god taking away its creation, implying conflict.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فدليلنا على استحالة وجود إلهين أنا فرضنا الكلام في جسم، وقدرنا من أحدهما إرادة تحريكه، ومن الثاني إرادة تسكينه في وقت واحد لم يخل الحال من أحد الثلاثة أمور، إما أن تنفذ إرادتهما فيؤدي إلى اجتماع الحركة والسكون في محل واحد في حالة واحدة وذلك بين الاستحالة، وإما أن لا تنفذ إرادتهما فيؤدي إلى عجز وقصور في إلهية كل واحد منهما وخلو المحل عن الضدين وذلك أيضا بين الاستحالة وإما أن تنفرد إرادة أحدهما دون الثاني فيصير الثاني مغلوبًا على إرادته، ممنوعًا من فعله مضطرًا في إمساكه، وذلك ينافي الإلهية قال الله تعالى : ( وَلَعَلَا بَعْضُهُمْ عَلَى بَعْض المؤمنون: ٩١).
Our proof for the impossibility of the existence of two gods is that we suppose discourse concerning a body, and we posit from one of them an intention to move it, and from the second an intention to still it at one time. The case cannot be except one of three matters: either both intentions are executed, leading to the combination of motion and rest in one locus at one state, and that is clearly impossible; or neither intention is executed, leading to incapacity and deficiency in the divinity of each of them and the absence of both opposites from the locus, and that is also clearly impossible; or the intention of one of them alone is executed without the second, so the second becomes overcome in his intention, prevented from his action, compelled in his restraint, and that contradicts divinity. God Almighty said: 'Then would each have taken away what it created' (al-Mu'minun: 91).
Source pages p. 89

Commentary
This passage presents the classical Kalam proof of mutual hindrance (دليل التمانع) specifically through the case of contradictory intentions. The author sets up a scenario: if there were two gods, consider a body and suppose that one god wills to move it while the other wills to still it at the same moment. Three possibilities arise: (1) both wills are executed, causing motion and rest to coexist in the same body at the same time, which is impossible; (2) neither will is executed, meaning both gods fail to achieve their will, implying impotence and imperfection in divinity, also impossible; (3) only one will is executed, making the other god overcome, prevented, and compelled, which contradicts the nature of divinity as sovereign. The Quranic verse (23:91) is cited as support: if there were multiple gods, each would have taken away its creation. This proof establishes the impossibility of two gods through a logical analysis of simultaneous contradictory wills.

Key Terms
تمانع
إله
إرادة
حركة
سكون

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The proof only applies to physical bodies or specific acts like motion.
Correction: The proof uses a body as an example, but the logic applies to any contingent act. The principle (tamānuʿ) is general: two gods cannot coexist because any conflict of will leads to impossibility.

Checkpoint

A) Two
B) Three
C) Four
D) Five

Key Takeaway
The proof of mutual hindrance (tamānuʿ) logically establishes that two gods cannot exist because any contradictory wills lead to inevitable absurdities.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?

?

?
The proof shows that even a single moment of contradiction would prove two gods false. Yet in your life, do you sometimes tolerate contradictions in your faith—saying you believe in one God but acting as if multiple forces control your destiny? How can this proof compel you to eliminate those contradictions today?

The Proof from Two Wills on a Single Act
Proof against two gods from concurrence on a single creative act

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why two gods cannot jointly create a single act, and how this disproves polytheism.

Core Idea
Two gods cannot share a single act of creation.

This lesson extends the argument against multiple gods by considering what happens if two gods direct their will and power toward the very same act of creation. The passage presents a logical dilemma that reveals the impossibility of sharing in the act of bringing something into existence.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without trying to analyse it. Then read the translation. Let the author's own rhythm come through before you interpret.
Notice the concise style — two clear possibilities are laid out.
2
Identify the two possibilities
The passage presents two alternatives: either the two gods share in the act of bringing into existence, or one does it alone. Identify which one is deemed impossible and why.
The key is that creation is a 'single decree' that cannot be split.
3
Understand the conclusion
The passage concludes that if one god acts alone, the other is 'overcome and subdued' — a state unworthy of divinity. Thus, only one can be the true god.
This proof reinforces the principle that divinity requires complete power and independence.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وكذلك لو فرضنا في توارد الإرادة والاقتدار على فعل واحد، فإما أن يشتركا في نفس الإيجاد وهو قضية واحدة لا يقبل الاشتراك، وإما أن ينفرد أحدهما بالإيجاد فيكون المنفرد هو الإله والثاني يكون مغلوبًا مقهوراً.
And likewise, if we suppose the concurrence of will and power upon a single act, then either they share in the very bringing into existence, which is a single decree that does not admit of sharing, or one of them alone brings into existence, so the one who is alone is the god, and the second becomes overcome and subdued.
Source pages p. 89

Commentary
This passage extends the proof to the case where both gods direct their will and power toward the same single act. Two possibilities are considered: (1) they both participate in the act of bringing the thing into existence, but an act of creation is a single indivisible event and cannot be shared; (2) one of them acts alone, making him the true god while the other is overcome and subdued, which does not befit divinity. This argument shows that two gods cannot coordinate on a single creative act.

Key Terms
توارد
إرادة
اقتدار
إيجاد

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Two gods could cooperate in creating the same thing by sharing the work.
Correction: The passage argues that creation is a single indivisible act; it cannot be shared. Therefore, two gods cannot cooperate in creating one thing.

Checkpoint

C) One alone creates, making the other subdued

Key Takeaway
The proof shows that only one god can be the creator of any particular act, disproving polytheism.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?

The Proof from Two Different Acts
Proof that separate creations lead to mutual domination.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how the independence of two separate gods logically implies mutual domination, undermining their divinity.

Core Idea
Independence from each other actually means dependence through superiority.

This passage presents a subtle argument against the possibility of two gods with separate domains. It shows that even if each god is independent in his own creation, that very independence becomes a claim of superiority, leading to conflict. Let's examine the logical steps.

Guided Path
1
Read the argument
Read the Arabic passage and its translation. Notice the key terms: استغناء (independence) and افتقار (dependence).
The argument is short but packed.
2
Identify the logical move
The passage claims that independence leads to dependence. Trace how independence becomes superiority, and superiority implies domination.
This is a paradox that reveals a flaw in the scenario.
3
Reflect on the implication
Consider why this argument refutes the existence of two gods. What does it say about the nature of divinity?
If two gods would dominate each other, neither is truly independent.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وكذلك لو فرضنا في فعلين متباينين حتى يذهب كل إله بما خلق فيكون استغناء كل واحد منهما عن الثاني افتقاراً إليه ; لأن نفس الاستغناء استعلاء وفي الاستعلاء إلزام قهر وغلبة على الثاني.
And likewise, if we suppose two different acts such that each god goes away with what he created, then the independence of each one from the other would be a dependence upon him, because independence itself is superiority, and in superiority there is an imposition of compulsion and domination over the other.
Source pages p. 90

Commentary
This argument considers the scenario where each god creates different, separate things. This would mean that each is independent of the other in his own domain. However, this independence is actually a form of dependence, because it implies that each god does not need the other, but that very independence is a claim of superiority and domination over the other. Thus, rather than being self-sufficient, each god would be asserting power over the other, leading to mutual rivalry or hierarchical deficiency. This undermines the concept of absolute divinity for both.

Key Terms
فعلين متباينين
استغناء
افتقار
قهر

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Independence among gods is harmless.
Correction: Independence implies superiority and domination, leading to conflict, which is incompatible with divine self-sufficiency.

Checkpoint

A) Because independence implies superiority and domination over the other
B) Because they need each other's creation
C) Because they cannot be independent
D) Because independence is impossible

Key Takeaway
The independence of two separate creators necessarily entails mutual domination, undermining their divinity.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?

Commit today to examine one relationship where you may be using 'independence' to control others, and instead practice humble reliance on Allah alone.
?
This argument shows that true independence is a claim to dominance. When you say 'I don't need anyone,' are you actually demanding that others submit to your way? What would it look like for you to be truly dependent on Allah, while serving others without needing to be above them?
?
The passage says independence itself is superiority. If only Allah can be independent without dominating others unjustly, how can your own desire for self-sufficiency be an attempt to usurp a divine attribute? Repent of any independence you have used to dominate others and embrace your need for Allah and for the believers.

The Proof from Absolute Independence (al-Istighna' bi al-Dhat)
Proof from mutual hindrance in independence by essence, stronger than the proof from action

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the proof from mutual hindrance in independence (al-istighna' bi al-dhat) and why it is considered stronger than the proof from action.

Core Idea
True absolute independence means being depended upon, not just being self-sufficient.

Sanusi strengthens the argument against polytheism by moving from the mutual hindrance in actions to a deeper proof: mutual hindrance in independence by essence. This argument reveals that if two beings were each independent of the other, neither would be truly independent, for absolute independence requires being the sole object of dependence. In this lesson, we unpack this concise but powerful reasoning.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once without analyzing. Then read the literal translation to grasp the flow.
Notice the structure: clarification, comparison, supposition, reasoning, Quranic citation.
2
Understand the definitions
Identify how the passage defines 'absolutely independent' (al-ghani 'ala al-itlaq). It says: he from whom one seeks independence and who is not independent of anyone.
This definition is key to the argument.
3
Follow the supposition and deduction
Assume two gods each independent of the other. Then ask: does either meet the definition? They are independent of each other, but they also are depended upon? Each is dependent? Actually, each is independent of the other, but neither is the one ultimately depended upon because each can do without the other. So they fall short of absolute independence.
The conclusion: two absolutely independent beings are impossible.
4
Connect to the Quranic verse
Reflect on verse 47:38: 'God is the Rich, and you are the poor'. This indicates that true independence belongs solely to God.
This serves as scriptural confirmation.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ولنزد هذا بيانا وشرحا فإن التمانع في الفعل إن أوجب استحالة الإلهين، والتمانع في الاستغناء بالذات أدل على استحالة الإلهين. فنقول : لو قدرنا إلهين استغنى كل واحد منهما عن صاحبه من كل وجه خرج المستغنى عنه عن كمال الاستغناء، فإن المستغنى على الإطلاق من يستغنى به ولا يستغنى عنه، فيكون كل واحد منهما مستغنى عنه، فيكون مفتقرا قاصرًا عن درجة الاستغناء المطلق، فلن يتصور إذا إلهان مستغنيان على الإطلاق ( الْغَنِيُّ وَأَنتُمُ الْفُقَرَآءُ وَإِن ( محمد : 138 إشارة إلى هذا المعنى
And we will increase this in clarification and explanation. For the mutual hindrance in action, if it necessitates the impossibility of two gods, then the mutual hindrance in independence by essence is more indicative of the impossibility of two gods. We say: if we suppose two gods, each being independent of his companion in every respect, then the one who is independent from the other departs from the perfection of independence. For the absolutely independent is he from whom one seeks independence and who is not independent of anyone. Thus each of them would be independent of the other, so each is one from whom one is independent, thus each is needy, deficient in the degree of absolute independence. Therefore, it is impossible to conceive two gods absolutely independent. The verse 'God is the Rich, and you are the poor' (Muhammad 38) is an indication of this meaning.
Source pages p. 90

Commentary
This passage introduces a stronger proof: the mutual hindrance in independence (التمانع في الاستغناء بالذات). The author argues that while the previous proofs from action are sufficient, the proof from absolute independence is even more definitive. He explains that if there were two gods, each would be completely independent of the other. However, absolute independence means being the one who is depended upon and not being dependent on anyone. If each is independent of the other, then neither is the one who is ultimately depended upon, because each can do without the other. This implies that they are not perfectly independent; they are needy in that they are not the sole object of dependence. Hence, two absolutely independent beings cannot coexist. The Quranic verse 'God is the Rich, and you are the poor' (Muhammad 38) is cited to indicate that true independence belongs only to God.

Key Terms
استغناء بالذات
تمانع
الغني
الفقير

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some think absolute independence means simply not needing anything or anyone.
Correction: The passage clarifies that absolute independence requires being the one depended upon (mustaghnā bihī) and not depending on anyone (lā yustaghnā ʿanhu). It is not merely self-sufficiency but being the ultimate source of sufficiency for all.

Checkpoint
According to the proof from absolute independence (استغناء بالذات), what is required for a being to be absolutely independent?
A) Not needing anything
B) Being depended upon and not being dependent on anyone
C) Being independent of all others
D) Having no creator

Key Takeaway
The impossibility of two gods is demonstrated by the definition of absolute independence: if two were independent, neither would be the ultimate object of dependence, thus both would be deficient.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Are there areas of your life where you are acting as if you are the ultimate source of your own sufficiency, independent of God? What would it mean to truly depend on God as the only one from whom all sufficiency comes? Make a concrete change this week to acknowledge your poverty before Him.
?
The passage says absolute independence requires being the one others depend on. In your relationships, do you strive to be a source of support for others (being 'dependable') or do you assert your independence from them? How does that reflect or contradict the nature of God's independence? Choose one relationship and actively put the other's needs before your own.
?
If you truly believed that God alone is al-Ghanī and you are al-faqīr (the poor), how would your daily worries and anxieties change? What would you need to let go of to live in that reality? Identify one worry you hold onto as if it depends on you, and surrender it to God in prayer today.

The Proof from Essential Attributes
Proof from essential attributes: difference or similarity both preclude two gods

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why the essential attributes argument proves the impossibility of multiple gods.

Core Idea
Two gods are impossible whether they are different or identical in essential attributes.

This passage presents a crisp logical argument based on the nature of essential attributes. The author considers the only two possibilities if two gods existed—difference or similarity—and shows both lead to contradiction. Understanding this reasoning is key to appreciating the uniqueness of Allah's nature.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without analysing. Then read the translation to get the overall argument.
Notice the conditional structure: 'if we suppose two gods... then either...'
2
Identify the two possibilities
The argument presents exactly two scenarios: the gods differ in essential attributes, or they are identical in them. Find and underline these two conditions.
These are exhaustive according to the author.
3
Understand the refutation of difference
If the gods differ in an essential attribute (like the attribute that sanctifies one as divine), then only the one possessing that attribute qualifies as god. The other lacks it, so cannot be a god.
This shows that difference leads to one god, not two.
4
Understand the refutation of similarity
If they are identical in all essential attributes, then their essences are indistinguishable. Any distinction would require external factors like place or time, which are contingent and unworthy of divinity.
The analogy of two blacknesses illustrates this point.
5
Reflect on the conclusion
Both branches lead to the impossibility of two gods. Reflect on how this argument supports tawhid (the oneness of Allah).

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ونقول : لو قدرنا إلهين؛ فإما أن يكونا مختلفين في الصفات الذاتية أو متماثلين، والمختلفان يستحيل أن يكونا إلهين؛ لأن الصفة الذاتية التي بها تقدس الإله عن غيره إذا كانا مختلفين فيها كان الذي اتصف بها هو الإله والمخالف ليس باله، وإما أن يكونا متماثلين في الصفات الذاتية من كل وجه فالمتماثلان ليس يتميز أحدهما عن الثاني بالحقيقة والخاصية، فإن حقيقتهما لواحد بل بلوازم زايدة على الحقيقة؛ مثل : المحل والمكان والزمان وكل ذلك ينافي الإلهية، أليس لما كان السوادان متماثلين لم يتميز أحدهما بحقيقته السوادية، بل باختلاف المحل أو باختلاف الزمان وكذلك
And we say: if we suppose two gods, then either they are different in essential attributes or similar. The different ones are impossible to be gods, because the essential attribute by which the god is sanctified above others, if they differ in it, then the one who is characterized by it is the god, and the other is not a god. And if they are similar in essential attributes in every respect, then the similar ones are not distinguished from each other by essence and specific property, for their essence is one. Rather, they would be distinguished by additional necessary concomitants, such as locus, place, and time, and all that contradicts divinity. Is it not so that when two blacknesses are similar, they are not distinguished by their black essence, but by difference of locus or difference of time, and similarly...
Source pages p. 90

Commentary
This passage introduces another argument based on essential attributes (الصفات الذاتية). Two gods must either differ in essential attributes or be identical. If they differ, then the one possessing the distinctive sanctifying attribute is the true god, and the other lacks it, thus not a god. If they are identical in all essential attributes, then they are indistinguishable in essence. Distinction would require external differentiators like locus, place, or time, which are contingent and unsuitable for divinity. An analogy is given: two blacknesses are not distinguished by their blackness but by location or time. Thus, two gods cannot be either different or identical; both scenarios lead to impossibility. The argument concludes that only one god can exist.

Key Terms
صفات ذاتية
متماثلين
مختلفين
تقدس

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some think that two identical essences could coexist as separate gods with no distinction.
Correction: The argument shows that if two essences are truly identical, they cannot be distinguished except by contingent properties like time and place, which are incompatible with divinity. Thus, identical essences cannot be two distinct gods.

Checkpoint
In the argument from essential attributes, if two gods were similar in all essential attributes, how would they be distinguished?
A) By their essence itself
B) They cannot be distinct
C) By additional necessary concomitants like time and place
D) By their actions

Key Takeaway
The essential attribute argument shows that the very concept of two gods is logically untenable.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
If the logical proof shows that only one God can exist, why do you sometimes fear or rely on created beings as if they hold power over your destiny? Examine your heart and commit to exclusive trust in Allah.
?
This argument proves that Allah cannot be limited by time or place. Do you still unconsciously imagine Him as being 'somewhere' or 'when'? How does this realization transform your worship?
?
The proof declares that no being shares Allah's essential attributes. Are there any attributes you ascribe to others that belong only to Him—like control over life, provision, or knowledge of the unseen? Repent and affirm His uniqueness.
