The Proof from Actions for God's Oneness
Shahrastānī proves God's oneness via the contingency of actions and the need for a single determinant.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how the contingency of observable actions proves that there can be only one God, and why multiple determinants are impossible.

Core Idea
One action requires one determinant—two would destroy independence.

Shahrastānī presents a rational argument for God's oneness based on the actions we observe. Since actions are contingent—they could exist or not—their existence requires a determinant. This argument shows that there cannot be two independent determinants, because that would lead to logical contradictions and undermine the attributes of God. Read the passage carefully to follow the logical steps.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Begin by reading the Arabic passage and its translation to get the overall flow of the argument.
Pay attention to the key terms: jawāz, murajjiḥ, istiqlāl.
2
Identify the initial premise
The proof starts with the claim that the way to prove the Maker is through actions, since senses cannot perceive Him directly.
Actions are contingent (jāʾiz); they are poised between existence and non-existence.
3
Trace the argument for a single determinant
Follow why contingency leads to a determinant, and why that determinant must be one. The passage spells out the impossibility of two determinants through scenarios.
Think about what happens if two gods both try to determine the same action.
4
Reflect on the conclusion
The argument concludes that only one God can be truly independent; any second would introduce deficiency and need, contradicting divinity.
Notice how this reinforces the earlier method of independence (istiġnā).

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ونقول : من المعلوم أن الطريق إلى إثبات الصانع هو الدليل بالأفعال؛ إذ الحس لا يشهد عليه، والأفعال التي شاهدناها دلت عليه من جهة جوازها وإمكانها في ذاتها، والجواز قضية واحدة تدل على الصانع من حيث إنها ترددت بين الوجود والعدم، فلما ترجح جانب الوجود اضطررنا إلى إثبات مرجح وليس في نفس الجواز، وترجحه ما يدل على مرجحين كل واحد منهما يستقل بالترجيح، فإنه لو لم يستقل بالترجيح لم يكن إلها، وإنما ترجح جانب الوجود لأنه أراد التخصيص بالوجود، وكما أراد علم أنه هو المرجح فلو قدرنا مرجحاً آخر وشاركه في الترجيح بطل الاستقلال، ولم يكن علمه وإرادته وقدرته أيضًا بأن يكون هو المرجح، وإن لم يشاركه في الترجيح وكان متعطلاً لم يكن علمه وإرادته وقدرته أيضا بأن يكون هو المرجح، بل يكون علمه متعلقا بترجيح غيره وإرادته كذلك، وإذا تعلق علمه بأن يكون غيره هو المرجع كان محالا أن يكون هو المرجح، فإن خلاف المعلوم محال الوقوع، وكذلك إرادته تكون تمنيًا وتشهيا من غيره حتى تخصص لا قصدًا وترجيحا وتخصيصا من ذاته، فقد تطرق النقص إلى كل صفة من صفاته، بل كان مفتقرا في جميع ذلك إلى غيره، والفقر ينافي الإلهية، وهذه الطريقة تعضد بيان طريقة الاستغناء وهي أحسن ما ذكر في هذه المسألة.
And we say: It is known that the way to prove the Maker is the proof through actions (al-afʿāl), since the senses do not bear witness to Him. The actions we have observed indicate Him from the aspect of their contingency (jawāz) and possibility in themselves. Contingency is a single judgment that indicates the Maker in that the action is poised between existence and non-existence. When existence becomes preponderant, we are compelled to posit a determinant (murajjiḥ), and this determinant is not within the contingency itself. Its preponderance indicates that there are not two determinants each independently performing the preponderance, for if it were not independent in preponderance, it would not be a god. Existence became preponderant only because He willed specification for existence, and just as He willed, He knew that He is the determinant. So if we suppose another determinant sharing in the preponderance, independence is nullified, and neither His knowledge, will, nor power would be such that He is the determinant. And if He does not share in the preponderance and remains inactive, then His knowledge, will, and power also would not be such that He is the determinant; rather His knowledge would be attached to the preponderance of another, and likewise His will. If His knowledge is attached to another being the determinant, it is impossible that He himself is the determinant, for the contrary of the known is impossible to occur. Similarly His will would become a wish and desire from another, not a purpose, preponderance, and specification from Himself. Thus deficiency affects every one of His attributes; rather He would be in need of another in all that, and need is incompatible with divinity. This method supports the clarification of the method of independence (istiġnā), and it is the best that has been mentioned on this issue.
Source pages p. 91

Commentary
This passage is a proof of the oneness of God based on the contingency of actions (afʿāl). The author states that the way to prove the existence of the Creator is through the actions we observe, since the senses cannot perceive God directly. These actions are contingent (jāʾiz) — they could exist or not exist. When they do exist, we must posit a determinant (murajjiḥ) that tipped the balance towards existence. The contingency itself does not contain this determinant. The preponderance of existence indicates that there cannot be two independent determinants; because if there were two, then either each independently preponderates the action (contradicting independence or leading to competition) or one remains inactive — both scenarios result in deficiency. The author explains: if two gods both will the same action, then neither is independent. If one wills and the other does not, then the one who does not will has his knowledge attached to the other's action, making him unable to act as a determinant — his will becomes a wish for another's action, not his own. Thus his attributes (knowledge, will, power) become deficient and dependent on another, which contradicts divinity. Therefore, there can only be one God. The author notes that this argument supports and reinforces the earlier argument from independence (istiġnā) and is the best one raised on this topic.

Key Terms
دليل بالأفعال
جواز
مرجح
استقلال
استغناء

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Two gods could cooperate in causing existence.
Correction: Cooperation would undermine each god's independence; independence is essential for divinity. The argument shows that any second being would be dependent on the first.

Checkpoint
Why does the proof from actions (afʿāl) show that there cannot be two gods?
A) Because two gods would always conflict.
B) Because the existence of one action requires a single determinant; two determinants would nullify independence.
C) Because actions are too many for one god.
D) Because the senses can only perceive one god.

Key Takeaway
The contingency of any action proves the existence of one independent determinant—none else can share in His act of determination.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
In what area of your life do you still act as if your own will or someone else's approval tips the balance, rather than submitting fully to the One who alone determines everything? What step will you take today to realign that area with tawḥīd?
?
If you truly believed that no other power—wealth, status, relationships—can independently affect your destiny, would you still fear losing them more than you fear displeasing Allah? What would change if you acted on that belief starting now?
?
This proof shows that even the thought of another determinant is a deficiency in one's understanding of God. Examine your heart: is there any hidden reliance on a 'second cause' that you haven't fully surrendered to Allah? What concrete action can you take this week to uproot that reliance?

Proof of the Creator from Contingent Actions
The author argues from contingent actions to a unique Preponderator.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how the contingency of observable actions leads to the affirmation of a unique, independent Preponderator (God).

Core Idea
Contingent actions point to a single, independent Cause.

In this passage, the author presents a foundational kalam argument for the existence and oneness of God. He argues that the actions we witness in the world are contingent—they could have been otherwise. This contingency demands a Preponderator who tips the scale toward existence. Moreover, the very fact of preponderance shows that there can only be one such independent Preponderator. This lesson unpacks that reasoning.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
The Arabic passage is dense. Read it once, then the translation to get the flow.
Notice how the author starts with the Quranic verse hinting at incomparability.
2
Grasp the role of contingency
Understand that contingency (jawaz) means an action could exist or not exist. The fact that it exists requires a cause external to its essence.
This is the core of the argument.
3
Follow the argument for a unique Preponderator
The author argues that the preponderance itself shows there cannot be two independent preponderators. Think about why two independent causes would lead to contradiction.
The logic is that if two beings both had the power to independently preponderate, then the outcome would not be determined—or there would be conflict.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الجوهران فدل ذلك على أن التماثل في الإلهية لن يتصور بوجه ( لَيْسَ كَمِثْلِهِ. م ﴾ [الشورى: ۱۱] إشارة إلى هذا المعنى. ونقول : من المعلوم أن الطريق إلى إثبات الصانع هو الدليل بالأفعال ؛ إذ الحس لا يشهد عليه، والأفعال التي شاهدناها دلت عليه من جهة جوازها وإمكانها في ذاتها، والجواز قضية واحدة تدل على الصانع من حيث إنها ترددت بين الوجود والعدم، فلما ترجح جانب الوجود اضطررنا إلى إثبات مرجح وليس في نفس الجواز، وترجحه ما يدل على مرجحين كل واحد منهما يستقل بالترجيح، فإنه لو لم يستقل بالترجيح لم يكن إلها، وإنما ترجح جانب الوجود لأنه أراد التخصيص بالوجود، وكما أراد علم أنه هو المرجح فلو قدرنا مرجحاً آخر وشاركه في الترجيح بطل الاستقلال، ولم يكن علمه وإرادته وقدرته أيضًا بأن يكون هو المرجح، وإن لم يشاركه في الترجيح وكان متعطلاً لم يكن علمه وإرادته وقدرته أيضا بأن يكون هو المرجح، بل يكون علمه متعلقا بترجيح غيره وإرادته كذلك، وإذا تعلق علمه بأن يكون غيره هو المرجع كان محالا أن يكون هو المرجح، فإن خلاف المعلوم محال الوقوع، وكذلك إرادته تكون تمنيًا وتشهيا من غيره حتى تخصص لا قصدًا وترجيحا وتخصيصا من ذاته، فقد تطرق النقص إلى كل صفة من صفاته، بل كان مفتقرا في جميع ذلك إلى غيره، والفقر ينافي الإلهية، وهذه الطريقة تعضد بيان طريقة الاستغناء وهي أحسن ما ذكر في هذه المسألة.
The two substances indicate that likeness in divinity is inconceivable in any way; the verse 'There is nothing like unto Him' (Quran 42:11) points to this meaning. And we say: It is known that the way to prove the Maker is the proof by actions; since sense perception does not testify to Him, and the actions we have witnessed indicate Him from the aspect of their contingency and possibility in themselves. Contingency is a single proposition that indicates the Maker in that it oscillated between existence and non-existence. When the side of existence preponderated, we are forced to affirm a Preponderator, and it is not in the contingency itself. And its preponderance indicates that there cannot be two preponderators each of whom independently exercises preponderance; for if he did not independently preponderate, he would not be a god. The side of existence preponderated only because He willed specification by existence. And just as He willed, He knew that He is the Preponderator. So if we suppose another preponderator who shares in the preponderance, independence is nullified, and His knowledge, will, and power would also not make Him the Preponderator. And if He does not share in the preponderance but is inactive, then His knowledge, will, and power would also not make Him the Preponderator; rather, His knowledge would be related to the preponderance of another, and His will likewise. And if His knowledge is attached to another being the preponderator, it is impossible that He be the Preponderator, for contradiction of the known is impossible of occurrence. Likewise, His will would be wishful thinking and desire from another, so that it becomes specification not by intention, preponderance, and specification from His essence. Thus deficiency attaches to every attribute of His attributes; rather, He would be dependent in all that on another, and dependence contradicts divinity. This method supports the exposition of the method of self-sufficiency, and it is the best what has been mentioned on this issue.
Source pages p. 91

Commentary
This passage presents an argument for the existence and oneness of God based on the contingency of observable actions. The author begins by noting that the verse 'There is nothing like unto Him' indicates the impossibility of any likeness in divinity. The main argument: the way to prove the Maker is through actions, since He is not directly perceived. These actions are contingent (possible) in themselves. Contingency is a single judgment that points to the Maker because it oscillates between existence and non-existence. When existence preponderates, we must affirm a Preponderator (murajjih). The preponderance cannot be due to the essence of the action itself. Moreover, the fact of preponderance shows that there cannot be two independent preponderators; if each were independent, then there would be a conflict, and the preponderance would not be uniquely determined. If one is not independent, he is not a god. If they share, independence is lost. If one is inactive, his attributes become parasitic. Ultimately, any partner would entail deficiency and dependence, which contradict divinity. The author concludes that this method supports the 'method of self-sufficiency' and is the best approach to the issue.

Key Terms
الصانع
الجواز
المرجح

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: One might think the argument assumes the existence of a preponderator without proof.
Correction: The argument does not assume; it infers the preponderator from the observed fact of preponderance. Contingency itself does not contain the cause; the preponderance forces us to affirm an external preponderator.

Checkpoint
What does the contingency (jawāz) of actions indicate about the Maker?
A) That He is directly perceivable
B) That He is the only possible independent Preponderator
C) That He shares His power with others
D) That He is composed of substances

Key Takeaway
Contingent actions necessarily lead to the affirmation of a single, independent Preponderator.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
You live every moment as a contingent being, yet you often act as if your existence is self-sufficient. In what specific area of your life are you denying the reality that you are entirely dependent on the Preponderator for your next breath? What will you do today to acknowledge His exclusive power over your existence?
?
The argument shows that any preponderator must be independent and unique. Who or what do you sometimes treat as a 'second preponderator'—trusting in your own strength, wealth, or status to tip the balance in your life? How can you repent from this subtle shirk and rely solely on Him?
?
You have now understood that the contingency of your actions demands a single, sovereign Will that gives them existence. Does your daily life reflect that you believe this, or have you been living as if your actions are self-generated? Name one action you will consciously attribute to His will alone from this moment on.

Why Sharing Preponderance Contradicts Divinity
The author argues that any partner in preponderance entails deficiency and dependence, contradicting divinity.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why any partner in the act of preponderance would make God dependent, contradicting His divinity, and how this supports the method of self-sufficiency.

Core Idea
Any partner would make God dependent, which is impossible for divinity.

In the second part of the passage, the author deepens the argument for oneness by examining what would happen if there were another preponderator. He shows that whether this other being shares in preponderance or remains inactive, the first being's attributes—knowledge, will, and power—would become deficient or dependent on another. This leads to the conclusion that dependence contradicts divinity, reinforcing the doctrine of God's self-sufficiency (istighna').

Guided Path
1
Review the uniqueness argument
This lesson builds on the previous one. Ensure you understand that the Preponderator must be unique from the fact of preponderance.
The author now considers hypothetical partners.
2
Analyze the first scenario: sharing
If another being shares in preponderance, independence is lost. Then the first being's knowledge, will, and power would not make Him the Preponderator.
This means His attributes would not be effective.
3
Analyze the second scenario: inactive partner
If another being is inactive, the first being's knowledge and will would be directed towards that other's preponderance, making His will wishful thinking.
Thus deficiency enters each attribute.
4
Grasp the conclusion
In either case, the first being becomes dependent on another, which contradicts divinity. This supports the method of self-sufficiency.
The author declares this the best approach.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الجوهران فدل ذلك على أن التماثل في الإلهية لن يتصور بوجه ( لَيْسَ كَمِثْلِهِ. م ﴾ [الشورى: ۱۱] إشارة إلى هذا المعنى. ونقول : من المعلوم أن الطريق إلى إثبات الصانع هو الدليل بالأفعال ؛ إذ الحس لا يشهد عليه، والأفعال التي شاهدناها دلت عليه من جهة جوازها وإمكانها في ذاتها، والجواز قضية واحدة تدل على الصانع من حيث إنها ترددت بين الوجود والعدم، فلما ترجح جانب الوجود اضطررنا إلى إثبات مرجح وليس في نفس الجواز، وترجحه ما يدل على مرجحين كل واحد منهما يستقل بالترجيح، فإنه لو لم يستقل بالترجيح لم يكن إلها، وإنما ترجح جانب الوجود لأنه أراد التخصيص بالوجود، وكما أراد علم أنه هو المرجح فلو قدرنا مرجحاً آخر وشاركه في الترجيح بطل الاستقلال، ولم يكن علمه وإرادته وقدرته أيضًا بأن يكون هو المرجح، وإن لم يشاركه في الترجيح وكان متعطلاً لم يكن علمه وإرادته وقدرته أيضا بأن يكون هو المرجح، بل يكون علمه متعلقا بترجيح غيره وإرادته كذلك، وإذا تعلق علمه بأن يكون غيره هو المرجع كان محالا أن يكون هو المرجح، فإن خلاف المعلوم محال الوقوع، وكذلك إرادته تكون تمنيًا وتشهيا من غيره حتى تخصص لا قصدًا وترجيحا وتخصيصا من ذاته، فقد تطرق النقص إلى كل صفة من صفاته، بل كان مفتقرا في جميع ذلك إلى غيره، والفقر ينافي الإلهية، وهذه الطريقة تعضد بيان طريقة الاستغناء وهي أحسن ما ذكر في هذه المسألة.
The two substances indicate that likeness in divinity is inconceivable in any way; the verse 'There is nothing like unto Him' (Quran 42:11) points to this meaning. And we say: It is known that the way to prove the Maker is the proof by actions; since sense perception does not testify to Him, and the actions we have witnessed indicate Him from the aspect of their contingency and possibility in themselves. Contingency is a single proposition that indicates the Maker in that it oscillated between existence and non-existence. When the side of existence preponderated, we are forced to affirm a Preponderator, and it is not in the contingency itself. And its preponderance indicates that there cannot be two preponderators each of whom independently exercises preponderance; for if he did not independently preponderate, he would not be a god. The side of existence preponderated only because He willed specification by existence. And just as He willed, He knew that He is the Preponderator. So if we suppose another preponderator who shares in the preponderance, independence is nullified, and His knowledge, will, and power would also not make Him the Preponderator. And if He does not share in the preponderance but is inactive, then His knowledge, will, and power would also not make Him the Preponderator; rather, His knowledge would be related to the preponderance of another, and His will likewise. And if His knowledge is attached to another being the preponderator, it is impossible that He be the Preponderator, for contradiction of the known is impossible of occurrence. Likewise, His will would be wishful thinking and desire from another, so that it becomes specification not by intention, preponderance, and specification from His essence. Thus deficiency attaches to every attribute of His attributes; rather, He would be dependent in all that on another, and dependence contradicts divinity. This method supports the exposition of the method of self-sufficiency, and it is the best what has been mentioned on this issue.
Source pages p. 91

Commentary
This passage presents an argument for the existence and oneness of God based on the contingency of observable actions. The author begins by noting that the verse 'There is nothing like unto Him' indicates the impossibility of any likeness in divinity. The main argument: the way to prove the Maker is through actions, since He is not directly perceived. These actions are contingent (possible) in themselves. Contingency is a single judgment that points to the Maker because it oscillates between existence and non-existence. When existence preponderates, we must affirm a Preponderator (murajjih). The preponderance cannot be due to the essence of the action itself. Moreover, the fact of preponderance shows that there cannot be two independent preponderators; if each were independent, then there would be a conflict, and the preponderance would not be uniquely determined. If one is not independent, he is not a god. If they share, independence is lost. If one is inactive, his attributes become parasitic. Ultimately, any partner would entail deficiency and dependence, which contradict divinity. The author concludes that this method supports the 'method of self-sufficiency' and is the best approach to the issue.

الإلهية
الاستغناء

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some might think that if another being also has power, they could cooperate without conflict.
Correction: The author shows that any sharing of the specific act of preponderance—the independent determination of existence—undermines the independence of each. Even if the second is inactive, the first's attributes become deficient.

Checkpoint

A) It establishes cooperation and confirms power
B) Independence is nullified, and the first's attributes do not make Him the preponderator

D) It creates a harmonious partnership

Key Takeaway
Any partner in the act of preponderance makes God dependent on another, which is impossible; thus, God must be absolutely self-sufficient.

?

What would it mean to live as if God alone is the Preponderator, independent of all? Make a specific commitment to surrender one such area to Him this week.
?
The passage says dependence contradicts divinity. In what subtle ways do you try to make God dependent on your actions—e.g., by thinking your worship 'earns' His favor as if He needs it? How can you realign your heart to worship Him as the utterly independent One, not out of need but out of love?

Refutation of the Proof of Mutual Hindrance
Refutes the proof of tamānuʿ by showing difference in will is inconceivable.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why the proof of mutual hindrance (tamānuʿ) is logically invalid because will must correspond to knowledge, making difference in will inconceivable.

Core Idea
If knowledge is fixed, will must match — so difference in will is impossible.

In this passage, the author rebuts a classic argument for the existence of one God known as the proof of mutual hindrance. The proof assumes that two gods would differ in will, but the author shows that difference in will is inconceivable because will follows knowledge. Since we cannot conceive of knowledge differing from its object, we cannot conceive of will differing either. Thus the proof collapses.

Guided Path
1
Read the Passage
Read the Arabic passage and its translation carefully. Notice how the author presents a conditional argument: if the known is motion, then the willed must be motion. Reflect on the logical connection.
2
Trace the Argument
Follow the author's reasoning: knowledge cannot differ from its object, so will also cannot differ. Understand that the proof of tamānuʿ requires difference, which is impossible.
3
Connect to the Commentary
Read the commentary to see how the refutation works. Then test your understanding with the checkpoint.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
كان المعلوم هو الحركة، فمن ضرورته أن يكون المراد هو الحركة، وتقدير الاختلاف في العلم غير متصور، فتقدير الاختلاف في الإرادة أيضا غير متصور، ومبنى دلالة التمانع على تحقيق الاختلاف أو تقديره وذلك غير جائز؛ فبطل التمسك بها.
If the known is the motion, then of necessity the willed is the motion. And imagining difference in knowledge is inconceivable, so imagining difference in will is also inconceivable. The basis of the proof of mutual hindrance (tamānuʿ) is to affirm difference or to imagine it, and that is not permissible; therefore, adhering to it is invalid.
Source pages p. 92

Commentary
This passage is a refutation of the proof of mutual hindrance (dalīl al-tamānuʿ) for the existence of one God. The author points out that if we know one motion, then the will must correspond to that knowledge. Since we cannot conceive of knowledge differing from its object, we also cannot conceive of will differing. The proof of tamānuʿ relies on either actual difference or imagined difference between two deities, but since difference in will is not conceivable (because will follows knowledge), the argument fails. Therefore, the proof is invalid.

Key Terms
المعلوم
المراد
الاختلاف
التمانع
التمسك

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The proof of mutual hindrance is a conclusive argument for the existence of one God.
Correction: The proof is invalid because it relies on the possibility of difference in will between two gods, but difference in will is inconceivable since will always follows knowledge. Therefore, the proof fails.

Checkpoint
Why does the author consider the proof of mutual hindrance invalid?
A) Because the willed must match the known, and difference in will is inconceivable.
B) Because the proof is based on a false assumption.
C) Because the proof requires actual difference which is impossible.
D) Because the proof is rejected by consensus.

Key Takeaway
The proof of mutual hindrance fails because difference in will between deities is inconceivable, given that will must correspond to knowledge.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
If you claim to know God's will, how certain are you that your will aligns with His? This lesson shows that will follows knowledge. Are you truly seeking knowledge of His commands to ensure your will is in harmony? What one area of your life do you need to urgently align your will with what you know to be true?
?
The proof of tamānuʿ fails because difference in will is inconceivable. But in your own life, do you ever find your will contradicting what you know? If so, does that not show a disconnect between your knowledge and your will? How will you bridge that gap starting today?
?
The author says that if knowledge is fixed, will must match. Do you live as if your knowledge of Allah is fixed and decisive? Or do you treat His commands as optional? What would change if you truly believed your will must follow your knowledge without exception?

Defining Divine Agency
Defines divine agency and rejects necessary causation.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how the author defines God's creative agency as an act of knowledge, will, and power, and why he avoids the term 'necessary' to prevent implying coercive causation.

Core Idea
God's agency is free and perfect, not coerced necessity.

Before tackling the possibility of two gods, the author clarifies what divine agency truly means. He insists that God's action flows from His knowledge, will, and power without change in His essence. This careful definition sets the stage for the subsequent argument against multiple deities.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage on divine agency
Read the first part of the source passage (up to '...so that necessary causation does not follow'). Notice how the author describes God's creative act as based on His knowledge, will, and power without altering His attributes.
2
Identify the key terms
Find the terms 'فاعلية' (agency) and 'الإيجاب بالذات' (necessary causation by essence). Understand that the author deliberately avoids saying God's existence is 'necessary' because that could imply His action is forced.
The author is sensitive to the nuance of language in theology.
3
Reflect on the reason
Ask yourself: why does the author reject the label 'necessary'? The commentary explains that it would imply coercive causation, which contradicts God's perfection. Free will and power are central.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ولهذا قلنا: إن معنى فاعلية الباري سبحانه وتعالى أنه لم علم وجود شيء في وقت مخصوص أو تقدير وقت إراده على مقتضى علمه، وأوجده بقدرته على مقتضى إرادته من غير أن تتغير ذاته أو صفة من صفاته وعلمه وإرادته وقدرته فيشبه أن يقال: لزم كونه ضرورة ولكنا لا نطلق هذا اللفظ ملاحظة منا جانب الإرادة والقدرة حتى لا يلزمنا الإيجاب بالذات وذلك ينافي الكمال، فنحن إنما عرفنا الجواز في الجايزات بقضية عقلية ضرورية، وعرفنا استناد وجود الموجودات إلى عالم قادر مريد لضرورة الاحتياج في الجايزات، فإن قدرناهما على كمال الاقتدار والاستبداد بالفعل والإبداع والتساوي في صفات الذات وصفات الفعل حتى يكون كل واحد منهما هو الموجد بقدرته وإرادته وعلمه؛ وقع التمانع في الفعل. وإن قدرناهما على تسليم أحدهما للثاني في جميع ما اشتغل به كان المسلّم عبدًا والمسلّم إليه إلها حقا، وإن كان كل واحد منهما مستقلا في بعض مسلّما في بعض كان كل واحد منهما محتاجًا من وجه وغنيا من وجه قاصراً من حيث الفعل، كاملا من حيث القوة، فلا يكون إلهين بل محتاجين إلى كامل من كل وجه.
Because of this we said: The meaning of the agency (fāʿiliyya) of the Creator, exalted and high, is that He knew the existence of something at a specific time or estimated a time of will according to His knowledge, and brought it into existence by His power according to His will, without His essence or any of His attributes, knowledge, will, and power changing. So it might be said: His existence is necessary, but we do not utter this expression due to our consideration of will and power so that necessary causation (ījāb bi-al-dhāt) does not follow, for that contradicts perfection. We only know possibility (jawāz) in possible things by a necessary rational judgment, and we know that the existence of existing things depends on a knowing, powerful, willing being due to the necessity of need in possible things. If we imagine both of them to possess complete power (istikmāl al-iqtidār) and independence in action and creation and equality in attributes of essence and attributes of action such that each one is the bringer into existence by his power, will, and knowledge, then mutual hindrance (tamānuʿ) occurs in action. If we imagine them as one submitting to the other in all that he undertakes, then the submitter is a servant and the one submitted to is truly God. If each is independent in some matters and submitting in others, then each is in need in one respect and independent in another, deficient in action, perfect in potential, so they are not two gods but rather two beings in need of a perfect being from every aspect.
Source pages p. 92

Commentary
This passage defines the agency of God properly: He creates by knowledge, will, and power without change. The author avoids the term 'necessary' to prevent implying coercive causation (ījāb bi-al-dhāt), which would contradict perfection. He then considers the case of two deities. If both are fully independent and equal, mutual hindrance results. If one submits to the other, the submitter is not divine. If they share independence, they are imperfect and need a perfect being. Therefore, two gods cannot exist.

Key Terms
فاعلية
الإيجاب بالذات

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: One might think that saying 'God's existence is necessary' is harmless or even correct.
Correction: The author shows that labeling God's existence as 'necessary' risks implying that His actions are coerced by His essence, which would contradict His free will and perfection.

Checkpoint
Why does the author avoid saying God's existence is necessary?
A) Because it contradicts the Quran.
B) Because it implies coercive causation which is not perfect.
C) Because it is not rational.
D) Because the attributes are separate.

Key Takeaway
God's agency is defined by knowledge, will, and power, and must not be described as 'necessary' to avoid implying coercive causation.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Do you ever act as if God's actions are mechanical or forced, as though He 'had to' create or decree certain things? How does the belief in His absolute free will affect your trust in His wisdom and mercy? This week, when something difficult happens, pause and remind yourself: 'God willed this freely, not out of necessity.'
?
If God's agency is free and not compelled, what does that mean for your own sense of accountability? Do you secretly excuse your sins by thinking 'God made me do it' or 'it was fated'? Confront that thought with the truth that God's will is free and your choices are real. How will you take full responsibility for your actions starting today?
?
The author avoids the term 'necessary' to preserve divine freedom. When you describe God, do you ever use language that subtly constrains Him (e.g., 'God had to send prophets,' 'God must forgive the repentant')? Rephrase those statements to reflect His perfect freedom. Write one specific way you will change your speech about God this week.

The Impossibility of Two Independent Gods
Argues against two independent gods through three scenarios.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the rational argument against the existence of two independent gods, based on mutual hindrance or the need for a superior being.

Core Idea
Two independent gods would either conflict or be imperfect.

Having established the correct understanding of divine agency, the author now considers the possibility of two gods. He systematically examines three scenarios, showing that each leads to a contradiction or to one god being subordinate. This argument, known as the 'mutual hindrance' (tamānuʿ) argument, is a classic proof of tawḥīd.

Guided Path
1
Read the second part of the passage
Read from 'فإن قدرناهما على كمال الاقتدار' to the end. The author lays out three possibilities for two gods: both fully independent, one submitting to the other, or each independent in some matters.
2
Analyze each scenario
For each possibility, consider the outcome: mutual hindrance, one being a servant, or both being deficient. Identify why each scenario proves that two independent gods cannot exist.
3
Connect to the conclusion
The author concludes that the only coherent possibility is one God who is perfect in every aspect. Summarize the logical flow: if there were two, either they conflict, or one is subordinate, or both are needy—thus impossible.

Rational Impossibility of Two Equal and Independent Beings
Demonstrates the rational impossibility of two fully equal and independent beings.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the rational argument that prevents the existence of two beings with complete independence and equality.

Core Idea
No two beings can be identical in every way—distinction is inevitable.

In this passage, the author concludes the proof of divine oneness by showing that any supposed pair of independent beings would inevitably be distinguished by some factor. This rational impossibility secures the necessity of a single Creator.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without analyzing it. Then read the translation. Let the author's argument unfold naturally.
Notice the strong opening: 'فإذا لا يتصور في العقل' – 'Thus, it is not conceivable in the mind.'
2
Identify the key claim
The author asserts that it is rationally inconceivable to have two beings with complete independence, power, will, and knowledge. Find this claim.
3
List the possible distinctions
The passage lists several ways two beings could be distinguished: by indication, place, time, action, or effect. Identify each one.
These distinctions prevent any two beings from being truly equal in all respects.
4
Understand the conclusion
The author concludes that the act (creation) points to a single agent (al-wāḥid). Reflect on why this follows from the impossibility of two equals.
The phrase 'مدلول الفعل' – 'the one indicated by the act' – is a key term.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فإذا لا يتصور في العقل تقدير موجودين على كمال الاستقلال، ولا في التجويز العقلي تقدير قادرين على التساوي في كمال الاقتدار، ولا مريدين ولا عالمين على التماثل في كمال الإرادة والعلم، بل ولا ذاتين متساويين من جميع الوجوه من غير أن يختص إحداهما عن الثانية؛ إما بإشارة إلى هذا أو ذلك، أو محل أو مكان محيز وزمان مقدم ومؤخر، أو بفعل خاص، أو أثر يدل على تغاير المصدر وتباين المظهر؛ ولهذا قلنا: إن الواحد مدلول الفعل، ولو قدرنا ثانيًا وثالثًا لتكافئوا من حيث العدد.
Thus, it is not conceivable in the mind to imagine two beings possessing complete independence, nor in rational consideration to imagine two powers equal in complete power, nor two willers nor two knowers similar in complete will and knowledge, nor even two essences equal in all respects without one being distinguished from the other; either by an indication to this or that, or by a place or a circumscribed location, or by a time earlier or later, or by a specific action, or by an effect indicating the difference of the source and the distinction of the manifestation. Because of this we said: The one (al-wāḥid) is indicated by the act. And if we imagined a second and a third, they would be equivalent in number.
Source pages p. 93

Commentary
This passage concludes the argument for divine oneness. The author asserts that it is rationally inconceivable to have two beings with full independence, power, will, and knowledge without distinction. Even if they were equal in all attributes, they would still be distinguished by something (e.g., spatial location, temporal priority, specific action, or effect). Therefore, the act points to a single agent. If there were multiple, they would be mere numerical equivalents, but not truly independent and equal in all respects. Hence, oneness is necessary.

Key Terms
الاستقلال
الاقتدار
التماثل
التغاير
مدلول الفعل

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Two gods could be identical in every way.
Correction: The passage shows that any two beings would necessarily be distinguished by something (e.g., place, time, action), so true equality is impossible.

Checkpoint
According to the passage, what distinguishes two supposedly equal essences?
A) Their names.
B) An indication, place, time, action, or effect.
C) Their knowledge.
D) Their will.

Key Takeaway
The act of creation points to a single divine agent; multiple independent beings are rationally impossible.

This proof shows that the act of creation points to a single source. Look at your daily actions—your speech, your choices, your worship. Do they point to one ultimate motive (pleasing Allah alone), or are they scattered among multiple goals? What would it take to unify your life around that one indication?

The Tamānuʿ Argument Against Two Gods
Shahrastānī presents the three-case tamānuʿ argument against polytheism.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the tamānuʿ argument against the possibility of two gods and why Islamic theologians consider it a decisive proof for God's oneness.

Core Idea
Two fully independent gods cannot coexist—they would either conflict or one would be subordinate, disproving their godhood.

This lesson presents the core of the tamānuʿ (mutual prevention) argument from Shahrastānī's Nihāyat al-Aqdām. The argument systematically examines what would happen if there were two gods, each with full power and independence. As you'll see, the only logical outcomes are conflict, subordination, or deficiency—each of which contradicts the very definition of God. This passage is a classic expression of the Islamic rational proof for tawḥīd.

Guided Path
1
Read the argument attentively
Read the Arabic and translation once to get an overall sense of the argument. Notice that it moves from general principles to specific cases.
2
Understand the preamble
The author begins by clarifying how we understand God's necessity and the contingency of creation. This sets the stage for the argument.
Pay attention to the phrase 'لزم كونه ضرورة' and the caution about using 'necessary'.
3
Analyze the three scenarios
Identify the three hypothetical situations: both gods fully independent, one submitting, and mixed independence. For each, note the logical consequence.
Use the guided breakdown below to keep track.
4
Grasp the rational impossibility
After the scenarios, the author generalizes: it is rationally impossible to have two identical, fully independent essences without any distinguishing feature.
This is the core of the tamānuʿ argument.
5
Connect to the conclusion
The argument concludes that the unity of the act (creation) indicates a single agent, and any additional 'gods' would be equal in number but not truly divine.
Reflect on why the tamānuʿ argument is considered a definitive proof for tawḥīd.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
أن يقال: لزم كونه ضرورة ولكنا لا نطلق هذا اللفظ ملاحظة منا جانب الإرادة والقدرة حتى لا يلزمنا الإيجاب بالذات وذلك ينافي الكمال، فنحن إنما عرفنا الجواز في الجائزات بقضية عقلية ضرورية، وعرفنا استناد وجود الموجودات إلى عالم قادر مريد لضرورة الاحتياج في الجائزات، فإن قدرناهما على كمال الاقتدار والاستبداد بالفعل والإبداع والتساوي في صفات الذات وصفات الفعل حتى يكون كل واحد منهما هو الموجد بقدرته وإرادته وعلمه؛ وقع التمانع في الفعل. وإن قدرناهما على تسليم أحدهما للثاني في جميع ما اشتغل به كان المسلّم عبداً والمسلّم إليه إلهاً حقاً، وإن كان كل واحد منهما مستقلاً في بعض مسلّماً في بعض كان كل واحد منهما محتاجاً من وجه وغنيّاً من وجه قاصراً من حيث الفعل، كاملاً من حيث القوة، فلا يكون إلهين بل محتاجين إلى كامل من كل وجه، فإذا لا يتصور في العقل تقدير موجودين على كمال الاستقلال، ولا في التجويز العقلي تقدير قادرين على التساوي في كمال الاقتدار، ولا مريدين ولا عالمين على التماثل في كمال الإرادة والعلم، بل ولا ذاتين متساويين من جميع الوجوه من غير أن يختص إحداهما عن الثانية؛ إما بإشارة إلى هذا أو ذلك، أو محل أو مكان محيز وزمان مقدم ومؤخر، أو بفعل خاص، أو أثر يدل على تغاير المصدر وتباين المظهر؛ ولهذا قلنا: إن الواحد مدلول الفعل، ولو قدرنا ثانياً وثالثاً لتكافئوا من حيث العدد.
It may be said: His existence is necessary by necessity, but we do not utter this expression out of consideration for will and power, so that necessitation by essence does not follow from us, and that contradicts perfection. We only know contingency in contingent things by a necessary rational judgment, and we know the dependence of the existence of existents on a knowing, powerful, willing [agent] due to the necessity of need in contingent things. If we suppose them both to be at the perfection of power and independence in action and creation and equality in attributes of essence and attributes of action, such that each one of them is the bringer-into-existence by His power, will, and knowledge, then mutual prevention (tamānuʿ) occurs in action. And if we suppose them both to be such that one submits to the other in all that he occupies himself with, then the submitter is a servant and the one submitted to is truly a god. And if each one of them is independent in some matters and submitting in some, then each one of them is needy in one respect and independent in another, deficient in terms of action, perfect in terms of potentiality; so they would not be two gods but rather two needy ones in need of one perfect from every aspect. Therefore it is not conceivable in the intellect to suppose two existents upon complete independence, nor in rational speculation to suppose two powerful ones upon equality in perfection of power, nor two willing ones nor two knowing ones upon similarity in perfection of will and knowledge, nor even two essences equal from all aspects without one being distinguished from the other—either by an indication to this one or that, or by a location or a containing place, a prior and posterior time, or by a specific act, or an effect indicating the difference of the source and the distinctness of the manifestation. And for this we said: The one is the indication of the act, and if we supposed a second and a third, they would be equal in terms of number.
Source pages p. 93

Commentary
This passage is a sustained argument against the possibility of two gods, known as the tamānuʿ (mutual prevention) argument. The author begins by noting that while we might say God's existence is necessary, we avoid that wording to preserve the notions of will and power, lest we fall into necessitarianism. Instead, we know contingency in contingent things by a necessary rational judgment, and we know that all contingent existents depend on a knowing, powerful, willing creator. The argument then examines three scenarios for two supposed gods: (1) If both are equally powerful and independent in every act, they would conflict (tamānuʿ) when willing opposite things. (2) If one completely submits to the other, the submitter is a servant, not a god. (3) If each is independent in some acts and submits in others, each is partly needy and partly independent, deficient in action though perfect in potential—they would need a perfect being beyond them. Therefore, it is rationally impossible to conceive two existents with complete independence, equality in power, will, and knowledge, or even two essences equal in every respect without some distinguishing feature (e.g., indication, place, time, act, or effect). Hence, the author concludes, the act indicates one agent, and any additional supposed gods would be equal in number but cannot coexist as truly divine.

Key Terms
تمانع (tamānuʿ)
إله (ilāh)
قادر (qādir)
مريد (murīd)
عالم (ʿālim)
وجوب (wujūb)
جواز (jawāz)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some think the tamānuʿ argument only applies to physical conflict between two gods, like them fighting over a single object.
Correction: The argument also covers logical incompatibility: two fully independent essences cannot exist because they would lack any distinguishing feature, leading to a contradiction in the very concept of divinity.

Checkpoint
According to the tamānuʿ argument, what happens if two gods are both fully independent and equal in power?
A) They cooperate perfectly.
B) They would cause mutual prevention (tamānuʿ) in action.
C) One would submit to the other.
D) They would both be necessary beings.

Key Takeaway
The tamānuʿ argument shows that the very concept of two fully independent gods is logically self-contradictory, affirming the rational necessity of God's oneness.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
The argument shows that any being that needs another in any aspect cannot be God. Is there any person, possession, or plan you rely on as if it were independent of Allah? What would it take for you to uproot that reliance today?
?
If you truly believe that only Allah is the independent creator and planner, why do you still worry about outcomes as if other forces have a say? To what extent does your daily anxiety reveal a hidden shirk in your heart?
?
The second scenario describes a being that submits to another in all matters—that being is called a 'servant', not a god. Are you fully submitted to Allah in your life, or are you holding back some area as your own domain? What is one concrete decision you can make tonight to surrender that area completely?

The First Way: Act Indicates One Creator
Shahrastani gives the first of two proofs refuting a second god via the act.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how Shahrastani uses the act of creation to argue that a second god cannot exist without causing deficiency.

Core Idea
A second god would have to create a separate world, proving deficiency — so only one Creator is possible.

This passage presents the first of two proofs in the tamānuʿ argument. Shahrastani responds to those who mock this method by showing that the very act of creation (the world) indicates a single, powerful, willing Creator. He then examines what would be required if a second god were to exist, revealing an inherent contradiction.

Guided Path
1
Read the Arabic and translation together
Read the Arabic passage once without analysing. Then read the translation. Let the author's own rhythm come through before you interpret.
Notice the concise argumentative style.
2
Identify the two possibilities for a second god
The passage presents two options for a supposed second god: either he has a specific proof for his existence, or he does not. Find these two branches.
3
Analyze the first possibility: creating another world
If the second god has a proof, it would be by creating a different world. But this leads to deficiency in both gods. Understand why that is.
This is a key step—reflect on what 'deficiency' means here.
4
Examine the second possibility: rational permissibility without proof
If the second god has no proof, it is rationally permissible to posit him, but then he must have divine attributes and create to be similar to the first. If he does not create, he is not a god.
Similarity (mathal) is crucial—true divinity demands equality in attributes and acts.
5
Conclude the impossibility of a second god
Both possibilities lead to contradiction or deficiency, so a second god cannot exist. The act itself points to one unique Creator.
This is the conclusion of the first way.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ولقد استهزأ بهذه الطريقة من لم يدرك غورها وأمكن تقريرها من وجهين: أحدهما: أن الفعل قد دل على وجود صانع للعالم مريد قادر، فإن قدر ثان فلا يخلو؛ إما أن يكون له دليل خاص على وجوده أو يجوز عقلاً بأن يقال: كما لا دليل على وجوده لا دليل على انتفائه، فإن له دليلاً بأن يخلق عالماً آخر غير العالم المعين، فيؤدي إلى قصور في الإلهين جميعاً - كما بينا - وإن جوز ذلك فيجب أن يكون عالماً بأن يخلق مريداً لأن يخلق قادراً على أن يخلق، وإذا لم يخلق دل على أنه لم يرد، أو إذا لم يرد علم أن لا يخلق، والإله يجب علمه بأن يخلق ويريد بأن يخلق حتى يكون مثلاً للأول، فإنما ليس بمثل له ليس بإله.
And indeed, one who did not grasp its depth mocked this method, yet it is possible to establish it in two ways: The first: The act indicates the existence of a maker of the world who is willing and powerful. If a second is supposed, it is not free from [one of two possibilities]: either he has a specific proof for his existence, or it is rationally permissible to say: just as there is no proof for his existence, there is no proof for his non-existence. If he has a proof, it would be by his creating another world other than the specified world, and that leads to deficiency in both gods—as we have shown. And if that is permitted, then he must be knowing that he creates, willing to create, powerful to create; and if he does not create, that indicates that he did not will, or if he did not will, it is known that he does not create. And a god must have knowledge that he creates and will that he creates, so that he be similar to the first; for that which is not similar to him is not a god.
Source pages p. 94

Commentary
This passage presents the first of two ways to establish the tamānuʿ argument, responding to those who mock it. The author states that the act (the world) indicates one Creator who is willing and powerful. If someone supposes a second god, there are two possibilities: (1) There is a specific proof for his existence, such as creating another world distinct from this one. But that would cause deficiency in both gods, as previously explained (they would conflict or be limited). (2) There is no proof for his existence, but it is rationally permissible to posit him because absence of proof is not proof of absence. However, if that second god is truly a god, he would have the attributes of knowledge, will, and power; he would have to create something to demonstrate his divinity. If he does not create, that shows he either did not will or lacked knowledge. But a true god must know and will to create in order to be similar (mathal) to the first god. If he is not similar, then he is not a god. Thus, the second god cannot exist as a truly divine being without creating a separate world, which leads to deficiency. Therefore, the first way shows the impossibility of a second god based on the act (the world) itself.

Key Terms
فعل
صانع
دليل
مثل
عالِم
مريد
قادر

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Since there is no evidence for a second god, he definitely does not exist.
Correction: The argument acknowledges rational permissibility of a second god absent proof, but then shows that such a being would still be impossible due to the requirement of similarity and action.

Checkpoint
According to the first way, what would happen if a second god had a specific proof for his existence?
A) He would be equal to the first god.
B) He would create another world, causing deficiency in both.
C) He would submit to the first god.
D) He would not need to create anything.

Key Takeaway
The act of creation uniquely points to one God; any supposed second god would either prove deficiency by creating another world or fail to demonstrate divinity by not creating.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
If the act of creation points to one God, what does your own existence as a created being demand from you? Are you living in a way that acknowledges the sole Creator, or do you act as if multiple 'creators' shape your life—such as your desires, other people's opinions, or your own plans? What would change if you truly lived as if only one God deserves your ultimate loyalty, starting today?
?
The argument says a supposed second god must act (create) to prove divinity. Do you sometimes try to 'prove' your worth through multiple sources—achievements, possessions, relationships—instead of grounding your identity solely in your Creator? How does relying on these 'secondary creators' distract you from the oneness of God, and what is one concrete step you can take this week to redirect your focus?
?
Shahrastani states that a being that does not act in a way that reflects divine attributes is not similar to God. Look at your own actions: in what areas of your life are you failing to reflect God's attributes of knowledge (seeking truth), will (intentional obedience), and power (using your abilities for His sake)? What would it look like for you to become more 'similar' to your Creator in your daily conduct, not in essence but in following His guidance?

The Second Way: Limitation of Number Proves Oneness
Shahrastānī argues against multiple gods by examining the implication of a finite number.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how Shahrastānī uses the limitation of number to argue that the number of gods must be one, not many.

Core Idea
A finite number of gods would require a cause; an infinite number is impossible.

Shahrastānī presents a second logical route to establish the tamānuʿ argument. This time he focuses on the very concept of number: any specific finite number of multiple gods would need a reason for being exactly that number, which leads to an infinite regress unless there is a single necessary being. Read on to see this crisp argument.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage and its translation. Notice that it sets up a dichotomy: either the number of gods is finite or not finite.
The passage breaks off at the end; the second branch (infinite) is implied.
2
Identify the two possible cases
The text says: 'the matter is not free from either stopping at a known number (finite) or not stopping (infinite).' For the finite case, it requires a limiter (ḥāṣir). Find that argument and the analogy with area.
3
Reflect on the analogy
Shahrastānī compares quantity in number to quantity in area. Just as a specific size requires a cause, a specific number of gods requires a cause. Ask yourself: why is this analogy powerful?
This shows that any specific number of gods would be contingent, needing a determiner.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
والوجه الثاني في تقرير التكافؤ: أن الأمر لا يخلو إما أن يقف في عدد معلوم فيستدعي الاقتصار على عدد محصور مقتضياً حاصر، فإن الكمية من حيث العدد كالكمية من حيث المساحة، أليس لو كان واحداً ذا حجم وعظم مشكل اقتضى مشكلاً لذلك إذا كان ذا مقدار وعدد اقتضى مقدراً، وإن لم يقف في
And the second way in establishing the mutual equality: the matter is not free from either stopping at a known number, necessitating restriction to a limited number that requires a limiter; for quantity in terms of number is like quantity in terms of area. Is it not that if one were a problematic size and magnitude, it would require a problematic [cause] for that? If it has measure and number, it requires a measurer. And if it does not stop at [a finite number]...
Source pages p. 94

Commentary
This passage presents the second way to establish the tamānuʿ argument, focusing on the number of gods. The author states that the matter (i.e., the number of gods) is either finite (stops at a known number) or infinite. If finite, it would require a reason for that specific number, which would necessitate a limiter (ḥāṣir) that determines exactly that number. The author draws an analogy: quantity in terms of number is like quantity in terms of area (masāḥa). Just as a body with a specific size and shape requires a cause that gives it that shape, so a specific number of gods would require a cause that determines that number. If the number is not finite, it would go on infinitely, which is also problematic. The passage is incomplete, but the argument is that positing multiple gods leads to an impossible regress or arbitrary limitation. Thus, only one god can be rationally maintained.

Key Terms
تكافؤ (takāfuʾ)
عدد (number)
كمية (quantity)
مساحة (area)
حاصر (limiter)
مقدر (measurer)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some think the second way is about numerical parity (i.e., all gods are equal).
Correction: It is actually about the impossibility of determining a finite number of gods without a cause; the focus is on the number itself, not equality.

Checkpoint
According to the second way, what does a finite number of gods imply?
A) That number is arbitrary and needs no cause.
B) That number requires a limiter (ḥāṣir) to determine it.
C) That the gods are equal in power.
D) That the number is necessarily infinite.

Key Takeaway
The second way shows that a finite number of gods would require a cause, which is impossible because the First Cause must be one.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you consider the sheer impossibility of a second necessary being, does your heart truly rest in the oneness of Allah, or do you still, in moments of anxiety, act as if there are other sources of ultimate control? What specific fear can you entrust only to Him today?
?
The argument shows that a finite number of gods would need a cause. If you are not fully convinced of tawhid, what 'cause' in your life is preventing you from submitting to the one God? Will you remove that idol today?
?
Shahrastānī uses a logical analogy: number requires a determiner. In your own life, what 'numbers' or measures (e.g., wealth, status, followers) do you treat as if they are self-sufficient, forgetting that the only true Determiner is Allah? Commit to one action that redirects your reliance from created measures to the Creator.

The First Proof: Action Indicates a Single Creator
The author presents the first aspect of the argument from action for one Creator.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the rational proof for the oneness of God based on the nature of action, and why a second creator would be deficient.

Core Idea
A second creator would be deficient, so only one God exists.

This lesson explores the first of two proofs for God's oneness, known as the argument from action (dalīl al-fi‘l). The author argues that the orderly universe points to a single Maker, and any supposed second creator would necessarily be deficient. The passage is dense but reveals a powerful logical reasoning.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once, then the English translation. Try to grasp the overall argument.
The passage is a single, continuous proof.
2
Identify the main claim
Notice that the passage begins by stating that action indicates a Maker who is willing and powerful.
3
Analyze the two possibilities for a second creator
The author says if we suppose a second creator, it either has a specific proof or not. Understand each branch.
This is a logical dichotomy.
4
Understand why the second creator is deficient
If it has a proof, it must create its own world, leading to deficiency in both; if no proof, it's baseless.
5
Reflect on the conclusion
The passage ends by stating that any being not like the first is not a god. Consider the implication for monotheism.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ولقد استهزأ بهذه الطريقة من لم يدرك غورها وأمكن تقريرها من وجهين: أحدهما: أن الفعل قد دل على وجود صانع للعالم مريد قادر، فإن قدر ثان فلا يخلو؛ إما أن يكون له دليل خاص على وجوده أو يجوز عقلا بأن يقال: كما لا دليل على وجوده لا دليل على انتفائه، فإن له دليلا بأن يخلق عالما آخر العالم المعين، فيؤدي إلى قصور في الإلهين جميعا - كما بينا - وإن جوز ذلك فيجب أن يكون عالما بأن يخلق مريدا لأن يخلق قادرًا على أن يخلق، وإذا لم يخلق دل على أنه لم يرد، أو إذا لم يرد علم أن لا يخلق، والإله يجب علمه بأن يخلق ويريد بأن يخلق حتى يكون مثلاً للأول، فإنما ليس بمثل له ليس بإله.
Many have mocked this method who have not grasped its depth, and it can be established from two aspects: First: The action has indicated the existence of a Maker of the world who is willing and powerful. If a second is supposed, then it is not free: either it has a specific proof for its existence, or it is rationally permissible to say: just as there is no proof for its existence, there is no proof for its non-existence. Then if it has a proof by creating another world besides the particular world, that leads to deficiency in both gods—as we have shown. And if that is allowed, then it must be that it is knowing to create, willing to create, powerful to create. And if it does not create, that indicates that it did not will, or if it did not will, it is known that it does not create. And the god must have knowledge that it creates and will to create in order to be a like of the first. For what is not like it is not a god.
Source pages p. 94

Commentary
This passage presents the first of two proofs for the oneness of God, known as the argument from action (dalīl al-fi‘l). The author begins by noting that some have mocked this method due to their lack of understanding, but he proceeds to establish it in two ways. The first way is as follows: The existence of the world—an orderly, contingent action—demonstrates that it has a Maker (ṣāni‘) who is willing (murīd) and powerful (qādir). If one supposes a second maker, that second would either have its own specific proof (dalīl khāṣṣ) for existence or not. If it has no proof, it is merely an arbitrary assumption. If it has a proof, it would have to create a distinct universe. But then both gods would be deficient: each would lack the other's creation, indicating a limitation. Moreover, for the second to be a genuine god, it must possess knowledge, will, and power to create. If it does not create, it shows it lacks will. A god must have the attributes of knowledge and will to create to be comparable to the first; anything less is not a god. Thus, only one god can exist.

Key Terms
دليل الفعل (action proof)
صانع (Maker)
مريد (willing)
قادر (powerful)
قصور (deficiency)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: One might think that the argument only proves a creator, not necessarily a single one.
Correction: The proof shows that any second creator would be deficient and thus not a god, so oneness is necessary.

Checkpoint
What does the first proof conclude about a supposed second creator?
A) It would be equal to the first.
B) It would be deficient and thus not a god.
C) It would require its own separate universe and be independent.
D) It would be possible if it had a specific proof.

Key Takeaway
The existence of a single Maker is proven by the deficiency that would arise from supposing a second.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Have you ever felt that something in your life—a person, a possession, or a goal—holds equal sway over you as Allah? How does the proof that a second would be deficient challenge you to reconsider what you truly rely on? Will you abandon that false reliance today?
?
The passage states that a true god must will, know, and be able to create. When you face a situation beyond your control, do you turn to Allah as the sole possessor of these attributes, or do you subconsciously expect help from other sources? What specific change can you make this week to direct your trust only to Him?
?
If you were to honestly examine your life, where have you acted as if you are the 'second'—trying to create your own world of success, recognition, or security? How does the argument that any second is deficient expose the futility of your efforts? Will you surrender that area to Allah alone now?

The Second Proof: Finite or Infinite Number of Gods
Al-Shahrastānī presents the second proof for divine oneness using a dichotomy of finite vs. infinite number.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how the argument from the limitation of number proves that the number of gods cannot be finite or infinite, leading to the necessity of one God.

Core Idea
The number of gods must be one; finite and infinite numbers both lead to absurdities.

This proof from mutual exclusion (takāfu') by al-Shahrastānī demonstrates the impossibility of multiple gods. By analyzing two possibilities—that the number of gods stops at a known number or continues infinitely—the author shows both are impossible, leaving only one God. Let's explore the reasoning.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without analysis, then the translation. Let the author's dichotomy come through.
Notice the conditional phrasing: 'either it stops at a known number or it does not'.
2
Identify the two branches
The passage presents two possibilities: a finite number or an infinite number. Find each and see how both are argued to be impossible.
3
Understand the analogy with size and number
The author compares quantity of number to quantity of area: a specific size requires a measurer, so a specific number of gods requires a delimiting agent.
This analogy is key to understanding why a finite number is problematic.
4
Recognize why infinite is impossible
An infinite number of coexisting gods would be unordered and contained in existence, which is rationally impossible.
Think about the implications of an actual infinite set of beings.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
والوجه الثاني في تقرير التكافي: أن الأمر لا يخلو إما أن يقف في عدد معلوم فيستدعي الاقتصار على عدد محصور مقتضيا حاصر، فإن الكمية من حيث العدد كالكمية من حيث المساحة، أليس لو كان واحدًا ذا حجم وعظم مشكل اقتضى مشكلا لذلك إذا كان ذا مقدار وعدد اقتضى مقدرا، وإن لم يقف في عدد معلوم اقتضى أعدادًا غير متناهية محصورة في الوجود غير مترتبة وذلك محال، وبالجملة ما لا دليل عليه عقلا فتجويز وجوده تقدير عقلي، والمجوز عقلا المقدر ذهنا ليس بإله. فلا تغفل عن هذه الدقيقة وافرق في المعقولات بين تقدير المحال لفظا أو فرضا، وبين تجويزه عقلا أو عقدا، واعلم أن التقدير المذكور في الكتاب فرض محال لفظاً ليس بطلانه عقلا.
The second aspect in establishing the argument of mutual exclusion: The matter is not free: either it stops at a known number, then it necessitates restriction to a limited number which requires a delimiting agent. For quantity in terms of number is like quantity in terms of area. Is it not that if it were one having size and magnitude, it would necessitate a form, so if it has measure and number, it necessitates a measurer? And if it does not stop at a known number, it necessitates infinite numbers contained in existence without order, and that is impossible. In summary, whatever has no rational proof for it, then allowing its existence is a rational estimation, and the one who allows rationally that which is estimated mentally is not a god. Do not neglect this subtlety, and distinguish in rational matters between estimation of the impossible in speech or assumption, and between allowing it rationally or as a belief. And know that the estimation mentioned in the book is a verbal assumption of the impossible, not its rational impossibility.
Source pages p. 94

Commentary
This passage presents the second proof for the oneness of God, known as the argument from mutual exclusion (ta‘ādul or takāfu’). The author argues that the number of gods must be determined: either it stops at a specific finite number or it does not. If finite, that number must have a cause that delimits it, implying a delimiting agent (ḥāṣir). This is analogous to how a specific size requires a measurer. If the number does not stop at a finite number, it would be infinite, but an infinite number of simultaneously existing gods is impossible because they would lack order and be contained in existence, which is absurd. The author then warns against a subtle confusion: allowing the existence of something without rational proof is mere mental estimation (taqdīr ‘aqlī), not real possibility. A god cannot be merely mentally estimated. He distinguishes between imagining the impossible in speech (lafẓ) and actually deeming it rationally possible. The estimation in the book is a verbal hypothesis of the impossible, not a rational concession.

Key Terms
تقرير التكافي
عدد معلوم
غير متناهية
محال

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
What does the second proof conclude about a finite number of gods?
A) It is possible if each has a specific role.
B) It requires a delimiting agent, which leads to an infinite regress.
C) It is impossible because it would need a cause for that number.
D) It is the only rational option.

Key Takeaway
Both finite and infinite numbers of gods are rationally impossible, proving the necessity of one God.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
If you truly believe that only one God exists, why do you sometimes act as if there are multiple masters in your life? What 'gods' compete for your obedience?
?
The argument shows that an infinite number of gods is impossible. But do you treat any created thing as if it were unlimited in its power over you? What is that thing, and what step will you take today to stop treating it as a god?
?
The proof demands a single delimiting agent for our existence. Are you willing to let God alone set the boundaries of your desires, or do you insist on being the one who defines what is enough? What do you need to surrender to feel complete?

The Subtle Distinction: Verbal Assumption vs. Rational Possibility
Al-Shahrastānī cautions against confusing verbal hypotheses with rational possibility.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will distinguish between merely imagining the impossible in speech and actually deeming it rationally possible, which is crucial for proper theological reasoning.

Core Idea
Imagining something verbally doesn't make it rationally possible.

After presenting the proof, al-Shahrastānī warns against a subtle confusion: the difference between hypothetically accepting an impossibility for the sake of argument and truly conceding its rational possibility. This paragraph clarifies that the earlier hypothetical scenario was a verbal exercise, not a rational concession.

Guided Path
1
Read the warning passage
Read the Arabic passage once without analysis, then the translation. Note the strong language and emphasis.
The author uses 'do not neglect this subtlety' (فلا تغفل عن هذه الدقيقة).
2
Identify the key terms
Pay attention to terms like 'تقدير عقلي' (rational estimation), 'لفظا' (verbally), 'فرضا' (hypothetically), 'عقلا' (rationally), and 'عقدا' (as belief).
These terms are crucial for the distinction.
3
Understand the distinction
The author distinguishes between: 1) تقدير المحال لفظا – estimating the impossible in speech/hypothesis, and 2) تجويزه عقلا – rationally allowing it as possible. Only the latter is relevant to creed.
4
Apply to the earlier proof
The finite/infinite hypothesis in the proof was a verbal assumption (تقدير المحال لفظا), not a rational concession that multiple gods could exist.
Realize that the proof does not rely on the actual possibility of multiple gods; it shows they are impossible.
