Objection and Answer: Good and Evil Do Not Imply Two Gods
The author records an objection based on good and evil and gives the beginning of the answer.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how the objector argues that the presence of both good and evil implies two gods, and how the mutakallimun respond by grounding the indication of action in contingency rather than moral quality.

Core Idea
Moral qualities do not indicate the agent; only contingency and existence do.

This lesson presents a classic objection to tawheed drawn from the existence of good and evil in the world. The objector claims that good and evil must come from different sources, since a single agent cannot will both. The answer, from the verifiers among the mutakallimun, shifts the ground of argument from moral quality to the sheer fact of existence and contingency. This reframes the debate entirely.

Guided Path
1
Read the Objection
Read the first paragraph of the Arabic passage and its translation. Notice how the objector uses the same method as the author's earlier argument from corruption, but applies it to good and evil.
The objector draws a parallel: just as two gods would corrupt the heavens, good and evil corruption implies two gods.
2
Identify the Core Premise
The objector asserts that a being who wills good absolutely cannot will evil absolutely. Ask yourself: is this necessarily true? The answer will challenge this.
3
Read the Answer's Opening
Read the second paragraph. The answer redefines the basis of 'indication of action' (dalalat al-fi'l). Instead of moral quality, it points to contingency (imkan) and preponderance of existence.
The answer is incomplete, but the key reframing is already clear.
4
Contrast the Two Approaches
Compare the objector's assumption (moral quality indicates agent) with the answer's claim (contingency and existence indicate agent). Which one is more fundamental?
The answer argues that existence as such is either wholly good or neutral, so moral qualities are not relevant to identifying the agent.
5
Reflect on the Incompleteness
Note that the answer cuts off. What might the mutakallimun say next? Likely they would elaborate that the same agent can produce both good and evil because the agent's will is not constrained by the moral quality of the act.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
سؤال على وجه دلالة الفعل:
فإن قيل: صادفنا في الموجودات خيرًا أو شرًا أو نظمًا وفسادًا، ووجه دلالة الخير يخالف وجه دلالة الشر بل وجود الخير يدل على مريد الخير، ووجود الشر يدل على مريد الشر، ومريد الخير على الإطلاق لا يكون مريدًا للشر على الإطلاق، كما أن مريد الخير في فعل مخصوص لا يكون مريد الشر في ذلك الفعل بعينه، فاختلاف وجه دلالة الفعل بالتضاد دل على اختلاف الفاعلين بالتضاد، وكما أنكم استدللتم بأنه لو كان معه إله لفسدت السموات والأرض، فنحن نستدل بفساد فيهما خيرًا أو شرًا على إلهين اثنين.

والجواب على قاعدة المتكلمين:
قال المحققون منهم: وجه دلالة الفعل على الفاعل هو الجواز والإمكان وترجح جانب الوجود على العدم، وذلك لم يختلف خيرًا كان أو شرًا، فالوجود من حيث هو وجود خير كله أو يقال: لا خير فيه ولا شر، والفعل من حيث وجوده
Question on the aspect of the indication of action: It is said: We encounter in existing things good or evil, or order and disorder. The aspect of indication of good differs from the aspect of indication of evil. Rather, the existence of good indicates a willing-of-good, and the existence of evil indicates a willing-of-evil. The willing-of-good absolutely cannot be a willing-of-evil absolutely, just as the willing-of-good in a specific act cannot be a willing-of-evil in that same act. So the difference in the aspect of indication of action by contradiction indicates a difference in the agents by contradiction. And just as you argued that if there were another god with Him, the heavens and earth would be corrupted, so we argue by the corruption in them—good or evil—for two gods.

The answer according to the rule of the mutakallimun: The verifiers among them said: The aspect of the indication of the action to the agent is possibility and contingency and the preponderance of the side of existence over non-existence. That does not differ whether it is good or evil. Existence qua existence is all good, or it is said: there is no good or evil in it. The action in terms of its existence
Source pages p. 95

Commentary
This passage presents an objection and the beginning of an answer. The objector argues that the presence of both good (khayr) and evil (sharr) in the world indicates two different agents, since a being who wills good absolutely cannot also will evil absolutely. The objector uses the same form of argument as the author: just as the author says two gods would corrupt the heavens, the objector says the existence of both good and evil corruption implies two gods. The answer, from the verifiers (muḥaqqiqūn) among the mutakallimun, states that the indication of an action to its agent is based on contingency (jawāz) and possibility (imkān) and the preponderance of existence over non-existence. This indication does not change whether the action is good or evil. Existence as such is either wholly good or neutral. Thus, the same agent can produce both good and evil. The answer is cut off here, but the core point is that the moral quality of an action is not part of the evidence for the agent.

Key Terms
دلالة الفعل (indication of action)
خير (good)
شر (evil)
الجواز والإمكان (contingency and possibility)
ترجح (preponderance)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Moral qualities (good/evil) directly indicate the moral character of the agent (i.e., a good act requires a good agent, an evil act requires an evil agent).
Correction: The mutakallimun argue that the indication of an action to its agent is based on contingency and existence, not on moral qualities. Therefore, good and evil do not imply different agents.

Checkpoint
According to the answer, what is the basis for indicating an agent from an action?
A) The moral quality of the action.
B) The order or disorder in the action.
C) The possibility and contingency of the action.
D) The presence of good or evil.

Key Takeaway
The indication of an action to its agent is based on contingency and existence, not on moral good or evil.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you see evil in the world, do you ever doubt that Allah alone is the Creator? How does this lesson's answer—that indication is based on contingency, not morality—challenge or strengthen your heart's conviction in tawhid? Will you actively remind yourself of this when you witness tragedy?
?
The objector assumed good and evil are contradictory and therefore require two gods. But have you ever, in your own life, judged God's actions or decrees by your own moral standards, as if He must act according to your sense of good and evil? What specific situation comes to mind? How will you realign your thinking with the truth that His action is known by existence, not by your moral approval?
?
If existence as such is all good or neutral, then every trial and hardship you have faced is ultimately good in terms of its existence. Can you honestly say you see your past difficulties this way? What would change in your relationship with Allah if you truly believed that every created thing is, in its existence, from Him alone and is good? Make a concrete commitment to thank Him for one hardship this week.

The Dualist Objection from Good and Evil
An objection from good and evil for two agents.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the dualist objection that good and evil in the world imply two separate agents, and how it challenges monotheism.

Core Idea
Good and evil seem to point to different wills — forcing us to ask: one God or two?

This passage presents a classic objection from dualists: the world contains both good and evil, order and corruption. Since a good-willing agent cannot will evil, the difference implies opposite agents. This objection uses the same logical structure as the monotheist argument for unity, but turns it against itself. Let's read the objection carefully.

Guided Path
1
Read the objection
Read the Arabic passage once without trying to analyse it. Then read the translation. Let the objector's own rhythm come through before you interpret.
Notice the parallel phrasing: good indicates a desirer of good, evil indicates a desirer of evil.
2
Identify the key move
The objector argues that differing implications (dalala) indicate opposing agents. Just as you (monotheists) argued that two gods cause corruption, we argue that the presence of both good and evil shows two gods.
This is a clever reversal of the standard monotheist proof.
3
Consider the challenge
Ask yourself: how could a monotheist respond? Is it necessary that a single agent cannot will both good and evil? The upcoming Ash'ari answer will address this.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فإن قيل : صادفنا في الموجودات خيرًا أو شرا أو نظما وفسادًا ، ووجه دلالة الخير يخالف وجه دلالة الشر بل وجود الخير يدل على مريد الخير، ووجود الشر يدل على مريد الشر، ومريد الخير على الإطلاق لا يكون مريدا للشر على الإطلاق، كما أن مريد الخير في فعل مخصوص لا يكون مريد الشر في ذلك الفعل بعينه، فاختلاف وجه دلالة الفعل بالتضاد دل على اختلاف الفاعلين بالتضاد، وكما أنكم استدللتم بأنه لو كان معه إله لفسدت السموات والأرض، فنحن نستدل بفساد فيهما خيرًا أو شرا على إلهين اثنين.
For it is said: We encounter in existing things good or evil, or order and corruption. The aspect of implication of good differs from the aspect of implication of evil; rather, the existence of good indicates a desirer of good, and the existence of evil indicates a desirer of evil. And the desirer of good absolutely is not a desirer of evil absolutely, just as the desirer of good in a particular act is not a desirer of evil in that same act. So the differing aspect of implication of the act through opposition indicates a difference of agents through opposition. And just as you have argued that if there were another god with Him, the heavens and earth would be corrupted, so we argue by the corruption in them of good or evil for two gods.
Source pages p. 95

Commentary
This is an objection (sual) against monotheism. It argues from the mixture of good and evil in the world. The opponent claims that the implication (dilala) of good is opposite to that of evil, so they must stem from opposed agents. A good-desiring agent cannot desire evil, so there must be two agents. They use the same method as the monotheist argument (that if there were two gods, the heavens and earth would be in corruption) but apply it to the existence of both good and evil to argue for two gods.

Key Terms
مريد الخير
مريد الشر
دلالة
تضاد

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: That the existence of good and evil necessarily requires two separate creators.
Correction: The Ash'ari response will show that good and evil are not absolute but relative to divine command, and that a single agent can will both good and evil for different reasons.

Checkpoint
What is the basis of the objection's argument?
A) The existence of order alone.
B) The existence of good and evil together.
C) The absence of any good.
D) The existence of only evil.

Key Takeaway
The dualist objection uses the mixture of good and evil to argue for two opposed agents, challenging the monotheistic claim of unity.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you personally experience hardship or witness evil, does a part of you instinctively question whether the same God who gives you good can also be behind your trials? How does the dualist argument expose a hidden doubt in your heart about God's oneness?
?
The objector claims that the desirer of good absolutely cannot be the desirer of evil absolutely. In your own life, do you ever find yourself attributing your blessings to God's love but blaming Him for your suffering? What does that reveal about your trust in the oneness of His will, and what step can you take today to surrender that division in your heart?

The Argument from Mixture of Good and Evil and the Philosophers' Way
Argument against dualism using mixture of good and evil.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how the mixture of good and evil in the world does not imply multiple divine agents, and how the Ash'ari response uses the unity of existence's implication.

Core Idea
Mixture of good and evil does not prove multiple sources; existence's implication is one.

The author presents a further argument against dualism. If good and evil can mix, why not posit a third agent for the mixture? But the opponent may claim the implication remains the same. The author turns the tables: just so, existence itself has a single implication, supporting monotheism. Finally, he notes that philosophers approach this differently.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without trying to analyse. Then read the translation. Let the author's own rhythm come through before you interpret.
Notice the rhetorical structure: question, objection, response.
2
Identify the argument's structure
The passage has four parts: (1) observation of mixture, (2) hypothetical objection for a third agent, (3) dualist counter-argument, (4) Ash'ari response and note on philosophers. Trace each part.
The key move is turning the dualist's own logic against them.
3
Reflect on the philosophical alternative
The author mentions that divine philosophers have a different method for proving unity. Reflect on how the conclusion is the same but the reasoning differs.
This shows that multiple valid arguments can support the same truth.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ونقول للتنويه : إنَّا كما صادفنا في الموجودات خيرًا وشرا متمايزين، فقد صادفنا في الموجودات خيرًا وشرًا مختلطين، فإن عالم الخير المحض هو عالم الملائكة، وعالم الشر المحض هو عالم الشياطين، وعالم الاختلاط والامتزاج هو عالم البشر، فهلا أثبتم ثالثًا ينسب إليه الامتزاج، فإن الممتزج من حيث هو ممتزج على طبيعة غير ما كان المنفرد عليها، فهلا كانت الطبيعة المذكورة دالة على ثالث، لكن قلتم : الخير والشر لم يختلفا بالامتزاج بل دلالتهما واحد، كذلك نقول : لم يختلفا في الوجود فإن دلالة الوجود واحدة. وقد سلك الفلاسفة الإلهيون طريقة أخرى في الوحدانية فأجابوا عن هذا السؤال على طريقتهم.
And we say for clarification: Indeed, just as we encounter in existents good and evil distinct, we also encounter in existents good and evil mixed. For the world of pure good is the world of angels, and the world of pure evil is the world of devils, and the world of mixture and blending is the world of humans. Why then do you not affirm a third [agent] to which the mixture is attributed? For the mixed inasmuch as it is mixed has a nature different from that which was separate. So why is not that nature indicative of a third? But you said: Good and evil do not differ by mixture; rather their implication is one. Similarly, we say: They do not differ in existence, for the implication of existence is one. And the divine philosophers have followed another method concerning unity, and they answered this question according to their way.
Source pages p. 95

Commentary
This passage presents another angle in the debate: if good and evil are distinct, their mixture would imply a third agent. But the opponent would say that the implication of good and evil is still the same even when mixed. The Ash'ari responds that exactly so: the implication of existence is one, so no need for multiple agents. Finally, it notes that the philosophers have a different approach to this issue.

Correction: The author argues that mixture does not change the implication of existence, so no third agent is needed. The implication of existence remains one, supporting monotheism.

A) It is purely good.
B) It is purely evil.
C) It is a mixture of good and evil.
D) It has no good or evil.

?

In your daily life, where do you tend to mix the roles of Allah and secondary causes—like attributing success to your own effort or failure to others? The Contingency Argument: Existence of a Necessary Being
Philosophers establish the existence of a Necessary Being via contingency and rejection of infinite regress.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how the philosophers prove the existence of a Necessary Being using the concepts of contingency and the impossibility of infinite regress.

Core Idea
Contingent beings cannot cause themselves; they require a preponderator, leading to a Necessary Being.

This lesson presents the first part of the philosophers' proof for God's oneness. They begin by dividing existence into the necessary and the contingent, then argue that an infinite chain of contingent causes is impossible. This forces us to recognize a Being that exists necessarily through itself.

Guided Path
1
Identify the division of existence
Read the opening sentence: existence is divided into what is necessary in itself and what is contingent in itself. Recognize that this dichotomy is foundational.
Contingent means its existence is not guaranteed by its own essence.
2
Understand the need for a preponderator
Every contingent needs something to tip its existence over non-existence. This 'preponderator' is what brings it into being.
Without a preponderator, a contingent would remain in non-existence.
3
Trace the two possible scenarios
Contingents either form an infinite chain or stop at a Necessary Being. The passage argues against the infinite chain.
The impossibility of infinite regress is a key rational principle.
4
Conclude the existence of a Necessary Being
Since infinite regress is impossible, the chain must end in a Being that is necessary through itself.
This Being is the foundation for all existence.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فأما طريقتهم قالوا : قد شهد العقل الصريح بأن الوجود ينقسم إلى ما يكون واجبًا في ذاته وإلى ما يكون ممكنا في ذاته وكل ممكن فإنما يترجح جانب الوجود منه على جانب العدم بمرجح ، فإما أن تذهب الممكنات إلى غير نهاية، أو تقف على واجب بذاته غير ممكن لكنها لو ذهبت إلى غير النهاية لما وجدت ؛ إذ كان يتوقف وجود كل ممكن على سبق وجود مرجحه وذلك محال، فلا بد أن يقف على واجب بذاته، ثم الواجب بذاته لا يجوز أن يكون لذاته مبادي يجتمع منها واجب الوجود لا أجزاء كمية ولا كالمادة والصورة والجنس والفصل، فإن المبادي يجب أن تكون سابقة على ذات واجب الوجود، ويكون واجبا بها لا بذاته، وقد فرضنا الواجب بذاته فهذا خلف. ولو قدرنا اثنين واجبي الوجود اشتركا في كون كل واحد منهما واجب الوجود فلا بد أن ينفصل أحدهما عن الثاني بفصل يخصه، فيكون وجوب الوجود مشتركا فيه وهو ذاتي لهما فيكون جنسا، وما يخص أحدهما دون الثاني فصلا، ويكون ذاته متركبًا منهما، ويجب أن تكون الأجزاء متقدمة بالذات على ذاته فيكون متأخرًا عنها بالذات فيكون واجبًا بها لا بنفسه وذاته فهو خلف، فإذا نوع واجب الوجود سواء أطلق إطلاقا أو خصص تخصيصا لا يجوز أن يكون إلا واحدا، فوجوده وجوبه، ووجوبه حقيقته وحقيقته وحدته، ووحدته تخصصه.
As for their method, they said: sound reason has witnessed that existence is divided into what is necessary in itself and what is contingent in itself. Every contingent has its side of existence preponderated over the side of non-existence by a preponderator. So either contingents go on to infinity, or they stop at something necessary in itself, not contingent. But if they went on to infinity, they would not exist, since the existence of every contingent depends on the prior existence of its preponderator, and that is impossible. So it must stop at something necessary in itself. Then, the necessary in itself cannot have principles from which the necessary of existence is composed, whether quantitative parts or like matter, form, genus, and differentia. For the principles must be prior to the essence of the Necessary Being, and He would be necessary through them, not through Himself, but we have assumed Him necessary in Himself, so this is a contradiction. And if we suppose two necessary in existence, they would share in the fact that each one of them is necessary in existence. Then one must be distinguished from the other by a differentia particular to it. Thus, necessity of existence would be shared between them, which is essential to both, so it would be a genus. And what is particular to one without the other would be a differentia, and the essence of each would be composed of them. The parts must be prior in essence to the essence, so it would be posterior to them in essence, and thus it would be necessary through them, not through itself and its essence; this is a contradiction. So the type of necessary of existence, whether considered absolutely or specifically, cannot be except one. So His existence is His necessity, His necessity is His reality, His reality is His unity, and His unity is His particularisation.
Source pages p. 97

Commentary
This passage presents the philosophers' (falasifa) proof for the oneness of God. It is a structured argument comprising two main parts: first, establishing the existence of a Necessary Being, and second, proving that this Being must be one and simple. The argument begins by dividing existence into necessary and contingent. Contingents require a preponderator to tip the scale from non-existence to existence. An infinite regress of contingents is impossible because each depends on a prior preponderator, which would never terminate, leading to non-existence. Thus, a Necessary Being must exist. Then, the author proves that this Necessary Being cannot have any composition. If it had parts (quantitative, or metaphysical like matter/form, genus/differentia), those parts would be prior to it, making it dependent—but it is necessary through itself, not through others. This is a contradiction. Furthermore, if there were two Necessary Beings, they would share the attribute of necessity (a genus) and differ by a differentia. Each would be composed of genus and differentia, again making them dependent. Therefore, the Necessary Being must be one, and its attributes are identical: existence equals necessity, necessity equals reality, reality equals unity, and unity equals particularization.

Key Terms
واجب الوجود
ممكن
مرجح

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: An infinite chain of causes can exist without a first cause.
Correction: The passage argues that if each contingent depends on a prior preponderator, an infinite regress would never begin, so no contingent would exist. Thus, a First Cause (Necessary Being) is necessary.

Checkpoint
Why can't the chain of contingents go on to infinity?
A) Because God prevents it.
B) Because each contingent depends on the prior existence of its preponderator, so an infinite chain would never start and nothing would exist.
C) Because the human mind cannot conceive of infinity.
D) Because the philosophers said so.

Key Takeaway
Contingent beings require a preponderator, and an infinite regress of causes is impossible; therefore, a Necessary Being must exist.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
You are a contingent being whose every moment depends on the Necessary Being. If you truly believed that your next breath is a gift from Him alone, how would your anxiety change? What one thought can you hold onto today to remind yourself of this dependence?
?
The philosophers argued that every contingent needs a preponderator. Look at the blessings in your life—your health, your faith, your family. Do you attribute them to your own efforts, or do you see Allah as the continuous Preponderator? Write one specific blessing you often take for granted and commit to thanking Him for it today.
?
An infinite regress of causes is impossible, and you exist. Are you living as though you are a self-sufficient being, or do you actively acknowledge the Necessary Being who initiated your existence? What small habit can you start today to make this acknowledgment a reality in your daily life?

Oneness and Simplicity of the Necessary Being
Proof that the Necessary Being is one and simple, with identity of attributes.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why the Necessary Being must be one and have no composition, and how His attributes are identical to His essence.

Core Idea
The Necessary Being cannot have parts or partners; He is simple, one, and His attributes are identical.

After establishing that a Necessary Being exists, the philosophers now prove that this Being must be one and simple. They argue that if He had any composition (parts, genus, differentia) or if there were two Necessary Beings, they would depend on something prior, contradicting His necessity. The lesson concludes with the identity of His attributes.

Guided Path
1
Understand why composition is impossible
Read the passage where it says the Necessary Being cannot have principles or parts. If He had parts, the parts would be prior to Him, making Him dependent—contradicting His necessity.
Parts include quantitative parts and metaphysical ones like matter/form, genus/differentia.
2
Examine the argument against two Necessary Beings
If there were two, they would share necessity (common genus) and differ by a differentia. Each would then be composed of genus and differentia, again making them dependent.
This argument rules out multiplicity in the Necessary Being.
3
Conclude that the Necessary Being is one and simple
The passage then states that the Necessary Being cannot be more than one, whether considered absolutely or specifically.
Unity is inherent in the concept of the Necessary Being.
4
Recognize the identity of attributes
The final sentence equates existence, necessity, reality, unity, and particularization—all are one and the same in the Necessary Being.
This eliminates any composition in the divine essence.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فأما طريقتهم قالوا : قد شهد العقل الصريح بأن الوجود ينقسم إلى ما يكون واجبًا في ذاته وإلى ما يكون ممكنا في ذاته وكل ممكن فإنما يترجح جانب الوجود منه على جانب العدم بمرجح ، فإما أن تذهب الممكنات إلى غير نهاية، أو تقف على واجب بذاته غير ممكن لكنها لو ذهبت إلى غير النهاية لما وجدت ؛ إذ كان يتوقف وجود كل ممكن على سبق وجود مرجحه وذلك محال، فلا بد أن يقف على واجب بذاته، ثم الواجب بذاته لا يجوز أن يكون لذاته مبادي يجتمع منها واجب الوجود لا أجزاء كمية ولا كالمادة والصورة والجنس والفصل، فإن المبادي يجب أن تكون سابقة على ذات واجب الوجود، ويكون واجبا بها لا بذاته، وقد فرضنا الواجب بذاته فهذا خلف. ولو قدرنا اثنين واجبي الوجود اشتركا في كون كل واحد منهما واجب الوجود فلا بد أن ينفصل أحدهما عن الثاني بفصل يخصه، فيكون وجوب الوجود مشتركا فيه وهو ذاتي لهما فيكون جنسا، وما يخص أحدهما دون الثاني فصلا، ويكون ذاته متركبًا منهما، ويجب أن تكون الأجزاء متقدمة بالذات على ذاته فيكون متأخرًا عنها بالذات فيكون واجبًا بها لا بنفسه وذاته فهو خلف، فإذا نوع واجب الوجود سواء أطلق إطلاقا أو خصص تخصيصا لا يجوز أن يكون إلا واحدا، فوجوده وجوبه، ووجوبه حقيقته وحقيقته وحدته، ووحدته تخصصه.
As for their method, they said: sound reason has witnessed that existence is divided into what is necessary in itself and what is contingent in itself. Every contingent has its side of existence preponderated over the side of non-existence by a preponderator. So either contingents go on to infinity, or they stop at something necessary in itself, not contingent. But if they went on to infinity, they would not exist, since the existence of every contingent depends on the prior existence of its preponderator, and that is impossible. So it must stop at something necessary in itself. Then, the necessary in itself cannot have principles from which the necessary of existence is composed, whether quantitative parts or like matter, form, genus, and differentia. For the principles must be prior to the essence of the Necessary Being, and He would be necessary through them, not through Himself, but we have assumed Him necessary in Himself, so this is a contradiction. And if we suppose two necessary in existence, they would share in the fact that each one of them is necessary in existence. Then one must be distinguished from the other by a differentia particular to it. Thus, necessity of existence would be shared between them, which is essential to both, so it would be a genus. And what is particular to one without the other would be a differentia, and the essence of each would be composed of them. The parts must be prior in essence to the essence, so it would be posterior to them in essence, and thus it would be necessary through them, not through itself and its essence; this is a contradiction. So the type of necessary of existence, whether considered absolutely or specifically, cannot be except one. So His existence is His necessity, His necessity is His reality, His reality is His unity, and His unity is His particularisation.

Commentary
This passage presents the philosophers' (falasifa) proof for the oneness of God. It is a structured argument comprising two main parts: first, establishing the existence of a Necessary Being, and second, proving that this Being must be one and simple. The argument begins by dividing existence into necessary and contingent. Contingents require a preponderator to tip the scale from non-existence to existence. An infinite regress of contingents is impossible because each depends on a prior preponderator, which would never terminate, leading to non-existence. Thus, a Necessary Being must exist. Then, the author proves that this Necessary Being cannot have any composition. If it had parts (quantitative, or metaphysical like matter/form, genus/differentia), those parts would be prior to it, making it dependent—but it is necessary through itself, not through others. This is a contradiction. Furthermore, if there were two Necessary Beings, they would share the attribute of necessity (a genus) and differ by a differentia. Each would be composed of genus and differentia, again making them dependent. Therefore, the Necessary Being must be one, and its attributes are identical: existence equals necessity, necessity equals reality, reality equals unity, and unity equals particularization.

Allah's Existence
beginner
4 min
Existence Divided: Necessary and Possible
Sanusi classifies existence and introduces the concept of the preponderating factor.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the rational division of existence into the necessary in itself and the possible in itself, and why every possible requires a preponderating factor.

Core Idea
Existence splits into necessary and possible, and possibilities need a preponderator.

Sanusi begins his proof with a pure reason classification. This foundational division underlies the entire kalam argument for God's existence, as it shows that not all existence is self-explanatory.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage
Read the Arabic and translation to grasp the initial classification.
Notice the phrase ‘shahida al-‘aqlu al-ṣarīḥu’.
2
Distinguish necessary and possible
Identify the definitions: necessary in itself cannot not exist, possible can exist or not.
3
Understand the role of the murajjih
Realize that every possible being leans toward existence only due to an external factor.
This sets up the need for a first cause.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فأما طريقتهم قالوا: قد شهد العقل الصريح بأن الوجود ينقسم إلى ما يكون واجبًا في ذاته وإلى ما يكون ممكنا في ذاته وكل ممكن فإنما يترجح جانب الوجود منه على جانب العدم بمرجح، فإما أن تذهب الممكنات إلى غير نهاية، أو تقف على واجب بذاته غير ممكن لكنها لو ذهبت إلى غير النهاية لما وجدت؛ إذ كان يتوقف وجود كل ممكن على سبق وجود مرجحه وذلك محال، فلا بد أن يقف على واجب بذاته، ثم الواجب بذاته لا يجوز أن يكون لذاته مبادي يجتمع منها واجب الوجود لا أجزاء كمية ولا كل المادة والصورة والجنس والفصل، فإن المبادي يجب أن تكون سابقة على ذات واجب الوجود، ويكون واجبا بها لا بذاته، وقد فرضنا الواجب بذاته فهذا خلف. ولو قدرنا اثنين واجبي الوجود اشتركا في كون كل واحد منهما واجب الوجود فلا بد أن ينفصل أحدهما عن الثاني بفصل يخصه، فيكون وجوب الوجود مشتركا فيه وهو ذاتي لهما فيكون جنسا، وما يخص أحدهما دون الثاني فصلا، ويكون ذاته متركبًا منهما، ويجب أن تكون الأجزاء متقدمة بالذات على ذاته فيكون متأخرا عنها بالذات فيكون واجبا بها لا بنفسه وذاته فهو خلف، فإذا نوع واجب الوجود سواء أطلق إطلاقا أو خصص تخصيصا لا يجوز أن يكون إلا واحدا، فوجوده وجوبه، ووجوبه حقيقته وحقيقته وحدته، ووحدته تخصصه وتعينه من غير أن يتمايز وجوب عن وجود، ووجود عن ماهية وحقيقة، وعن هذا نفوا صفات الباري تعالى زائدة على الذات، كما سيأتي تفصيل ذلك في مسألة الصفات إن شاء الله تعالى.
As for their method, they said: Pure reason has witnessed that existence is divided into what is necessary in itself and what is possible in itself. Every possible is such that the side of its existence outweighs the side of its non-existence only by a preponderating factor. So either the possible things go on to infinity, or they stop at a necessary being in itself not possible. But if they went on to infinity, they would not exist, since the existence of every possible depends on the prior existence of its preponderating factor, and that is impossible. Thus they must stop at a necessary being in itself. Then, the necessary being in itself cannot have principles from which it is composed as necessary existence—neither quantitative parts nor matter and form nor genus and differentia—because principles must be prior to the essence of the necessary being, and it would then be necessary through them and not through itself, but we have supposed it necessary through itself, so this is a contradiction. And if we suppose two beings both necessary in existence, they share in each being necessary in existence; then one must be distinguished from the other by a differentia specific to it. Thus necessity of existence is common to them and is essential to them, so it becomes a genus, and what distinguishes one from the other becomes a differentia, and its essence becomes composed of them. The parts would then be essentially prior to its essence, so it would be posterior to them essentially, and thus it would be necessary through them and not through its own self and essence—a contradiction. Therefore, the type of necessary existence, whether taken absolutely or specified, can only be one. So its existence is its necessity, its necessity is its reality, its reality is its unity, its unity is its specification and determination, without any distinction between necessity and existence, or between existence and quiddity and reality. And on this basis they negated attributes of the Most High beyond the essence, as will be detailed in the topic of attributes, God willing.
Source pages p. 97

Commentary
This passage is a rational proof (burhān) for the existence and oneness of God. It begins by stating that pure reason testifies that existence is divided into the necessary in itself (wājib bi-dhātihi) and the possible in itself (mumkin bi-dhātihi). For every possible being, its existence outweighs non-existence only due to a preponderating factor (murajjih). This leads to a dilemma: either the chain of possible beings goes on infinitely, or it ends in a necessary being. The author argues that an infinite regress is impossible because each possible depends on a prior preponderating factor, resulting in an infinite chain of dependencies—which is absurd. Therefore, the chain must terminate in a necessary being that is not possible. Next, the author argues that this necessary being cannot be composite, whether in quantity (like parts) or in definition (like matter, form, genus, differentia). If it had components, those components would be prior to it, and it would be necessary through them, contradicting its being necessary through itself. Similarly, if there were two necessary beings, they would share the attribute of necessary existence, making that a genus, and each would have a differentia distinguishing it. This would make each essence composite, with the parts prior, so they would be necessary through others—again a contradiction. Hence, the necessary being must be one. Finally, the author draws a theological consequence: because the necessary being is absolutely simple, without any distinction between its essence and attributes, the Ash'aris (the author's school) negate attributes separate from the essence. This is a preview of the later discussion on divine attributes.

Key Terms

ممكن
مرجح

According to the passage, what is needed for a possible being to exist?
A) Its own essence
B) A preponderating factor
C) An infinite regress
D) Nothing

Key Takeaway
Existence is either necessary in itself or possible, and possibilities depend on an external preponderator.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
If every possible being—including you—depends on an external preponderator for existence, what does that say about your self-sufficiency? Are you living as though you are self-made, or do you constantly acknowledge your dependence on the Necessary Being?
?
This passage shows that reason itself testifies to the need for a preponderator. When have you ignored reason to assume you can manage without God? Will you today consciously trace your existence back to Him, not just as a concept but as a living reality?
?
The preponderator is not a thing you can manipulate. How does this truth challenge your desire to control your own destiny? Are you willing to surrender your imagined autonomy and let the Necessary Being be the decisive factor in every aspect of your life?

Ending the Infinite Regress: The Necessary Being
Sanusi argues against infinite regression and establishes the existence of a necessary being.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why an infinite regress of possible beings is impossible and why the chain must terminate in a necessary being.

Core Idea
An infinite chain of possibles is impossible, so a necessary being must exist.

Having established that each possible requires a preponderating factor, Sanusi now presents the dilemma: either the chain of possibles goes on infinitely or it ends with a necessary being. He demonstrates that infinite regress leads to an absurdity.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage
Read the Arabic and translation to follow the dilemma.
Identify the two options.
2
Understand the impossibility of infinite regress
Each possible's existence depends on the prior existence of its preponderator, leading to an infinite chain that would never begin.
This is a key argument in kalam.
3
Conclude the necessary being
The only logical alternative is a necessary being that is not itself possible.
This necessary being is the First Cause.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فأما طريقتهم قالوا: قد شهد العقل الصريح بأن الوجود ينقسم إلى ما يكون واجبًا في ذاته وإلى ما يكون ممكنا في ذاته وكل ممكن فإنما يترجح جانب الوجود منه على جانب العدم بمرجح، فإما أن تذهب الممكنات إلى غير نهاية، أو تقف على واجب بذاته غير ممكن لكنها لو ذهبت إلى غير النهاية لما وجدت؛ إذ كان يتوقف وجود كل ممكن على سبق وجود مرجحه وذلك محال، فلا بد أن يقف على واجب بذاته، ثم الواجب بذاته لا يجوز أن يكون لذاته مبادي يجتمع منها واجب الوجود لا أجزاء كمية ولا كل المادة والصورة والجنس والفصل، فإن المبادي يجب أن تكون سابقة على ذات واجب الوجود، ويكون واجبا بها لا بذاته، وقد فرضنا الواجب بذاته فهذا خلف. ولو قدرنا اثنين واجبي الوجود اشتركا في كون كل واحد منهما واجب الوجود فلا بد أن ينفصل أحدهما عن الثاني بفصل يخصه، فيكون وجوب الوجود مشتركا فيه وهو ذاتي لهما فيكون جنسا، وما يخص أحدهما دون الثاني فصلا، ويكون ذاته متركبًا منهما، ويجب أن تكون الأجزاء متقدمة بالذات على ذاته فيكون متأخرا عنها بالذات فيكون واجبا بها لا بنفسه وذاته فهو خلف، فإذا نوع واجب الوجود سواء أطلق إطلاقا أو خصص تخصيصا لا يجوز أن يكون إلا واحدا، فوجوده وجوبه، ووجوبه حقيقته وحقيقته وحدته، ووحدته تخصصه وتعينه من غير أن يتمايز وجوب عن وجود، ووجود عن ماهية وحقيقة، وعن هذا نفوا صفات الباري تعالى زائدة على الذات، كما سيأتي تفصيل ذلك في مسألة الصفات إن شاء الله تعالى.
As for their method, they said: Pure reason has witnessed that existence is divided into what is necessary in itself and what is possible in itself. Every possible is such that the side of its existence outweighs the side of its non-existence only by a preponderating factor. So either the possible things go on to infinity, or they stop at a necessary being in itself not possible. But if they went on to infinity, they would not exist, since the existence of every possible depends on the prior existence of its preponderating factor, and that is impossible. Thus they must stop at a necessary being in itself. Then, the necessary being in itself cannot have principles from which it is composed as necessary existence—neither quantitative parts nor matter and form nor genus and differentia—because principles must be prior to the essence of the necessary being, and it would then be necessary through them and not through itself, but we have supposed it necessary through itself, so this is a contradiction. And if we suppose two beings both necessary in existence, they share in each being necessary in existence; then one must be distinguished from the other by a differentia specific to it. Thus necessity of existence is common to them and is essential to them, so it becomes a genus, and what distinguishes one from the other becomes a differentia, and its essence becomes composed of them. The parts would then be essentially prior to its essence, so it would be posterior to them essentially, and thus it would be necessary through them and not through its own self and essence—a contradiction. Therefore, the type of necessary existence, whether taken absolutely or specified, can only be one. So its existence is its necessity, its necessity is its reality, its reality is its unity, its unity is its specification and determination, without any distinction between necessity and existence, or between existence and quiddity and reality. And on this basis they negated attributes of the Most High beyond the essence, as will be detailed in the topic of attributes, God willing.
Source pages p. 97

Commentary
This passage is a rational proof (burhān) for the existence and oneness of God. It begins by stating that pure reason testifies that existence is divided into the necessary in itself (wājib bi-dhātihi) and the possible in itself (mumkin bi-dhātihi). For every possible being, its existence outweighs non-existence only due to a preponderating factor (murajjih). This leads to a dilemma: either the chain of possible beings goes on infinitely, or it ends in a necessary being. The author argues that an infinite regress is impossible because each possible depends on a prior preponderating factor, resulting in an infinite chain of dependencies—which is absurd. Therefore, the chain must terminate in a necessary being that is not possible. Next, the author argues that this necessary being cannot be composite, whether in quantity (like parts) or in definition (like matter, form, genus, differentia). If it had components, those components would be prior to it, and it would be necessary through them, contradicting its being necessary through itself. Similarly, if there were two necessary beings, they would share the attribute of necessary existence, making that a genus, and each would have a differentia distinguishing it. This would make each essence composite, with the parts prior, so they would be necessary through others—again a contradiction. Hence, the necessary being must be one. Finally, the author draws a theological consequence: because the necessary being is absolutely simple, without any distinction between its essence and attributes, the Ash'aris (the author's school) negate attributes separate from the essence. This is a preview of the later discussion on divine attributes.

Key Terms
واجب الوجود
ممكن
مرجح
غير النهاية

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
Why is an infinite regress of possible beings impossible according to the passage?
A) Because it leads to a contradiction in the definition of possible
B) Because existence of each possible depends on the prior existence of its preponderator, creating an infinite chain without a first cause
C) Because infinite chains are impossible in time
D) Because the necessary being would be unnecessary

Key Takeaway
An infinite regress of possibles is impossible, proving the existence of a necessary being.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
If everything you rely on for stability—your health, job, relationships—is dependent on something else, what is the one thing that must be the ultimate anchor? And how does your daily life reflect that trust?
?
The argument shows an infinite chain of dependencies is impossible. Is there any area of your life where you are still waiting for a 'first cause' to change before you commit? What would it mean to stop that regress and ground yourself in the Necessary Being today?
?
An infinite regress means nothing would exist. Yet you exist right now. Does that fact press you to ask who or what is the necessary being that makes your very existence possible? And are you living as if that being is real?

The Oneness and Simplicity of the Necessary Being
Sanusi demonstrates that the necessary being is simple and one, and negates additional attributes.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why the necessary being must be simple (non-composite) and one (unique), and why this leads to negating attributes distinct from the essence.

Core Idea
The necessary being is indivisible, non-composite, and absolutely one.

Having proven the existence of a necessary being, Sanusi now examines its nature. He argues that it cannot have any composition—whether physical parts or metaphysical components like genus and differentia—and that there cannot be two necessary beings. This culminates in the Ash'ari position that God's attributes are not distinct from His essence.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage
Read the full passage covering the arguments against composition and plurality.
Pay attention to the logical structure: first against composition, then against duality.
2
Understand the argument against composition
If the necessary being had parts (physical or conceptual), those parts would be prior to it, making it dependent on them and thus not necessary through itself.
This is a reductio ad absurdum.
3
Understand the argument against two necessary beings
Two necessary beings would share the attribute of necessary existence, making it a genus, and each would have a distinguishing differentia, leading to composition and dependency.
4
Draw the conclusion
The necessary being is one, and its existence, necessity, reality, and unity are identical. Hence, attributes are not additional to the essence.
This previews the later discussion on attributes.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فأما طريقتهم قالوا: قد شهد العقل الصريح بأن الوجود ينقسم إلى ما يكون واجبًا في ذاته وإلى ما يكون ممكنا في ذاته وكل ممكن فإنما يترجح جانب الوجود منه على جانب العدم بمرجح، فإما أن تذهب الممكنات إلى غير نهاية، أو تقف على واجب بذاته غير ممكن لكنها لو ذهبت إلى غير النهاية لما وجدت؛ إذ كان يتوقف وجود كل ممكن على سبق وجود مرجحه وذلك محال، فلا بد أن يقف على واجب بذاته، ثم الواجب بذاته لا يجوز أن يكون لذاته مبادي يجتمع منها واجب الوجود لا أجزاء كمية ولا كل المادة والصورة والجنس والفصل، فإن المبادي يجب أن تكون سابقة على ذات واجب الوجود، ويكون واجبا بها لا بذاته، وقد فرضنا الواجب بذاته فهذا خلف. ولو قدرنا اثنين واجبي الوجود اشتركا في كون كل واحد منهما واجب الوجود فلا بد أن ينفصل أحدهما عن الثاني بفصل يخصه، فيكون وجوب الوجود مشتركا فيه وهو ذاتي لهما فيكون جنسا، وما يخص أحدهما دون الثاني فصلا، ويكون ذاته متركبًا منهما، ويجب أن تكون الأجزاء متقدمة بالذات على ذاته فيكون متأخرا عنها بالذات فيكون واجبا بها لا بنفسه وذاته فهو خلف، فإذا نوع واجب الوجود سواء أطلق إطلاقا أو خصص تخصيصا لا يجوز أن يكون إلا واحدا، فوجوده وجوبه، ووجوبه حقيقته وحقيقته وحدته، ووحدته تخصصه وتعينه من غير أن يتمايز وجوب عن وجود، ووجود عن ماهية وحقيقة، وعن هذا نفوا صفات الباري تعالى زائدة على الذات، كما سيأتي تفصيل ذلك في مسألة الصفات إن شاء الله تعالى.
As for their method, they said: Pure reason has witnessed that existence is divided into what is necessary in itself and what is possible in itself. So either the possible things go on to infinity, or they stop at a necessary being in itself not possible. But if they went on to infinity, they would not exist, since the existence of every possible depends on the prior existence of its preponderating factor, and that is impossible. Thus they must stop at a necessary being in itself. Then, the necessary being in itself cannot have principles from which it is composed as necessary existence—neither quantitative parts nor matter and form nor genus and differentia—because principles must be prior to the essence of the necessary being, and it would then be necessary through them and not through itself, but we have supposed it necessary through itself, so this is a contradiction. And if we suppose two beings both necessary in existence, they share in each being necessary in existence; then one must be distinguished from the other by a differentia specific to it. Thus necessity of existence is common to them and is essential to them, so it becomes a genus, and what distinguishes one from the other becomes a differentia, and its essence becomes composed of them. The parts would then be essentially prior to its essence, so it would be posterior to them essentially, and thus it would be necessary through them and not through its own self and essence—a contradiction. Therefore, the type of necessary existence, whether taken absolutely or specified, can only be one. So its existence is its necessity, its necessity is its reality, its reality is its unity, its unity is its specification and determination, without any distinction between necessity and existence, or between existence and quiddity and reality. And on this basis they negated attributes of the Most High beyond the essence, as will be detailed in the topic of attributes, God willing.

Inconsistency of the Muʿtazila on Tamānuʿ
Critique of Muʿtazila's inconsistent use of tamānuʿ argument.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how the Muʿtazila inconsistently applied the tamānuʿ argument by permitting differences in will and action between God and humans.

Core Idea
The Muʿtazila borrowed the tamānuʿ argument but abandoned it when it came to God's will and human will.

The tamānuʿ argument is a powerful tool used to prove that there cannot be two gods. Both the Ashʿarīs and the Muʿtazila used it, but the author points out a critical flaw in the Muʿtazila's application. Let's examine their inconsistency and why it matters for theological coherence.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage and translation. Notice the two parts: the Muʿtazila's agreement on tamānuʿ and their subsequent inconsistency.
Focus on the shift from agreement to contradiction.
2
Understand the tamānuʿ argument
Recall that tamānuʿ means mutual exclusivity: if two gods existed, their wills would conflict, leading to absurdity. The Muʿtazila accepted this for gods, but not for God and humans.
3
Identify the inconsistency
The passage says the Muʿtazila permitted differences in God's will and human will, and in their actions. Why does this undermine tamānuʿ? Because tamānuʿ requires complete conflict of wills; if difference is allowed, the argument collapses.
Ask yourself: if God and humans can will opposite things without contradiction, why can't two gods?
4
Reflect on the theological lesson
The author uses this inconsistency to show that the Muʿtazila did not fully apply rational consistency. This highlights the importance of coherent reasoning in theology.
This passage is a critique of method, not just a point of doctrine.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وقد سلكت المعتزلة طريق التمانع في استحالة الإلهين كما سلكناه، ولم يستمر لهم ذلك حيث جوزوا اختلافًا في إرادة الباري سبحانه وإرادة العبد، واختلافا في الفعل فلا تمانع
And the Muʿtazila followed the path of mutual exclusivity (tamānuʿ) concerning the impossibility of two gods, as we followed it, but they did not continue on that [path] since they permitted a difference in the will of the Exalted God and the will of the servant, and a difference in action, so there is no mutual exclusivity.
Source pages p. 98

Commentary
This passage is a critical observation about the Muʿtazila's use of the tamānuʿ argument. The author notes that the Muʿtazila also employed the argument of mutual exclusivity to prove the impossibility of two gods, just as the author's own school did. However, they were inconsistent because they allowed a differentiation between God's will and human will, and a differentiation in actions between God and humans. This inconsistency undermines the tamānuʿ argument, which requires that two independent agents cannot both will contradictory things. By allowing such differences, the Muʿtazila fail to maintain the necessary mutual exclusivity.

Key Terms

إلهين
إرادة
فعل

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The tamānuʿ argument is unique to Ashʿarīs and the Muʿtazila never used it.
Correction: The passage states that the Muʿtazila also followed the tamānuʿ path, but they applied it inconsistently.

Checkpoint
Why does the author say the Muʿtazila did not consistently apply the tamānuʿ argument?
A) Because they denied the existence of two gods.
B) Because they allowed differences in will and action between God and humans.
C) Because they rejected the argument entirely.
D) Because they used it only for the attributes, not for the essence.

Key Takeaway
The Muʿtazila's acceptance of differences in will and action between God and humans breaks the logical consistency of the tamānuʿ argument they used against multiple gods.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Are you willing to follow a truth wherever it leads, even if it challenges your own accustomed beliefs? The Mu'tazila used an argument against others but abandoned it when it touched their own position. Where in your life do you apply standards to others that you fail to apply to yourself?
?
Is there any belief you hold about God or His attributes that you apply inconsistently in your daily life? How can you commit to logical consistency in your understanding of tawhid, even when it is uncomfortable?
?
When you encounter a contradiction between what you claim to believe about God's oneness and your actual reliance on created things, do you adjust your actions to match your claims, or do you rationalize the inconsistency? The Mu'tazila chose the latter. What do you choose?

Al-Kaʿbī's Proof and Its Critique
Al-Kaʿbī's proof and the Neoplatonic objection

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand al-Kaʿbī's argument for God's oneness and why it requires additional conditions to rule out the possibility of multiple eternal intellects.

Core Idea
Al-Kaʿbī's simple proof fails unless extra conditions exclude distinct eternal intellects.

In this passage, al-Kaʿbī proposes a concise rational argument for the impossibility of two gods. However, the objection comes quickly: what about Neoplatonic multiple eternal intellects? This shows that theological arguments must be carefully constructed to avoid counterexamples. As you read, notice how the opponent's objection forces al-Kaʿbī's method to be refined.

Guided Path
1
Read al-Kaʿbī's argument
Read the first part of the passage where al-Kaʿbī presents his proof that two indistinguishable self-subsisting entities are impossible.
Notice the key condition: no distinction by time, place, or property.
2
Examine the objection
The opponent points out that rationalists affirm multiple eternal substances with distinct properties. Consider why this refutes the simple version of the proof.
3
Understand the required refinement
The passage concludes that to make the proof work, one must add conditions to exclude the possibility of such intellects. Reflect on what those conditions might be.
This highlights the importance of precision in kalam arguments.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وسلك الكعبي (۱) طريقاً آخر (۳) وقال : لو قدرنا قايمين بأنفسهما لا يتميز أحدهما عن الآخر بزمان أو بمكان أو حيز، ولا يكون لأحدهما حقيقة خاصية يمتاز أحدهما عن الثاني بها فهو مستحيل عقلا، والخصم ربما يقول : هذا هو نفس النزاع ا عبرت عنه (۳) تعبيراً وصيرته (٤) دليلا، فإن جماعة من العقلاء صاروا إلى إثبات جواهر عقلية من عقول مجردة ونفوس مجردة قديمات قائمات بأنفسها، ويمتاز بعضها عن بعض بخواص وحقائق ولم تمنع العقول ،ثبوتها ومن أراد أن يسلك هذه الطريقة فلا بد أن يزيد فيها شروطا حتى يتحقق الاستحالة وتتضح للعقل، والله أعلم.
And al-Kaʿbī (1) followed another path (3) and said: If we suppose two self-subsisting entities that are not distinguished from each other by time or by place or by location, and neither has a particular reality by which it is distinguished from the other, that is intellectually impossible. And the opponent may say: This is the very point of contention that you expressed (3) as an expression and made it (4) a proof. For a group of rationalists have gone to asserting intellectual substances (jawāhir ʿaqliyya) from abstract intellects (ʿuqūl mujarrada) and abstract souls (nufūs mujarrada), eternal, self-subsisting, distinguished from each other by properties and realities, and intellects did not prevent their existence. And whoever wants to follow this method must add conditions to it until the impossibility is realized and becomes clear to the intellect. And God knows best.
Source pages p. 99

Commentary
This passage presents al-Kaʿbī's alternative argument for the impossibility of two gods. He proposes that if we suppose two self-subsisting entities (qāʾimayn bi-anfusihimā) that are indistinguishable in time, place, or location, and each lacks any unique property (khāṣṣiyya), then their existence is intellectually impossible (mustaḥīl ʿaqlan). However, the opponent may object that this merely restates the point at issue, since some rationalists (like Neoplatonists) affirm the existence of multiple eternal, self-subsisting substances—such as abstract intellects and souls—that are distinguished only by their properties and realities. Therefore, al-Kaʿbī's simple formulation fails; to establish impossibility, one must add additional conditions to exclude the possibility of such distinct eternal entities.

Key Terms
كعبي
جواهر عقلية
عقول مجردة
نفوس مجردة
قديم
قائم بنفسه
مستحيل

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Al-Kaʿbī's proof by itself proves that two gods cannot exist.
Correction: The proof fails unless it excludes the possibility of multiple eternal intellects that are distinguished by their properties.

Checkpoint
What objection does the author mention against al-Kaʿbī's proof?
A) That it is too complicated to understand.
B) That rationalists affirm the existence of multiple eternal self-subsisting intellects and souls.
C) That it relies on scripture rather than reason.
D) That it has been refuted by the Muʿtazila.

Key Takeaway
Al-Kaʿbī's simple proof for oneness fails unless refined to exclude the possibility of multiple eternal intellects with distinct properties.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Have you ever assumed an argument was convincing without examining the assumptions it hides? This passage shows that even a proof for tawhid can be contested. Which of your beliefs about Allah's oneness have you not critically examined? Will you commit today to studying a deeper, more robust proof, like the argument from contingency?
?
The opponent here restates the point of contention rather than engaging with a deeper critique. In your own practice, do you truly listen to counterarguments to your faith, or do you merely restate your position? What is one objection to tawhid you have avoided addressing, and how will you seek to answer it from the Qur'an and reason?
?
Al-Kaʿbī's proof fails unless extra conditions are added. How often do you settle for simple, surface-level reasons for your faith? Are you willing to do the intellectual work to build a foundation that can withstand critical scrutiny? What step will you take today to strengthen your conviction in Allah's oneness?

The Proof from Contingency: Every Originated Thing Needs a Creator
Proof from contingency and impossibility of infinite regress.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the proof from contingency that every originated being requires a necessary creator, and why an infinite regress of causes is impossible.

Core Idea
Every possible thing needs a cause to exist; an endless chain of causes is impossible.

This lesson introduces a clear proof for the existence of a necessary creator. The argument is based on the observation that anything that comes into existence is contingent—it could have been otherwise—and therefore requires a cause that tips the balance. The author then shows that this cause cannot itself be contingent without leading to an infinite regress, which is rationally impossible. Reading this passage will help you grasp a foundational argument in Islamic theology.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once, then the translation. Pay attention to the logical flow.
2
Identify the key concepts
Find the terms for 'originated thing', 'preponderator', 'possible existence/non-existence', and 'infinite regress'. Notice how they fit together.
3
Understand the regress argument
The author argues that if the preponderator itself is originated, we need another preponderator, ad infinitum. Ask: why is an infinite regress problematic? Because the series itself would be contingent and need an external cause.
This is the heart of the proof.
4
Consider the rebuttal of distinctions
The text says the need is based on possibility, not on similarity or difference. Why does this matter? It blocks objections that would allow a different kind of cause.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
برهان آخر أوضح مما قد سبق، وهو أن كل حادث يحتاج إلى محدث من حيث إنه كان في نفسه وباعتبار ذاته جائز الوجود والعدم، فلما ترجح جانب الوجود على العدم احتاج إلى مرجح بالضرورة، ثم ذلك المرجح إن كان حادثا احتاج إلى مرجح ثم يتسلسل القول فيه إلى ما لا نهاية له وجهة الاحتياج لا يختلف الحال فيها بين حادث في ذاته سبحانه وتعالى وبين محدث مباين ذاته، فإنه إنما احتاج بجهة بين طرفي جواز الوجود والعدم لا بجهة التباين وغير التباين، وهذا قاطع لا تردد به اعتراض عليه.
Another proof clearer than what has preceded: that every originated thing needs an originator, inasmuch as it was in itself and by consideration of its essence possible of existence and non-existence. So when the side of existence outweighed non-existence, it needed a preponderator necessarily. Then if that preponderator is originated, it needs a preponderator, and then the argument would regress infinitely. And the aspect of need does not differ between an originated thing in His Essence, exalted be He, and an originated thing distinct from His Essence. For it only needs by virtue of the two sides of possibility of existence and non-existence, not by virtue of distinctness or non-distinctness. And this is decisive, with no hesitation in objecting to it.
Source pages p. 113

Commentary
This passage presents a proof for the necessity of a creator. It begins by stating that every originated thing (hadith) is contingent—possible in itself to exist or not exist. To come into existence, it requires a preponderator (murajjih) that tilts the balance toward existence. If that preponderator is also originated, it too would need a preponderator, leading to an infinite regress (tasalsul). The author then emphasizes that the need for a preponderator is based solely on the thing's possibility (between the two sides of existence and non-existence), not on whether the preponderator is similar to or distinct from the originated thing. Thus, the argument is decisive and not subject to objection.

Key Terms
حادث
محدث
جائز الوجود والعدم
مرجح
تسلسل

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: A temporal being could be caused by another temporal being in an infinite chain, and that would be sufficient.
Correction: The proof shows that an infinite chain of contingent causes is impossible because each member still requires a cause, leaving the entire chain unexplained. Only a necessary being can provide an ultimate explanation.

Checkpoint
What is the basis for a contingent thing's need for a preponderator?
A) Its distinctness from the preponderator.
B) Its possibility of existence and non-existence.
C) Its similarity to other originated things.
D) Its temporal origination.

Key Takeaway
The proof from contingency shows that every originated thing requires a necessary creator, and an infinite chain of contingent causes is impossible because the need for a cause is based on contingency itself, not on any relation.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
You are a contingent being: you could have not existed. Do you live each day with the awareness that your existence is a gift sustained moment by moment by the Creator, or do you act as if you own your own life? What would change if you truly felt your contingency?
?
The proof shows that infinite regress is impossible—you cannot postpone your accountability indefinitely. Is there an area of your life (repentance, obedience, sincerity) where you assume you will have endless future chances? How does the impossibility of infinite regress challenge that?
?
If the need for a cause arises from contingency itself, then every breath you take depends on a preponderator. Are you conscious of that dependency in your daily activities? Name one specific habit through which you will acknowledge your need for Allah today.

Refutation of Two Objections on the Origination of Temporal Beings
Refutation of two objections: self-origination and origination by eternal power/will.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how the author refutes two objections against the necessity of an originator for temporal beings, and why linguistic distinctions do not imply rational differences.

Core Idea
Neither self-origination nor eternal power suffices; all temporal beings need an originator.

In this passage, the author anticipates possible objections to the doctrine that every temporal being requires an originator. He considers two alternatives: that a temporal being could arise by itself, or that it could arise by an eternal power and will. He then refutes both, showing that reason demands an originator and that linguistic distinctions are irrelevant to rational necessity.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once without analysing. Then read the translation to grasp the overall argument.
Pay attention to the structure: statement of possibilities, then two refutations.
2
Identify the two objections and refutations
The passage presents three possibilities, but two are objections. Identify each objection and how the author refutes it.
The first objection is self-origination; the second is origination by eternal power and will.
3
Analyze the linguistic argument
Notice the author's distinction between linguistic and rational differences. The terms 'lazim' and 'muta'addi' are key.
The author uses examples like 'he stood' to show that even intransitive verbs involve act and object in a way reason does not separate.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ونقول : إن تصور وجود حادث لا بإحداث فإما أن يقال : يحدث ذلك الحادث بنفسه أو بقدرته أو بمشيئة قديمة. فإن قيل : يحدث بنفسه فقد باهتوا العقل الصريح بضرورة حكمه بأن ما لم يكن فكان احتاج إلى محدث مكون. فإن قيل : أحدث بقدرة ومشيئة فقد ناقضوا قضية العقل، فإن ذلك الحادث إذا جاز أن يحدث بالقدرة فلم لا يجوز أن يحدث المحدثات كلها بقدرته ومشيئته ؛ إذ لا فرق في نظر العقل بين الحادث والمحدث من حيث إنه لم يكن فكان. والفرق بينهما من حيث اللغة : أن أحدهما لازم، والثاني متعد لا ينتهض فرق من حيث العقل، فإنا إذا قلنا: قام وقعد وجاء وذهب، كان الحكم لازماً والقيام والقعود والمجي والذهاب فعله ومفعوله كما هو فعله، والعقل لا يفرق بين فعل الإنسان شيئًا في نفسه وبين فعله في غيره على مذهب من قال به، ومن قال : إن الفعل لا يباين محل القدرة، فلم يفرق فيه بين الفعل والمفعول، ولا متمسك للخصم في هذه المسألة إلا بأمر لغوي ولفظ اصطلاحي.
And we say: The conception of an originated thing without bringing into existence – then either it is said: that originated thing occurs by itself, or by its power, or by an eternal will. If it is said: it occurs by itself, then they have blasphemed against clear reason by its necessary judgment that what was not and then was needs an originator that brings it into existence. If it is said: it occurred by power and will, then they have contradicted the verdict of reason. For if that originated thing is allowed to occur by power, why is it not allowed for all originated things to occur by His power and will? For there is no difference in the view of reason between the originated and the originator insofar as it was not and then was. And the difference between them from the linguistic standpoint: that one is intransitive and the other transitive, which does not constitute a difference from the rational standpoint. For when we say: he stood, he sat, he came, he went, the ruling is intransitive, and standing, sitting, coming, going are his act and his object as they are his act. And reason does not differentiate between a human's doing something in himself and his doing it in another, according to the view of whoever said it. And whoever said that the act does not leave the locus of power did not differentiate between act and object. And the opponent has no support in this issue except a linguistic matter and a conventional expression.
Source pages p. 114

Commentary
This passage is an objection and answer. The author presents a hypothetical: if someone claims a temporal being (hadith) can exist without an originator (created by nothing), there are three possibilities: it occurs by itself, by its own power, or by an eternal will. The author refutes the first by stating that reason necessarily judges that anything that comes into existence needs a maker (muhdith). For the second possibility (by eternal power and will), the author argues that if one temporal being can occur by that power, then why can't all? Reason sees no difference between an originated thing and the originator in terms of coming into existence from non-existence. The linguistic distinction between intransitive (lazim) and transitive (muta'addi) verbs does not justify a rational difference. Examples like 'he stood' (intransitive) and its act 'standing' are both act and object; reason does not separate between a human acting in himself and acting on another. Thus the opponent's argument relies only on language and convention, not rational necessity.

Key Terms
لا بإحداث
باهتوا العقل
ناقضوا القضية
لازم
متعد

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The linguistic distinction between transitive and intransitive verbs implies a rational difference in the nature of origination.
Correction: The author shows that the distinction is merely linguistic and does not affect rational necessity; examples like 'he stood' demonstrate that even intransitive acts involve an act and object.

Checkpoint
What does the author say about the distinction between intransitive and transitive verbs in this context?
A) It is a rational distinction.
B) It is a linguistic distinction only.
C) It proves that some acts cannot be done by God.
D) It shows that humans can create.

Key Takeaway
The author argues that neither self-origination nor origination by eternal power can avoid the rational necessity of an originator; linguistic differences do not change rational judgment.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?

Have you ever used a linguistic or conventional distinction to avoid the rational truth that everything in your life—including your own actions—ultimately originates from Allah? In what specific area have you done this, and what would it cost you to admit that you have no independent power?

?
The author argues that reason does not differentiate between an act in oneself and an act in another. If your next 'good deed' is not actually from you but from Allah's origination, then what is your responsibility? Does this realization relieve you or burden you with gratitude and accountability?

The Rational Necessity of a Creator for Contingent Events
Proof that a contingent event requires a creator, refuting two alternatives.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the rational argument that any contingent event (something that did not exist and then came to be) must have a creator, and why alternative explanations are impossible.

Core Idea
A contingent event cannot cause itself or arise without a creator.

This passage presents a decisive rational proof that every contingent event requires a creator. The author anticipates two alternative explanations – self-causation and production by eternal power alone – and demonstrates why both are logically untenable. By carefully following the argument, you will see why the necessity of a creator is a rational certainty.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and its translation
Read the Arabic passage once without analyzing, then read the English translation. Let the flow of the argument settle in your mind.
Notice the author's use of 'قاطع' (decisive) to emphasize the strength of the proof.
2
Identify the two alternatives
The passage presents two ways someone might claim a contingent event exists without a creator: either by itself (self-causation) or by an eternal power and will. Find these in the text.
The word 'فإما' introduces the alternatives.
3
Examine the refutation of self-causation
The author dismisses self-causation as a violation of the rational necessity that whatever did not exist then exists needs a maker. Understand why this is considered a 'باهت' (absurdity).
4
Examine the refutation of production by power/will
The author argues that if one event can be produced by eternal power, then all events can, collapsing any distinction. Note the key point: reason sees no difference between a contingent event and a created thing from the perspective of coming into existence.
The distinction between 'لازم' (intransitive) and 'متعد' (transitive) is introduced here.
5
Understand the linguistic vs rational distinction
The author shows that the difference between intransitive and transitive verbs is merely linguistic, not rational. Reason sees no difference between an agent acting upon himself and acting upon another. This undercuts the opponent's position.
The opponent's only support is linguistic convention, not rational argument.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
بين طرفي جواز الوجود والعدم لا بجهة التباين وغير التباين، وهذا قاطع لا تردد له باعتراض عليه. ونقول : إن تصور وجود حادث لا بإحداث فإما أن يقال : يحدث ذلك الحادث بنفسه أو بقدرته أو بمشيئة قديمة. فإن قيل : يحدث بنفسه فقد باهتوا العقل الصريح بضرورة حكمه بأن ما لم يكن فكان احتاج إلى محدث مكون. فإن قيل : أحدث بقدرة ومشيئة فقد ناقضوا قضية العقل، فإن ذلك الحادث إذا جاز أن يحدث بالقدرة فلم لا يجوز أن يحدث المحدثات كلها بقدرته ومشيئته؛ إذ لا فرق في نظر العقل بين الحادث والمحدث من حيث إنه لم يكن فكان. والفرق بينهما من حيث اللغة : أن أحدهما لازم، والثاني متعد لا ينتهض فرقا من حيث العقل، فإنا إذا قلنا: قام وقعد وجاء وذهب، كان الحكم لازما والقيام والقعود والمجيء والذهاب فعله ومفعوله كما هو فعله، والعقل لا يفرق بين فعل الإنسان شيئا في نفسه وبين فعله في غيره على مذهب من قال به، ومن قال : إن الفعل لا يباين محل القدرة، فلم يفرق فيه بين الفعل والمفعول، ولا متمسك للخصم في هذه المسألة إلا بأمر لغوي ولفظ اصطلاحي.
Between the two ends of possibility of existence and non-existence, not by way of opposition and non-opposition, and this is decisive, having no hesitation due to an objection against it. And we say: The conception of a contingent event existing without an act of bringing into existence: either it is said that that event comes to be by itself, or by His power, or by an eternal will. If it is said: it comes to be by itself, then they have confounded clear reason by the necessity of its judgment that what was not and then came to be needs a maker who brings it into existence. If it is said: it came to be by power and will, then they have contradicted the judgment of reason, for if that event is possible to come to be by power, then why is it not possible for all things brought into existence to come to be by His power and will? Since there is no difference in the view of reason between the contingent event and the thing brought into existence from the perspective that it was not and then came to be. And the difference between them from the perspective of language is that one is intransitive and the other is transitive, which does not constitute a difference from the perspective of reason. For if we say: he stood and he sat and he came and he went, the judgment is intransitive, and the standing and sitting and coming and going are his action and his object as they are his action. And reason does not differentiate between a man's action upon something in himself and his action upon something else, according to the view of one who says so, and one who says that action does not separate from the locus of power, so he did not differentiate between the action and the object. And the opponent has no support in this matter except a linguistic matter and a terminological expression.
Source pages p. 114

Commentary
This passage is a proof that a contingent event (hadith) cannot occur without a creator (muhdith). It begins by stating that the distinction between possible existence and non-existence is decisive and not subject to valid objection. Then it presents a dilemma: either the contingent event comes to be by itself, or by an eternal power and will. The first alternative is dismissed as an absurdity that contradicts the rational necessity that anything that did not exist and then exists requires a maker. The second alternative leads to a contradiction because if one contingent event can be produced by eternal power, then all events can, which would collapse the distinction between God's actions and created events. The author argues that the difference between intransitive verbs (like 'he stood') and transitive verbs (like 'he made') is merely linguistic, not rational. Reason sees no difference between an agent acting upon himself and acting upon another. Therefore, the opponent's position has no basis except in linguistic convention.

لازم (lazim)
متعد (muta'addi)
