Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some think the difference between God creating and a creature acting is merely linguistic, so the argument for a creator is weak.
Correction: The author argues that the linguistic distinction between intransitive and transitive verbs does not hold rationally. Reason sees no difference between an agent acting in himself and acting upon another, so the opponent has no rational basis to distinguish between a created event and God's action.

Checkpoint

Key Takeaway
The argument establishes that contingent events necessitate a creator, and attempts to bypass this by self-causation or by appealing to linguistic distinctions are rationally invalid.

The Fitra Proof for the Existence of the Creator
The author presents the fitra proof and supports it with Quranic verses.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand that the existence of the Creator is known through innate human nature (fitra) and that this is supported by Qur'anic verses and the state of distress.

Core Idea
The existence of God is self-evident through human nature.

This passage presents the fitra proof for the existence of the Creator. The author argues that sound human natures instinctively recognize the existence of a wise, knowing, and powerful Maker. He then cites several Quranic verses that confirm this innate recognition and shows that even those who neglect it in good times turn to God in times of distress. This lesson will help you understand the strength and simplicity of this argument.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once without analyzing, then read the English translation. Let the words and verses sink in.
Pay attention to the key terms: fitra, darura, badiha.
2
Identify the core claim about fitra
Find where the author states that sound human natures testify to a Creator. Understand the phrase 'bida' of their thought'.
This is the logical foundation of the argument.
3
Notice the Quranic verses and their role
List the verses cited. They show that even polytheists acknowledge God as Creator, and people turn to Him in distress.
The verses serve as inductive evidence for the fitra claim.
4
Reflect on the distress argument
The author adds that people naturally turn to God in times of hardship, even if they neglect Him in good times. This reinforces the fitra.
5
Consider the implications
Think about what this means for those who claim atheism: they are either suppressing their nature or unaware of it.
This connects to the earlier lesson on rational judgments.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فإن الفطر السليمة الإنسانية شهدت بضرورة فطرتها وبديهة فكرتها على صانع حكيم عالم قدير: {أَفِي اللَّهِ شَكٌّ فَاطِرِ السَّمَوَاتِ وَالْأَرْضِ} {وَلَئِن سَأَلْتَهُم مِّنْ خَلَقَكُمْ لَيَقُولُنَّ اللَّهُ} {وَلَئِن سَأَلْتَهُم مِّنْ خَلَقَ السَّمَوَاتِ وَالْأَرْضَ لَيَقُولُنَّ خَلَقَهُنَّ الْعَزِيزُ الْعَلِيمُ}. وإن هم غفلوا عن هذه الفطرة في حال السراء، فلا شك أنهم يلوذون إليه في حال الضراء: {دَعَوُا اللَّهَ مُخْلِصِينَ لَهُ الدِّينَ} {وَإِذَا مَسَّكُمُ الضُّرُّ فِي الْبَحْرِ ضَلَّ مَن تَدْعُونَ إِلَّا إِيَّاهُ}.
For sound human natures (al-fiṭar al-salīma al-insāniyya) have testified, by the necessity of their innate disposition (fiṭra) and the intuitiveness of their thought (badīha fikratihā), to a wise, knowing, powerful Maker: {Is there doubt about Allah, the Originator of the heavens and the earth?} {And if you ask them who created you, they will certainly say: Allah} {And if you ask them who created the heavens and the earth, they will certainly say: The Mighty, the Knowing created them.} And even if they are heedless of this innate disposition (fiṭra) in times of ease, there is no doubt that they take refuge in Him in times of distress: {They call upon Allah, making their devotion pure for Him} {And when harm touches you at sea, those you call upon vanish except Him}.
Source pages p. 121

Commentary
This passage provides the positive proof for the existence of the Creator, building on the previous discussion. The author asserts that sound human natures (al-fiṭar al-salīma al-insāniyya) bear witness, by the necessity of their innate disposition (ḍarūrat fiṭratihā) and the immediacy of their thought (badīha fikratihā), to the existence of a wise, knowing, and powerful Maker. This is a claim of intuitive knowledge, not discursive reasoning. To support this, the author cites several Quranic verses. The first (Ibrahim 10) is a rhetorical question implying that there is no doubt about God as the Originator of the heavens and earth. The second (Zukhruf 87) notes that if you ask polytheists who created them, they will answer 'Allah'. The third (Zukhruf 9) similarly shows they admit that the Mighty, the Knowing created the heavens and earth. The author then adds that even if people neglect this innate recognition in times of prosperity, they inevitably turn to God in times of distress, citing Yunus 22 and Isra 67, which describe people calling earnestly upon God when in danger. Thus, the fitra (innate disposition) and distress-driven instinct both confirm the existence of the Creator. This argument establishes that the existence of God is known by necessity, not by theoretical deduction, and therefore the Dahriyya claim of chance does not constitute a genuine denial.

Key Terms

الضرورة
البديهة
الصانع
الحكيم
العليم
القدير

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: One might think that proving God's existence requires elaborate philosophical arguments.
Correction: The author argues it is self-evident through fitra and confirmed by Quranic verses and universal human experience.

Checkpoint

D) The prohibition of shirk

Key Takeaway
The existence of the Creator is known intuitively through innate human nature and is confirmed by Quranic verses and the instinctive turning to God in times of distress.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Recall a moment when you were in a crisis or great difficulty and you instinctively called out to Allah. How did that moment reveal the fitra within you? What can you do today to remember that raw sincerity and carry it into your daily life?
?
The lesson says that people are heedless of fitra in times of ease. When have you found yourself drifting from the awareness of Allah because life was comfortable? What would change if you committed to a daily reminder of His presence, even when everything is going well?
?
If this innate knowledge of Allah is truly universal, then what excuses remain for your own negligence of His commands? How will you stop suppressing your fitra and start living in accordance with its testimony?

The Subject of Divine Command: Tawhid, Not Existence
The author distinguishes between innate and commanded knowledge, using hadith and Quran to establish tawhid as the core of divine command.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why the divine command (taklif) concerns tawhid and rejection of shirk rather than knowledge of God's existence, and why the point of dispute between prophets and their people was tawhid.

Core Idea
Prophets were sent to preach tawhid, not to prove God's existence.

Having established that knowledge of the Creator's existence is innate through fitra, the author now explains the true subject of divine command. The prophets were not commanded to teach people that God exists—that is already known. Rather, the central call was to affirm God's oneness and reject partners. This lesson presents the evidence from hadith and Quran that tawhid is the core of the prophetic message.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once, then the English translation. Notice the logical flow: first a statement, then a hadith, then Quranic verses as proof.
The author uses a tafsir-style reasoning: since fitra gives existence, taklif focuses on tawhid.
2
Understand the distinction
Identify the key distinction: knowledge of God's existence (known innately) vs. knowledge of tawhid (commanded). Reflect on why this distinction matters for theology and for understanding the prophets' mission.
The hadith 'I was commanded to fight the people...' shows the practical criterion of faith: the shahada.
3
Examine the Quranic evidence
Look at the three Quranic verses cited. They all show that the dispute was about tawhid—people reacted negatively when Allah alone was mentioned, but accepted when partners were associated.
These verses confirm that the core issue in prophecy is monotheism, not theism.
4
Correct the common misunderstanding
Note the common misconception: that prophets primarily came to prove God's existence. The passage shows this is not the case; they came to call to tawhid.
This has implications for interfaith dialogue and dawah.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ولهذا لم يرد التكليف بمعرفة وجود الصانع، وإنما ورد بمعرفة التوحيد ونفي الشريك: {أمرت أن أقاتل الناس حتى يقولوا لا إله إلا الله}. ولهذا جعل محل النزاع بين الرسل وبين الخلق في التوحيد: {ذلك بأنه إذا دعي الله وحده كفرتم وإن يشرك به تؤمنوا}، {وَإِذَا ذُكِرَ اللَّهُ وَحْدَهُ اشْمَأَزَّتْ قُلُوبُ الَّذِينَ لَا يُؤْمِنُونَ بِالْآخِرَةِ}، {وَإِذَا ذُكِرَ رَبُّكَ فِي الْقُرْآنِ وَحْدَهُ وَلَّوْا عَلَى أَدْبَارِهِمْ نُفُورًا}.
And for this reason, the command (taklīf) was not revealed concerning knowledge of the existence of the Maker, but rather it was revealed concerning knowledge of tawḥīd (oneness) and denial of partners: {I was commanded to fight the people until they say: there is no god but Allah.} And for this reason the point of dispute between the messengers and the people was made to be tawḥīd: {That is because when Allah alone was invoked, you disbelieved, and if partners are associated with Him, you believe}, {And when Allah alone is mentioned, the hearts of those who do not believe in the Hereafter shrink}, {And when you mention your Lord alone in the Qur'an, they turn their backs in aversion}.
Source pages p. 122

Commentary
This passage draws a crucial theological distinction based on the previous fitra argument. Since the existence of the Creator is known innately (by fitra), the divine command (taklīf) was not concerned with knowing that God exists; rather, it was concerned with knowing tawḥīd (His oneness) and rejecting partners (shirk). The author supports this with the well-known ḥadīth: 'I was commanded to fight the people until they say: there is no god but Allah' (Bukhari, Muslim). This indicates that the required belief is the declaration of tawḥīd, not merely acknowledging a Creator. The author further notes that the actual point of dispute (maḥall al-nizā') between the messengers and their people was tawḥīd, as shown in three Qur'anic verses: About those who disbelieved when Allah alone was invoked but believed when partners were associated (Ghafir 12? Actually it is from Ghafir 12 or similar), and the verses from Az-Zumar 45 and Al-Isra 46, which describe aversion when Allah alone is mentioned. Thus, the central message is the exclusive worship of one God, not the abstract acknowledgment of a Creator, which is already universally accessible through fitra.

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The prophets' primary mission was to prove God's existence to people who were atheists or uncertain of a Creator.
Correction: The passage shows that the command and the dispute were about tawhid, not about God's existence, because the existence of the Maker is already known innately. Prophets called people to worship God alone and abandon shirk.

C) Because the prophets were sent only to pagans
D) Because the Qur'an does not mention it

Key Takeaway
Divine command (taklif) centers on tawhid and rejection of shirk, not on proving God's existence, which is already ingrained in human nature.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?

?
The passage says the prophets' dispute was about tawhid, not existence. Examine your own interactions: do you ever argue about God's existence but neglect the deeper call to exclusive devotion? What would change if you prioritized tawhid in your conversations?
?
If taklif centers on tawhid, then standing firm on 'la ilaha illa Allah' is the core of faith. Is there an area of your life where you are associating partners with Allah, even subtly, like seeking approval from people more than from Him? How will you realign your intentions today?

The Two Standard Proofs: Temporal Origination and Contingency
The author enumerates the two standard kalam proofs and states their self-evident nature.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the two main rational proofs used by theologians to establish the existence of the Creator, and how each is considered self-evident in its own domain.

Core Idea
The two proofs are complementary rational paths to the same truth.

In this passage, the author summarizes the two classical kalam proofs: one from temporal events (ḥudūth) to a temporal originator, and one from contingency (imkān) to a preponderator. He notes that both are called necessary and self-evident in their respective inferential contexts.

Guided Path
1
Read the Arabic passage
Read the description of the two proofs once to grasp the author's presentation of the theologians' and early philosophers' methods.
2
Identify the two proofs
Notice the difference: one uses temporal origination (ḥawādith → muḥdith), the other uses contingency (mumkināt → murajjiḥ).
Each proof starts from a different aspect of the world.
3
Understand the claim of necessity
The author says each proof is called necessity (ḍarūra) and self-evidence (badīha) in its direction of inference. This means that within its own framework, the inference is immediate.
This is a claim of certainty, not that the proofs are absolutely simple.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وقد سلك المتكلمون طريقين في إثبات الصانع تعالى؛ وهو بالحوادث على محدث صانع، وسلك الأوايل طريقاً آخر وهو الاستدلال بإمكان الممكنات على مرجح لأحد طرفي الإمكان، ويدعى كل واحد في جهة الاستدلال ضرورة وبديهة. وأنا أقول ما شهد به الحدوث أو دل عليه الإمكان بعد تقديم المقدمات دون ما شهدت به الفطرة الإنسانية من احتياج في ذاته إلى مدبر هو منتهى الحاجات فيرغب إليه ولا يرغب عنه ويستغني به ولا يستغني عنه، ويتوجه إليه ولا يعرض عنه ، ويفزع إليه في الشدائد والمهمات فإن احتياج نفسه أوضح له من احتياج الممكن الخارج إلى الواجب والحادث إلى المحدث، وعن هذا كانت تعريفاته الخلق سبحانه في هذا التنزيل على هذا المنهاج ) أمن يجيب المضطر إذا دعاه ) أمن ينجيكم من ظلمات البر) ( أمن يرزقكم من السماء والأرض ) ( أمن يبدأ الخلق ثم يعيده ) ، وعن هذا قال النبي صلى الله عليه وسلم : خلق الله تعالى على معرفته فاحتالهم الشيطان عنها، فتلك المعرفة هي ضرورة الاحتياج، وذلك الاحتيال من الشياطين هو تسويله الاستغناء ونفي الاحتياج والرسل مبعوثون لتذكير وضع الفطرة وتطهيرها عن تسويل الشيطان ، فإنهم الخلق الباقون على أصل الفطرة ( وَمَا كَانَ لَهُ عَلَيْهِم مِّن سُلْطَن ، وقال : ( فذكر إن نفعت الذكرى سيذكر من يخشى الأعلى ، وقوله : ( فَقُولَا لَهُ قَوْلاً لِّيَنا لعَلَّهُ يَتَذَكَّرُ أَوْ يَخْشَى [ طه : ٤٤]. ومن رحل إلى الله قربت مسافته حيث رجع إلى نفسه أدنى رجوع فعرف احتياجه إليه في تكوينه وبقائه وتقلبه في أحواله وأنحائه ، ثم استبصر في آيات الآفاق إلى آيات الأنفس، ثم استشهد به على الملكوت لا بالملكوت عليه : ( أو لم يكف بربك أنه على كل شيء شهيد ) [فصلت: ٥٣] عرفت الأشياء بربي، وما عرفت ربي بالأشياء، ومن غرق في بحر المعرفة لم يطمع في شط، ومن تعالى إلى ذروة الحقيقة لم من حط، فثبت بالدلايل والشواهد أن العالم لا يتعطل عن الصانع الحكيم القادر العليم سبحانه وتقدس.
The theologians have followed two ways in proving the Creator, Exalted is He: one is through temporal events (al-ḥawādith) to a temporal originator (muḥdith ṣāniʿ), and the early ones followed another way, which is inference through the contingency (imkān) of contingent things to a preponderator (murajjiḥ) of one of the two sides of possibility, and each one is called in the direction of inference necessity (ḍarūra) and self-evidence (badīha). And I say: what is attested by temporal origination (ḥudūth) or indicated by contingency (imkān) after presenting the premises, is not like what is attested by the human innate disposition (fiṭra) of need in its very essence for a Manager (mudabbir) who is the ultimate end of needs, so that one inclines to Him and does not turn away from Him, seeks sufficiency from Him and does not seek sufficiency from other than Him, turns to Him and does not turn away from Him, and takes refuge in Him in hardships and important matters. For one's own need is clearer to him than the need of an external contingent thing for the Necessary (al-wājib) and the temporal for the Temporal Originator (al-muḥdith). And because of this, His definitions of the creatures, glory be to Him, in this revelation follow this method: 'Is He who responds to the distressed when he calls Him?' [27:62], 'Is He who delivers you from the darknesses of the land and sea?' [6:63], 'Is He who provides for you from the sky and the earth?' [10:31], 'Is He who begins creation then repeats it?' [10:34]. And because of this, the Prophet, peace be upon him, said: 'God created humanity upon His recognition, then Satan made them doubt it.' So that recognition is the necessity of need, and that doubt from the devils is his embellishment of self-sufficiency and denial of need. And the messengers are sent to remind of the sound state of the innate disposition (fiṭra) and to purify it from the embellishment of Satan. For they are the creatures remaining upon the original innate disposition: 'And he had no authority over them' [15:42], and He said: 'So remind, if the reminder benefits; he who fears will remember' [87:9-10], and His saying: 'And speak to him with gentle speech that perhaps he may be reminded or fear' [20:44]. And whoever travels to God, his distance becomes near when he returns to himself the smallest return and recognizes his need for Him in his formation, his preservation, and his fluctuation in his states and conditions. Then he perceives with insight the signs of the horizons and the signs within themselves. Then he calls Him to witness over the dominion (malakūt), not the dominion over Him: 'Is it not sufficient that your Lord is over all things a Witness?' [41:53]. I know things by my Lord, and I do not know my Lord by things. And whoever drowns in the sea of knowledge does not aspire to a shore, and whoever ascends to the summit of reality does not descend from it. So it is established by proofs and evidences that the world is not devoid of the Wise, Powerful, Knowing Creator, glory be to Him and exalted.
Source pages p. 123

Commentary
This passage is a comparative evaluation of three approaches to proving the existence of God. The author first describes the two standard methods of the theologians (al-mutakallimūn): the proof from temporal origination (ḥudūth) which argues that the world's events require a temporal originator, and the proof from contingency (imkān) used by earlier philosophers, which argues that contingent things need a preponderator to tip the scales of possibility. Both are considered necessary and self-evident in their respective domains. The author then presents his own preferred approach: the innate human disposition (fiṭra) that feels an immediate need for a Manager (mudabbir) who fulfills all needs. He argues that this felt need is clearer than the rational inference from external contingents to the Necessary or from temporal events to a temporal originator. To support this, he cites Quranic verses that appeal to human need (the distressed caller, deliverance from darkness, provision, and creation) and a hadith that humans are created with innate knowledge of God but are led astray by Satan. The role of prophets is to remind people of this innate disposition and purify it. He then elaborates on the method of introspection: by turning to oneself, one recognizes need for God in all aspects of existence, then observes the signs in the universe, and finally witnesses God through the dominion (malakūt) rather than the other way around. The key point is experiential: 'I know things by my Lord, and I do not know my Lord by things.' The conclusion is that the world cannot be devoid of the Creator.

Key Terms
حدوث
إمكان
ضرورة
بديهة
محدث
مرجح

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: One might think the two proofs are contradictory or that one is superior to the other.
Correction: The author presents them as parallel methods, both yielding certainty.

Checkpoint
According to the passage, how many ways have the theologians followed in proving the Creator?
A) One way
B) Two ways
C) Three ways
D) Four ways

Key Takeaway
The two classical proofs are the proof from temporal origination and the proof from contingency, each regarded as self-evident in its inferential domain.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
The author says both proofs are self-evident. If that is true, what does it say about your doubts when they arise? Are you avoiding the self-evident because it demands a change in your life? Commit to reviewing these proofs each time doubt creeps in.
?
Which of the two proofs—temporal origination or contingency—speaks more directly to your own experience of existence? How does that affect your sense of dependence on God right now, in this moment?
?
If you truly believed that every contingent thing around you, including yourself, needs a preponderator at every instant, would you still take your next breath for granted? What would you do differently today to live in constant acknowledgment of that dependence?

The Innate Proof: The Felt Need for a Manager
The author presents the innate proof of need and its superiority over rational proofs.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why the author considers the innate proof from human need to be clearer and more immediate than the rational proofs.

Core Idea
Your own need for God is more obvious than any external proof.

After presenting the two standard proofs, the author argues that the innate human disposition (fiṭra) testifies to a need for a Manager (mudabbir) in a way that is more immediate than any rational inference. He supports this with Quranic verses and a hadith, and explains how introspection leads to direct knowledge of God.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage
Read the author's declaration and his justification for preferring the innate proof over the two rational proofs.
2
Understand the superiority claim
The author says one's own need is clearer than the need of external contingent things. This is a psychological argument.
He uses the phrase 'one's own need is clearer to him'.
3
Examine the Quranic support
The author quotes verses that appeal to human needs like answering distress, providing sustenance, etc., to show that the Quran itself uses this method.
These verses are meant to awaken the innate recognition.
4
Reflect on the introspection method
The author describes a process: returning to oneself, recognizing need, then observing signs in the universe, and finally calling God to witness over the dominion.
This leads to the statement 'I know things by my Lord, not my Lord by things'.

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: One might think that rational proofs are the only way to know God.
Correction: The author argues that innate need is a more immediate and clearer proof.

Checkpoint

A) It requires less reflection.

C) It is based on Scripture.
D) It was taught by the early philosophers.

Key Takeaway
The innate feeling of need for a Manager is a more direct proof of God than any rational argument.

Refuting the Dahriyya on Temporal Origination
The author refutes the Dahriyya claim that pre-eternity entails تعطيل.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the refutation of the Dahriyya claim that the pre-eternity of the world entails God's suspension from action (taʿṭīl) and the parallel argument against making God's action caused (taʿlīl).

Core Idea
God's creation is only meaningful when there is something to create; pre-eternity does not imply idleness.

The Dahriyya, who assert the world's pre-eternity, argue that if God never began creating, He must be inactive—a state they call taʿṭīl (suspension). The author counters by clarifying that creation (ījād) is only conceivable when there is a subject to be created; without such a subject, no suspension occurs. He then draws a powerful parallel: just as taʿṭīl is impossible for God, so is taʿlīl—the notion that God's act is necessitated by an external cause. In this lesson, we unpack this concise argument and its theological stakes.

Guided Path
1
Read the Arabic passage carefully
Read the passage once to get the flow. Notice how the author uses key terms: تعطيل, صنع, إيجاد, تعليل. The passage is dense, so do not rush.
The Arabic ends abruptly; the refutation is complete in its essence.
2
Identify the Dahriyya's claim
The Dahriyya argue that if the world is pre-eternal (qadīm), then God never created it, which they interpret as تعطيل (suspension of action). Mark where this appears.
3
Understand the author's refutation
The refutation has two parts: 1) ījād (bringing into existence) is only actualized where it is conceivable; if there is no subject, there is no taʿṭīl. 2) Just as taʿṭīl is impossible, so is taʿlīl—attributing a cause to God's action. The Dahriyya, by claiming pre-eternity, implicitly commit taʿlīl.
This passage connects to broader kalam principles about divine agency.
4
Reflect on the key terms
Note the contrast between taʿṭīl (suspension) and taʿlīl (attribution of a cause). The author argues that both are impossible for God. Consider how this protects God's absolute power and freedom.
These terms are central to the debate on divine attributes and action.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وأما تعطيل الصانع عن الصنع فقد اذهب وهم الدهرية القائلين بقدم العالم إلى أن الحكم بقدم العالم في الأزل تعطيل الصانع عن الصنع، وقد سبق الرد عليهم بأن الإيجاد قد تحقق حيث تصور الإيجاد وحيث ما لم يتصور لم يكن تعطيلا، وكما أن التعطيل ممتنع ، كذلك التعليل ممتنع ، وأنتم عللتم وجود
As for the suspension (taʿṭīl) of the Creator from creating, the illusion of the Dahriyya, who assert the pre-eternity (qidam) of the world, led them to the view that judging the pre-eternity of the world in eternity entails the suspension of the Creator from creating. And the refutation against them has already preceded: that bringing into existence (ījād) is actualized where bringing into existence is conceivable, and where it is not conceivable, it is not suspension (taʿṭīl). And just as suspension (taʿṭīl) is impossible, likewise attribution of a cause (taʿlīl) is impossible, and you have attributed a cause (ʿallaltum) to existence.
Source pages p. 124

Commentary
This passage is a refutation of the Dahriyya (materialists or eternalists) who claim that the world is pre-eternal (qadīm). They argue that if the world is eternal, then God did not create it at some point, which would mean He is 'suspended' from action (taʿṭīl). The author counters by stating that creation (ījād) is only meaningful when there is a subject to be created; if the world is eternal, then there is no moment of creation, but that does not entail taʿṭīl because the concept of 'suspension' applies only when action is possible but not performed. Since in eternity past the world was not there to be created, there is no suspension. Furthermore, the author draws a parallel: just as taʿṭīl is impossible for God, so too is taʿlīl (attributing a cause to God, meaning that God's act is necessitated by an external cause). The Dahriyya, by claiming the world is eternal, effectively attribute a cause to God's existence or non-action? The text ends abruptly with 'and you have attributed a cause to existence,' presumably accusing the Dahriyya of making God's non-creation have a cause. The argument is that both extremes are impossible.

Key Terms

صنع
دهرية
قدم العالم

تعليل

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: That if God did not create in time, He is inactive or suspended from action (taʿṭīl).
Correction: Suspension applies only where action is possible but not performed; in pre-eternity, there is no subject to create, so no suspension occurs.

Checkpoint
What is the Dahriyya argument that the author refutes?
A) That the world is created in time.
B) That pre-eternity of the world entails تعطيل of the Creator.
C) That God must have a cause for His actions.
D) That the world cannot have a Creator.

Key Takeaway
The pre-eternity of the world does not entail God's suspension from action, and claiming otherwise leads to the equally impossible notion that God's action is caused.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?

The Dahriyya insisted on knowing a 'reason' for God's non-creation in pre-eternity. Are there areas where you demand to know God's 'why' before you trust Him? Would you be willing, right now, to commit to surrendering that demand and resting in His perfect wisdom?
?
If God's action is not subject to cause and effect like human actions, then your own logic about what God 'should' do is fundamentally flawed. What is one belief about God that you hold based on your own reasoning—and how can you intentionally replace it with a truth from revelation starting today?

Knowing God through Self and Creation
The author argues that knowledge of God comes from introspection and observation of creation.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the threefold path to knowledge of God: introspection, observation of signs, and witnessing by God, as presented in the passage.

Core Idea
Knowledge of God starts with self-awareness and ends with witnessing His lordship over all.

This passage presents a spiritual epistemology rooted in the Quran. The author argues that knowing God begins not with external proofs but with a return to oneself, where one realizes total dependence on the Creator. Then, by observing the signs in the universe and within, one testifies to God's sovereignty. This lesson unpacks that journey.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage once
Read the Arabic passage and its translation to get an overall sense of the argument. Notice the Quranic verses embedded in the text.
The passage uses verses from Surah Ta-Ha and Surah Fussilat.
2
Identify the three stages
Look for the sequence: returning to oneself, then looking into the signs of horizons and self, then testifying by God to the kingdom. These are the three steps of the path.
The Arabic verbs 'رجع', 'استبصر', 'استشهد' mark the stages.
3
Understand the conclusion
The passage ends with a firm conclusion: the world cannot be without a Wise, Powerful, Knowing Maker. See how this follows from the prior stages.
4
Reflect on the reversal
Note the statement: 'I came to know things through my Lord, and I did not come to know my Lord through things.' This reverses common thinking. Discuss what it means.
This highlights the author's epistemological priority.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
(الباقون على أصل الفطرة) وما كان له عليهم من سلطان، وقال: (فذكر إن نفعت الذكرى سيذكر من يخشى الأعلى)، وقوله: (فقولا له قولاً ليناً لعله يتذكر أو يخشى) [طه: 44]. ومن رحل إلى الله قربت مسافته حيث رجع إلى نفسه أدنى رجوع فعرف احتياجه إليه في تكوينه وبقائه وتقلبه في أحواله وأنحائه، ثم استبصر في آيات الآفاق إلى آيات الأنفس، ثم استشهد به على الملكوت لا بالملكوت عليه: (أو لم يكف بربك أنه على كل شيء شهيد) [فصلت: 53] عرفت الأشياء بربي، وما عرفت ربي بالأشياء، ومن غرق في بحر المعرفة لم يطمع في شط، ومن تعالى إلى ذروة الحقيقة لم يحط، فثبت بالدلائل والشواهد أن العالم لا يتعطل عن الصانع الحكيم القادر العليم سبحانه وتقدس.
"Those who remain upon the original disposition, and he did not have any authority over them. He said: 'So remind, if the reminder should benefit; he who fears [Allah] will remember.' And His saying: 'And speak to him with gentle speech that perhaps he may be reminded or fear.' [Ta-Ha: 44]. Whoever travels to God, his distance becomes short; when he returns to himself the slightest return, he recognizes his need for Him in his formation, his permanence, and his movement through his states and aspects. Then he looks with insight into the signs of the horizons to the signs of the self. Then he testifies by Him to the kingdom, not by the kingdom to Him: 'Is it not sufficient for your Lord that He is, over all things, a Witness?' [Fussilat: 53]. I came to know things through my Lord, and I did not come to know my Lord through things. Whoever drowns in the sea of knowledge does not desire a shore, and whoever ascends to the peak of reality has not fallen. So it is established by proofs and evidences that the world is not devoid of the Wise, Powerful, Knowing Maker, glory be to Him and exalted."
Source pages p. 124

Commentary
This passage is a reflective argument on the path to knowledge of God. The author begins by citing Quranic verses that affirm that some people remain upon their original disposition (fitra) and that reminders are effective. He then describes a spiritual method: first, by introspection—returning to oneself even slightly reveals one's utter dependence on God for existence and sustenance. Second, by observing the external signs in the universe (آيات الآفاق) and internal signs within oneself (آيات الأنفس). Third, by witnessing God's lordship over the entire kingdom without making the kingdom a means to know Him. The conclusion is that the world necessarily has a Maker who is wise, powerful, and knowing. The author emphasizes that true knowledge comes from God, not from things.

Correction: The passage teaches that knowledge begins with introspection and self-awareness, then moves outward, finally witnessing by God rather than through creation alone.

Refuting the Dahriyya: Divine Action and Eternity
The author rebuts the Dahriyya's claim that the world's eternity implies God's inactivity.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the Dahriyya's argument that the eternity of the world negates divine action, and the author's response that creation is based on conception, not temporality.

Core Idea
Eternal creation does not negate divine action; it depends on conception.

The Dahriyya (materialists) thought that if the world is eternal, God cannot be an agent. In this passage, the author quickly refutes that by explaining that bringing into existence (إيجاد) is realized where it is conceived, not temporally. This preserves God's agency even for an eternal world.

Guided Path
1
Read the Arabic and translation
Read the first part of the passage carefully. Note the structure: first the Dahriyya's claim, then the refutation.
Pay attention to the term إيجاد.
2
Understand the Dahriyya's claim
The Dahriyya assert that judging the world to be eternal in pre-eternity is a negation of the Maker from action. They equate eternity with inactivity.
3
Analyze the author's refutation
The author says that bringing into existence (إيجاد) is realized where it is conceived, and where it is not conceived, it is not a negation. So divine action is not temporal but conceptual.
This refutes the idea that eternity implies inactivity.
4
Reflect on wider implications
Consider how this argument defends divine attributes while acknowledging the world's eternity. The author plants the seed for the next refutation against causation.
This is a nuanced position.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وأما تعطيل الصانع عن الصنع فقد أذهب وهم الدهرية القائلين بقدم العالم إلى أن الحكم بقدم العالم في الأزل تعطيل الصانع عن الصنع، وقد سبق الرد عليهم بأن الإيجاد قد تحقق حيث تصور الإيجاد وحيث ما لم يتصور لم يكن تعطيلا، وكما أن التعطيل ممتنع، كذلك التعليل ممتنع، وأنتم عللتم وجود العالم بوجوده وسميتم معبودكم علة ومبدئا وموجبا، وذلك يؤدي إلى أمرين محالين: أحدهما بثبوت المناسبة بين العلة والمعلول، وقد سبق تقريره، والثاني أن العلة توجب معلولها لذاتها، والقصد الأول وجود العالم من لوازم وجوده بالقصد الأول، فإن العالي لا يريد أمرا لأجل الأسفل، فبطل أيضا التعليل، وهذا من الإلزامات المفحمة التي لا جواب عنها.
"As for the negation of the Maker from action, the illusion of the Dahriyya (materialists) who assert the eternity of the world has led them to [believe] that judging the world to be eternal in pre-eternity is a negation of the Maker from action. The refutation against them has already preceded: that bringing into existence is realized where it is conceived, and where it is not conceived, it is not a negation. Just as negation is impossible, so also causation (ta'lil) is impossible. And you have posited a cause for the existence of the world by its existence and called your worshipped being a cause, a beginner, and a necessitator. That leads to two impossible things: first, the establishment of a relation between cause and effect, which has already been established; second, that the cause necessitates its effect by its essence, and the first intention is that the existence of the world is among the necessary concomitants of its existence by the first intention. Indeed, the higher does not will something for the sake of the lower. Thus causation is also invalidated. This is among the compelling constraints for which there is no answer."
Source pages p. 124

Commentary
This passage is a refutation of the Dahriyya (materialists) who argue that the eternity of the world implies that God does not act (تعطيل الصانع عن الصنع). The author responds by stating that the concept of creation (إيجاد) is still applicable even if the world is eternal, because it is conceived as dependent on God. He then attacks the alternative view of the philosophers (the 'divine philosophers') who posit a necessary emanation (تعليل). He argues that their view leads to two absurdities: (1) it requires a relation (مناسبة) between cause and effect, which implies composition in God, and (2) it entails that the cause necessitates the effect by essence, making the world a necessary concomitant of God, which contradicts God's free will and superiority over creation. The author concludes that this is an irrefutable refutation.

Key Terms
تعطيل
الدهرية
إيجاد

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: That an eternal world necessarily means God is not an agent.
Correction: The author clarifies that creation is realized when conceived, regardless of temporal priority.

Checkpoint
What is the Dahriyya's main claim according to the passage?
A) The world is eternal, so God must be eternal as well.
B) The world is eternal, which negates God's power.
C) Judging the world to be eternal in pre-eternity is a negation of the Maker from action.
D) The world is created in time by a timeless God.

Key Takeaway
Divine action is not negated by the world's eternity; it is based on conception, not temporal origination.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Have you ever felt that because your circumstances haven't changed for a long time, God is no longer active in your life? How does the author's point that divine action is based on conception, not time, challenge you to reconsider your moments of stagnation? What would it mean for you to 'conceive' of God's creative power right now, in your current situation?
?
If God's action is not tied to temporal beginnings, then why do you often think that your past mistakes make you beyond His mercy? Could it be that your conception of His mercy is limited by your sense of time? Will you today take a moment to conceive of His forgiveness as real, here and now, regardless of how long ago your sin was?

Refuting the Philosophers' Causation (Ta'lil)
The author refutes the philosophers' view that God is a necessary cause (علة) for the world.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the author's refutation of the philosophers' view of God as a necessary cause, focusing on the two impossible consequences.

Core Idea
The view that God is a necessary cause leads to impossible consequences about relation and necessitation.

After refuting the Dahriyya, the author turns to the philosophers who claim God is a cause (علة) that necessitates the world. He argues that this causation (تعليل) is impossible because it leads to two absurdities: positing a relation between cause and effect, and necessitation by essence which makes the world a necessary concomitant and denies God's free will.

Guided Path
1
Read the second part of the passage
Focus on the section starting with 'وكما أن التعطيل ممتنع' to the end. Understand the two impossible things listed.
The author uses a strong rhetorical style.
2
Identify the two impossibilities
The first is establishing a relation (مناسبة) between cause and effect, implying composition. The second is that the cause necessitates its effect by essence, making the world a necessary concomitant.
These are the core of the refutation.
3
Understand why these are impossible
The first implies composition in God, which is inconsistent with divine simplicity. The second denies God's free will and superiority, as the higher does not will for the lower.
4
Appreciate the force of the refutation
The author concludes that this is a compelling constraint with no answer, showing the strength of his argument against causation.
This is a key point in Islamic theology against necessary emanation.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books

"As for the negation of the Maker from action, the illusion of the Dahriyya (materialists) who assert the eternity of the world has led them to [believe] that judging the world to be eternal in pre-eternity is a negation of the Maker from action. A) It denies God's oneness.

Key Takeaway

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?

Contradiction on Wujūd and Wujūb
Al-Shahrastani presents the first contradiction: the philosophers add wujūb to wujūd.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how al-Shahrastani uses the argument of contradiction (ilzām) to show that the philosophers inadvertently admit distinct meanings, similar to attributes.

Core Idea
Adding necessity to existence reveals a hidden attribute.

Al-Shahrastani targets the philosophers who deny real attributes but still use terms like wujūd for both God and creatures. He points out that when they distinguish between necessary and contingent existence, they are adding a meaning (wujūb) that is not part of wujūd itself. This forces them to admit a real distinction, undermining their own position.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without trying to analyse it. Then read the translation. Let the author's own argumentative flow come through.
Notice the technical terms: wujūd, wujūb, jawāz, ishtirāk, tashkīk.
2
Identify the philosophers' two moves
First, they apply 'wujūd' to both the Necessary by Itself and the necessary through another via ishtirāk (homonymy). Then, they specify one existence as wujūb and the other as jawāz.
3
Understand the contradiction
Al-Shahrastani argues that wujūb is not part of the meaning of wujūd, so adding wujūb introduces a separate meaning. This contradicts the denial of attributes because it implies a distinction in the intellect.
Think of the analogy: 'accidentality' and 'color-ness' are distinct even if united in a single substance.
4
Examine and reject the tashkīk defense
The philosophers try to escape by saying wujūb is applied by gradation (tashkīk), but al-Shahrastani shows that the terms 'more deserving' (awlā) and 'prior' (awwal) are themselves additional differentiae (fuṣūl), which only confirms the multiplicity.
5
Reflect on the implication
Consider how this argument supports the Ashʿarī position on attributes: even the philosophers, who deny attributes, end up admitting distinct considerations (iʿtibārāt) that function like attributes.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
منها أنهم أطلقوا لفظ الوجود على الواجب بذاته، وعلى الواجب لغيره شمولا وعموما على سبيل الاشتراك، ثم خصصوا أحد الوجودين الوجوب والثاني بالجواز ومن المعلوم أن الوجوب لم يدخل في معنى الوجود حتى يتصور الوجود، فهذا إذا معنى آخر وراء الوجود، والمعنيان إن اتحدا في الخارج من الذهن فقد تمايزا في العقل مثل العرضية واللونية، وليس يغني عن هذا الإلزام قولهم إطلاق لفظ الوجوب على الواجب بذاته والجائز لذاته بالتشكيك أي هو في أحدهما أولى وأول، وفي الثاني لا أولى ولا أول إذ الأولى والأول فصل آخر تميز به أحد الوجودين عن الثاني، وذلك يؤكد الإلزام ولا يدفعه.
Among them is that they applied the term 'existence' (wujūd) to the Necessary by Itself (al-wājib bi-dhātih) and to the necessary through another (al-wājib li-ghayrih) by inclusion and generality in the manner of homonymy (ishtirāk). Then they specified one of the two existences with necessity (wujūb) and the second with contingency (jawāz). Yet it is known that necessity does not enter into the meaning of existence such that existence would be conceived. So this is then another meaning beyond existence. And if the two meanings are united in external reality from the mind, they are distinguished in the intellect, like 'accidentality' and 'color-ness'. And their saying is of no avail against this imposition: 'The application of the term necessity to the Necessary by Itself and the contingent by itself is by gradation (tashkīk), i.e., it is in one of them more deserving (awlā) and prior (awwal), and in the second not more deserving nor prior' — for 'more deserving' and 'prior' are another differentia (faṣl) by which one of the two existences is distinguished from the second, and that confirms the imposition and does not repel it.
Source pages p. 126

Commentary
This passage is an argument (ilzām) against the philosophers who deny real attributes but use terms like wujūd for both God and creatures. The author points out a contradiction: they apply wujūd universally via ishtirāk (homonymy), yet then differentiate by adding wujūb or jawāz. But wujūb is not part of the meaning of wujūd, so they admit an extra meaning. If the two meanings are united externally but distinct in the mind (like 'accidentality' and 'colorness'), then they have a real distinction. Their recourse to tashkīk (gradation) does not help because the gradation terms (awlā and awwal) themselves introduce a new differentia, which confirms rather than removes the multiplicity. Thus the philosophers are forced to admit distinct considerations (i'tibārāt), which is exactly what the mutakallimūn do with attributes.

Key Terms

الوجوب
الجواز
الاشتراك

الإلزام
فصل

Misconception: Some may think that tashkīk resolves the issue by making wujūb a mode of wujūd, but the author shows that the gradation terms are themselves additional differentiae.
Correction: Tashkīk does not remove the multiplicity; the terms 'more deserving' and 'prior' are new distinct meanings, reinforcing the original argument.

Checkpoint
According to al-Shahrastani, what is the problem with the philosophers' use of 'wujūd' and 'wujūb'?
A) They use them synonymously.
B) They add wujūb as a separate meaning beyond wujūd, contradicting their denial of attributes.
C) They claim wujūd is univocal.
D) They deny wujūb altogether.

Key Takeaway
Al-Shahrastani's ilzām forces the philosophers to admit that wujūb is a meaning distinct from wujūd, which aligns with the Ashʿarī view of attributes as distinct concepts.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you reflect on your own use of the word 'existence' for both Allah and yourself, do you unconsciously add a separateness in your heart? Do you truly feel the absolute difference in necessity, or do you treat His existence as ‘greater’ in a merely quantitative way? This lesson forces you to ask: is your tawḥīd merely verbal, or does it penetrate your understanding of who He is versus who you are?
?
The philosophers tried to avoid adding attributes by using gradation, but the author showed that even their terms of comparison (‘more deserving’, ‘prior’) are hidden differentiae. What ‘comforting’ or ‘softening’ terms do you use about Allah to avoid the stark reality of His absolute uniqueness? Have you ever considered that phrases like ‘Allah is closer to us than our jugular vein’ might be misused to diminish His transcendence? This lesson calls you to examine your own speech: do your words uphold His incomparability, or do they smuggle in hidden comparisons?
?
Al-Shahrastani’s argument is an ilzām—a logical obligation to admit something you resist. He is not just proving a point; he is forcing an opponent to own the consequences of their premises. Is there an area of your faith where you resist the logical implications of what you claim to believe? Are you willing to follow the truth even when it dismantles a comfortable position? This lesson offers a mirror: will you accept the full weight of Islamic theology, or will you hide behind convenient ambiguities?

The Nufat's Counter-Argument: Existence and the Dilemma
Nufat turn the argument against muthbitun regarding existence and spatial attributes.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how the nufat turn the argument against the muthbitun by creating a dilemma over the status of existence in atoms.

Core Idea
The nufat trap the muthbitun: either existence in atoms implies the world's eternity or leads to absurdity.

This passage presents the final move by the nufat (deniers) in their debate with the muthbitun (affirmers of attributes). They turn the very argument used against them back on their opponents, focusing on existence and its dependent attributes like spatial occupation and receptivity to accidents. The nufat force a dilemma that undermines the muthbitun's position.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage and its translation. Notice how the nufat list the attributes they claim are dependent on existence according to the muthbitun.
The attributes include spatial occupation, receptivity to accidents, and self-subsistence.
2
Identify the two options of the dilemma
The nufat ask: is existence negated from atoms or is it fixed? Find the two branches and their consequences.
The options are 'thabit' (fixed) and 'manfi' (negated).
3
Analyze the theological outcomes
Think about what each branch implies. If existence is fixed, that would mean the world is eternal and uncreated. If negated, the muthbitun's own principle leads to a contradiction.
This shows the nufat are skilled dialecticians.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
على أنا نلزمكم الوجود نفسه والصفات التابعة للوجود عندكم مثل: التحيز للجوهر وقبوله للعرض وقيامه بالذات، ومثل احتياج المعرض إلى الجوهر وقيامه به، فنقول: الوجود منفي من الجواهر أم ثابت؟ فإن كان ثابتا فهو تصريح بقدر العالم، وتقرير أن لا إبداع ولا اختراع، ولا أثر لقادرية الباري سبحانه وتعالى أصلا، وإن كان منفيا، فكل منفي عندكم معدوم، أوكل معدوم ثابت، فعاد الإلزام عليكم جذعا.
Moreover, we oblige you concerning existence itself and the attributes dependent on existence according to you, such as: occupying space for the atom, its receptivity to accidents, its self-subsistence, and like the need of the accident for the atom and its inherence in it. So we say: Is existence negated from atoms or is it fixed? If it is fixed, then that is a declaration of the pre-eternity of the world, and an affirmation that there is no origination nor creation, and no effect for the power of the Creator, glorified and exalted, at all. And if it is negated, then every negated thing is according to you a non-existent, or rather every non-existent is fixed, so the obligation returns to you completely.
Source pages p. 152

Commentary
In this final passage, the nufat turn the tables on the muthbitun. They consider existence itself and its attendant attributes (like spatial occupation for atoms, receptivity to accidents, self-subsistence). They ask: is existence negated from atoms or is it fixed? If the muthbitun say existence is fixed in atoms, that would imply the world is eternal and uncreated—a clear theological error. If they say existence is negated from atoms, then according to their own principle (that every negated thing is non-existent, and every non-existent is fixed), the obligation (ilzam) rebounds upon them; that is, they would have to affirm that existence, though negated, is fixed—which is absurd. Thus the nufat expose a dilemma in the muthbitun's position.

Key Terms
وجود
تحيز
جوهر
عرض
إبداع
اختراع
قادرية

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The nufat are denying the existence of atoms or attributes altogether.
Correction: They are not denying existence; they are showing that the muthbitun's stance leads to an unacceptable dilemma.

Checkpoint
What conclusion do the nufat draw if existence is said to be fixed in atoms?
A) The world is eternal and uncreated.
B) The world is created ex nihilo.
C) Atoms are non-existent.
D) Existence is negated.

Key Takeaway
The nufat's argument forces the muthbitun to either accept the world's eternity or face a logical contradiction, thus undermining their position.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you hold a belief about the nature of existence, are you willing to follow it to its logical conclusions even if they contradict core Islamic truths? What if one of your own assumptions, if pursued consistently, would lead to denying Allah's power—would you abandon that assumption, or cling to it and reinterpret the Qur'an?
?
The Nufat's argument traps the muthbitun in a contradiction. Is there an area in your own life where you hold two incompatible beliefs—one from revelation and one from your own reasoning—without resolving them? How long will you allow that tension to persist without seeking the truth from the Qur'an and Sunnah?
?
The passage warns that if existence is fixed in atoms, there is 'no effect for the power of the Creator.' Do you live as though Allah's power is active in every moment, or have you unconsciously attributed stability and permanence to the world around you, as if it exists independently of His continuous creation? What would change if you truly believed that at every instant, existence itself is a gift from Allah?

The General Does Not Necessitate the Specific
Establishes that existence as a general attribute does not entail specific quiddities.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why the existence of a general attribute like existence does not imply the existence of a specific nature, and that specific quiddities require additional determination from the Creator.

Core Idea
Existence alone does not make a thing what it is.

This lesson focuses on a key principle in kalam: the most general attribute of all existents is existence itself. But bringing the general into existence does not automatically bring the specific. The example of whiteness and blackness illustrates that the same existence could have been for any specific quality. The author argues that the specific must be attributed to the Originator beyond mere existence.

Guided Path
1
Read the Arabic passage and translation
Read the passage once to get the overall argument. Notice the key claim about existence being the most general attribute.
The passage uses the example of whiteness and blackness.
2
Identify the key principle
Find the sentence: 'إيجاد الأعم لا يوجب وجود الأخص'. This is the core principle.
The author uses 'أيضا' to introduce this as an additional point.
3
Understand the example
Trace how the author uses whiteness to show that if it were only related to existence, it could have been blackness instead. This shows that existence does not specify.
The example illustrates that the specific nature is not necessitated by general existence.
4
Synthesize the conclusion
The author concludes that from the necessity of the specific, the general existence is appropriate but not sufficient. Therefore, the specific must be willed and known by the Creator.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
هو عام لا يتحقق له وجود، بل وجود ممكن هو بصفة كذا أو كذا، ويتحقق له وجود فيثبت أن المعين المشار إليه هو المفتقر إلى الموجد، لكن لا يتحقق له وجود إلا ويريده الموجد، وليس يريده الموجد إلا ويعلمه قبل إيجاده فيتخصص وجوده وجودًا عرضًا جوهرًا في علم الموجد، فالمعلوم يتخصص مرادا، والمراد يتخصص وجودًا هو بعينه جوهر إلا أنه في ذاته يكون شيئًا فيخصصه الوجود بعد الشيء، حتى تتخصص الشيئية الخارجة جوهرًا، وتتخصص الجوهرية العامة الخارجة بهذا الجوهر. وأيضا فإن الوجود أعم صفات الموجودات، وإيجاد الأعم لا يوجب وجود الأخص، فلو كان البياض مثلا منتسباً إلى الموجد من حيث وجوده فقط لكان يكون موجودًا لا بياضًا، بل لو عكس الأمر وقلب الحال فقبل أوجده سوادًا أو بياضا. وانتسبت البياضية إلى الموجد فقط، ولكن من ضرورة وجود الأخص وجود الأعم كان أمثل من حيث العقل، ولذلك نقول: إن البياض يضاد السواد ببياضيته، فإذا انتفى السوادية انتفى الوجود؛ إذ ليس من قضية العقل انتفاء السوادية وبقاء الوجود، وعند القوم أن المعدوم عادم لوجوده، كما أن الموجود واجد لوجوده، فيلزم على ذلك أن لا يوجد بياض ولا يعدم سواد، ولا يتحقق جوهر ولا يتخصص عرض
It is general, its existence is not realized; rather a possible existence is of such and such a quality, and its existence is realized so it is established that the specific individual referred to is the one in need of the Originator. But its existence is not realized except that the Originator wills it, and the Originator does not will it except that He knows it before His bringing it into existence, so its existence becomes specified as an existence of an accident or substance in the knowledge of the Originator. Thus the known becomes specified as willed, and the willed becomes specified as an existence which is itself a substance, except that in its essence it is a thing, so existence specifies it after the thing, until the thingness external to the substance becomes specified, and the general substantiality external to this substance becomes specified. Also, existence is the most general attribute of existents, and bringing into existence the general does not necessitate the existence of the specific. For if whiteness, for example, were related to the Originator only from the aspect of its existence, it would be existent but not whiteness. Rather, if the matter were reversed and the situation inverted, He could have brought it into existence as blackness or whiteness. And whiteness would be attributed to the Originator alone; but from the necessity of the existence of the specific, the existence of the general is more appropriate from the perspective of reason. Therefore we say: whiteness is opposed to blackness by its whiteness. So if blackness is negated, existence is negated, for it is not a requirement of reason that blackness be negated while existence remains. According to the [opposing] group, the non-existent is devoid of its existence, just as the existent possesses its existence. Consequently, it would follow that whiteness would not come into existence, blackness would not cease to exist, no substance would be realized, and no accident would become specified.
Source pages p. 160

Commentary
This passage is a complex argument about existence and specificity. It begins by stating that a general concept (likely existence as such) does not itself have a realized existence; rather, it is possible existents that are qualified by specific attributes. The specific individual, when realized, demonstrates its need for an Originator. The Originator's will and knowledge precede existence, and in His knowledge, the existence becomes specified as an accident or substance. The passage then introduces a key principle: existence is the most general attribute of all existents, but bringing the general into existence does not automatically bring the specific. The example of whiteness illustrates this: if whiteness were only related to the Originator by its existence, it would be existent but not specifically whiteness—it could have been blackness. Therefore, the specific nature (e.g., whiteness) must be attributed to the Originator beyond mere existence. The author then criticizes an opposing view (likely the Mu'tazila or a group that denies the reality of attributes in the state of non-existence) that holds the non-existent is devoid of existence. If that were true, then negating blackness would negate existence itself, leading to the absurd consequence that no change—no coming into being of whiteness, no passing away of blackness, no realization of a substance, and no specification of an accident—would occur. The argument thus defends the reality of specific attributes and the coherence of change.

Key Terms
الوجود (al-wujud)
الأعم (al-a‘amm)
الأخص (al-akhass)
البياض (al-bayad)
السواد (al-sawad)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Many assume that if a general attribute like existence is present, the specific nature is automatically determined.
Correction: Existence alone does not specify what a thing is; the specific quiddity requires an additional determination from the Creator.

Checkpoint
What does the author illustrate with the example of whiteness?
A) Whiteness is the only possible existence.
B) Existence alone does not necessitate whiteness; it could have been blackness.
C) Whiteness is impossible without existence.
D) Blackness is superior to whiteness.

Key Takeaway
Existence alone does not make a thing what it is; specific qualities require separate determination.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Have you ever assumed that simply existing as a Muslim guarantees specific virtues like patience or gratitude? This lesson challenges that: general existence does not determine specific qualities. Examine one area where you have relied on your generic identity rather than actively cultivating the specific trait. What concrete step will you take today to work on that virtue?
?
The author argues that whiteness could have been blackness; only the Originator determined its specific nature. In your life, what character trait have you left undefined, expecting it to appear automatically? What would it mean for you to consciously seek Allah’s guidance to shape that specific quality?
?
The passage states that negating blackness negates existence. Think of a negative quality you have allowed to define you. By holding onto it, are you in effect negating your very purpose as a believer? What specific quality must you replace it with, and what small action will you take to start that transformation tomorrow?

The Consequence of Denying Existence to the Non-Existent
Critiques the view that non-existents lack existence by showing it entails absurd consequences.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the absurd consequences that follow from the view that the non-existent is completely devoid of existence, and how this view undermines the possibility of change and specification.

Core Idea
Denying existence to non-existents makes change impossible.

This lesson examines the critique of a specific theological position—likely that of the Mu'tazila—which holds that the non-existent (al-ma'dum) is wholly lacking in existence. The author argues that if this were true, then negating a specific quality like blackness would also negate existence itself, leading to the absurd conclusion that no substance or accident could ever be realized or changed.

Guided Path
1
Read the Arabic and translation for the critique
Focus on the part starting with 'وعند القوم أن المعدوم عادم لوجوده'. Understand the opposing view.
This is the conclusion of the author's argument.
2
Identify the absurd consequence
The author shows that if the non-existent lacks existence, then when blackness is negated, existence itself is negated, meaning whiteness could never come to be.
This leads to the impossibility of change.
3
Recognize the four outcomes
The author lists four things that would not occur: whiteness would not exist, blackness would not cease, no substance realized, no accident specified. This is a reductio ad absurdum.
The four outcomes show the complete stasis.
4
Relate to the broader argument
This critique reinforces the earlier principle that specific attributes have reality even before existence, known in the divine knowledge.
The non-existent has a determinate reality in knowledge.

Source Text

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Have you ever assumed that something could come from absolute nothing—like a new habit or a change in your character—without recognizing that God already knows it and gives it reality? How does this lesson challenge your view of personal transformation?
?
Because change requires that what does not yet exist has some reality in God's knowledge, do you live as if your future actions are already known and thus meaningful? Will you start your next decision with the awareness that God knows it before you do it?
?
This argument shows that denying reality to the non-existent leads to absurdity. Is there any area of your life where you treat the future as if it is pure nothing, and thus feel no responsibility toward it? How can you change that today?

Plato's Proof for Universal Forms and the Interdependence of Matter and Form
Plato's proof for universal forms and the interdependence of matter and form.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand Plato's argument for universal forms from intellectual conception and the necessary interdependence of matter and form, culminating in the necessity of a bestower of form.

Core Idea
Matter cannot stand alone; form comes from a transcendent bestower.

Plato's proof for universal forms is a classic argument in philosophy. In this passage, the author presents Plato's reasoning that what is conceived in the intellect must also exist externally. He then clarifies that matter and form are interdependent, and that form ultimately requires a bestower. This establishes a causal chain pointing to a transcendent source.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic and translation once without breaking it down. Get an overall sense of the two parts: Plato's claim and the proponents' refinement.
Notice the shift from 'he said' to 'the proponents of forms said'.
2
Understand Plato's proof
Plato argues that universal concepts in the intellect correspond to real, external entities. Identify the example of celestial intellects as such universals.
3
Grasp the matter-form interdependence
The proponents explain that potentiality (imkan) requires matter, and matter always has some form. Ask yourself: why can't bare matter receive action from an agent?
The text says bare matter has no location, position, or place.
4
Follow the causal chain
Trace the chain: hyle subsists through form, form subsists through its bestower. See how this leads to a transcendent cause.
The chain ends with 'wahib al-sura', the bestower of form.
5
Check your understanding
Answer the checkpoint question to confirm you grasped the central relationship.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وهذا دليل أفلاطن ألا هي في حدوث العالم بصورته وهيولاه، قال: وكما تصور وجود موجودات كلية في العقل تصور وجود موجودات كلية في الخارج عن العقل، وهي حقائق مختلفة تختلف بالخواص كالعقول السماوية.
وقال أصحاب الصور هذا البرهان صحيح في الموجودات الزمانية، لكن كما لا يتحقق إمكان إلا في مادة لا تتصور مادة إلا متصورة بصورة، فإنها إن كانت عرية عن الصور كلها كان لا يتحقق نحوها قصد الفاعل حتى يفيض عليها الصور ; إذ لا حيث لها ولا وضع ولا أين وكما أن الإمكان ليس شيئًا متقوما إلا بالهيولي، والهيولي ليس شيئًا متقوماً إلا بالصورة، فيقوم الهيولي بالصورة، وتقوم الصورة بواهب الصورة، وليس تنفك إحداهما عن الثانية بحال أصلا.
This is Plato's proof: Indeed, it is in the temporal origination of the world with its form (sura) and its matter (hayula). He said: Just as the existence of universal existents is conceived in the intellect, so the existence of universal existents is conceived outside the intellect, and they are different realities differing by properties, like the celestial intellects. The proponents of forms said: This proof is valid for temporal existents, but just as potentiality (imkan) is not actualized except in a matter, and matter is not conceived except as being formed by a form, then if it were stripped of all forms, the intention of the agent would not be actualized towards it until he bestows forms upon it; for it has no location, no position, and no place. And just as potentiality is not a self-subsistent thing except through hyle, and hyle is not self-subsistent except through form, so hyle subsists through form, and form subsists through the bestower of form (wahib al-sura), and one of them is never separated from the other at all.
Source pages p. 167

Commentary
This passage presents Plato's proof for the existence of universal forms and elaborates on the relationship between matter and form. It begins by stating Plato's argument: just as universal existents are conceived in the intellect, they also exist externally as distinct realities with specific properties, like celestial intellects. Then the proponents of forms clarify that this proof holds for temporal beings, but they emphasize that potentiality (imkan) can only be realized in matter, and matter is always conceived with some form. If matter were entirely devoid of form, the agent (al-fa'il) would have no intention towards it because it would lack location, position, or place. The passage then explains the mutual dependence: potentiality is not self-subsistent except through hyle, and hyle is not self-subsistent except through form. Thus hyle subsists through form, and form subsists through the bestower of form. The conclusion is that hyle and form are never separated from each other under any circumstance. The argument establishes that form is necessary for the actuality of matter, and that form ultimately comes from a giver, pointing to a transcendent cause.

Key Terms
هيولي
صورة
واهب الصورة
إمكان

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Matter can exist independently without any form.
Correction: Matter is always formed; it cannot be conceived without some form, and form requires a bestower.

Checkpoint
According to the proponents of forms, what is the relationship between matter and form?
A) Matter can exist without form.
B) Form is impossible without matter.
C) Matter and form are interdependent and form comes from a bestower.
D) Matter is eternal and form is accidental.

Key Takeaway
Plato's proof and the subsequent analysis show that matter and form are inseparable, and that form ultimately points to a transcendent bestower.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you observe the physical world, do you see it as self-sufficient or as constantly receiving form and existence from a higher source? How would your daily actions change if you truly internalized that everything around you—including your own body—is dependent on a Bestower of form?
?
The passage says matter without form has no location or position, so an agent cannot direct intention to it. Think of a time you felt 'formless'—without purpose or direction. Could it be that you were not truly 'bare' but rather missing the form that only the Bestower can give? What would it mean for you to actively seek that form?
?
If form subsists through the Bestower, then your very identity and essence are held in existence by Him. Is there an area of your life where you act as if you are the 'bestower' of your own identity? Will you surrender that role today and acknowledge your dependence?
