One Becoming Two: The Need for Form and the Infinite Regress
Sanusi analyzes the first scenario of unity becoming plurality.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how the scenario of one becoming two demonstrates that hyle cannot change without form, and that attempting to explain change without a common matter leads to infinite regress.

Core Idea
Change requires form, and avoiding an infinite regress forces us to accept a common substrate.

This lesson examines the first half of the second proof from the text. We suppose hyle is existent and ask: if it is one and becomes two, how does that happen? The two possibilities—by addition or by self-multiplication—both lead to the conclusion that form is involved and that a common matter is necessary, or else there is an infinite regress. This lays the groundwork for the broader point that matter never exists without form.

Guided Path
1
Read the Arabic passage
Read the passage from 'برهان آخر' to 'ويتسلسل'. Notice the structure: first a supposition, then two branches, each analyzed.
The passage is compact; take your time.
2
Identify the two branches
Under the assumption that hyle is one and becomes two, note the two sub-possibilities: by external addition or by self-multiplication. See how each is argued to involve form.
The text uses 'فإما أن يقال : هي واحدة أو كثيرة' as the initial division.
3
Understand the infinite regress argument
In the self-multiplication branch, the text shows that if the same thing has unity and multiplicity at different times, it requires a common matter that receives both, leading to a regress. Grasp why this is problematic.
This is the key logical move: positing a common matter leads to an infinite chain.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
برهان آخر لو قدرنا الهيولي موجودة متقومة، فإما أن يقال : هي واحدة أو كثيرة، فإن كانت واحدة ثم صارت اثنين، أفبانضمام آخر إليه أم بتكثير ذلك الواحد في نفسه من غير انضمام من خارج؟ فإن قدر الأول فهما جوهران انضم أحدهما إلى الثاني، ويكون الهيولي اثنين وما تحقق فيه الاثنينية قبل القسمة إما بالانفصال إذا كانا متصلين وإما بالعدد إذا كان مقدارين وكل ذلك صورة، وإن تكثر الواحد في نفسه من غير انضمام من خارج كان حين كان واحدا لم يقبل القسمة ثم صار قابلا للقسمة فتكون له صورة الوحدة تارة، وصورة التكثر تارة، فيكون ذا صورتين، ويلزم أن يكون بين الحالتين مادة مشتركة بها يقبل الوحدة تارة والكثرة أخرى، فيكون للمادة مادة ويتسلسل.
وكذلك لو فرضنا اثنين جوهرين ثم خلعنا صورة الاثنينية فصار شيئًا واحدا، فلا يخلو إما أن يتحدا وكل واحد منهما موجود، أو أحدهما موجود والآخر معدوم، أو كلاهما معدومان، وحصل ثالث بالإيجاد، فإن كان فهما إذا اثنان لا واحد، وإن اتحدا وأحدهما معدوم والآخر موجود فالمعدوم كيف يتحد بالموجود؟ وإن عدما بالإيجاد حصل ثالث فهما غير متحدين بل لا بد من معدومين، ويلزم أيضا في خلع صورة الاثنينية مادة مشتركة، كما لزم في لباس صورتها مادة مشتركة، فيتحقق بهذه البراهين أن الهيولي قط لا تعري عن الصورة، بل قوامها بالفعل يكون بالصورة وقوام الصورة من حيث ذاتها لا يكون بها، بل بواهب الصورة، فكانت الهيولي حافظة لها قبول حافظة لها قبولا، وكانت الصورة مقومة لها وجودًا، فالصورة لا تحدث إلا في الهيولي، والهيولي لا تعري عن الصورة، وكل واحد منهما جوهر ; لأن الجسم مركب منهما، والجسم جوهر والبسيطان جوهران والتمييز بينهما بالفعل غير متصور والفصل بينهما فصل بالعقل.
Another proof: If we suppose hyle to be existent and self-subsistent, then it must be said: Is it one or many? If it is one and then becomes two, does that happen by the addition of another to it, or by the multiplication of that one in itself without external addition? If the first is supposed, then they are two substances (jawharan) joined one to the other, and hyle becomes two, and the twoness before division is either by separation if they were connected, or by number if they were quantities, and all of that is form. If the one multiplies in itself without external addition, then while it was one it was not divisible, then it became divisible, so it has the form of unity at one time and the form of multiplicity at another, thus it has two forms, and it follows that there must be between the two states a common matter (mada mushtaraka) by which it accepts unity at one time and multiplicity at another, so matter would have matter, leading to infinite regress (tasalsul). Likewise, if we suppose two substances and then remove the form of twoness so they become one thing, then it must be either that they unite while each of them is existent, or one of them is existent and the other non-existent, or both are non-existent and a third comes into existence by creation. If it is the first, then they are two, not one. If they unite while one is non-existent and the other existent, how can the non-existent unite with the existent? If both become non-existent and a third comes into existence by creation, then they are not united, but rather there must be two non-existents. And likewise, removing the form of twoness also requires a common matter, just as putting on its form required a common matter. Thus, by these proofs it is established that hyle is never stripped of form; rather, its actual subsistence is through form, and the subsistence of form in itself is not through itself but through the bestower of form. So hyle is a preservative of form in terms of receptivity, and form is constitutive of hyle in terms of existence. Therefore, form does not occur except in hyle, and hyle is never stripped of form, and each of them is a substance (jawhar), because the body is composed of them, and the body is a substance, and the two simples are two substances, and the distinction between them in actuality is inconceivable, and the separation between them is conceptual.
Source pages p. 167

Commentary
This passage provides a second proof for the inseparability of hyle (matter) and form (sura) through a logical analysis of unity and plurality. It begins by supposing hyle is existent and self-subsistent. It then examines two scenarios: (1) if hyle is one and becomes two, and (2) if two become one. In the first scenario, if the change from one to two occurs by addition of another, then both are substances and the twoness involves form. If it occurs by self-multiplication, then the same thing would have both unity and multiplicity at different times, requiring a common substrate that receives both, leading to an infinite regress. In the second scenario, if two become one, either both exist, one exists and the other does not, or both cease and a third arises. Each option leads to contradictions unless a common matter is posited. Therefore, the proofs establish that hyle is never without form. The passage concludes that hyle's actual subsistence is through form, and form's subsistence comes from the bestower of form. Hyle is receptive, form is constitutive. They are both substances, and their distinction is conceptual, not actual. This argument reinforces the necessity of form for matter and the dependence of form on an external giver.

Key Terms
هيولي
صورة
جوهر
تسلسل
مادة مشتركة

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Matter can change numerically without any form.
Correction: The proof shows that any change in unity or plurality necessarily involves form, and without a common substrate, we end in infinite regress.

Checkpoint
According to the passage, what is the consequence of the 'self-multiplication' branch?
A) Hyle becomes two simple substances.
B) It leads to an infinite regress of matters.
C) It proves that form is unnecessary.
D) It shows that addition is the only valid possibility.

Key Takeaway
Change from one to two cannot occur without form, and any attempt to avoid form leads to an infinite regress of matters.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
You have experienced moments when your heart was unified in purpose, then became divided by distractions. This lesson shows that such change from unity to multiplicity requires a common substrate. What is that unchanging core within you? If it is your nafs, what does that imply about its need for form? And does this not compel you to reflect on the One who alone is truly One and Changeless? Today, identify one area where your heart is divided and ask Allah to grant you a single-minded devotion.
?
The proof demonstrates that any attempt to change without an underlying substrate leads to infinite regress. In your spiritual life, do you try to change by merely adding external acts without transforming the inner 'matter' of your heart? This lesson forces you to realize that true change requires a common substrate that receives new form. What is that substrate? Is it your heart? Then commit today to purifying that heart so it can receive the form of true iman, knowing that without a stable foundation, your efforts will unravel.

Two Becoming One: The Necessity of a Common Substrate
Sanusi analyzes the second scenario of plurality becoming unity.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how the scenario of two becoming one also requires a common matter and reinforces the inseparability of hyle and form.

Core Idea
Whether merging two into one or splitting one into two, form and a common substrate are unavoidable.

This lesson continues the second proof by examining the reverse scenario: if two substances become one. The author systematically considers three possibilities—both existing, one existing, or both ceasing—and shows that each leads to a contradiction unless a common matter is assumed. This reinforces the conclusion that hyle is never without form and that form itself depends on an external bestower.

Guided Path
1
Read the Arabic passage
Read from 'وكذلك لو فرضنا اثنين جوهرين' to the end. Follow the three possibilities and the final conclusion.
This is a dense but systematic argument.
2
Identify the three possibilities
When two become one, the text lists three options: both exist, one exists, or both cease. Note how each is refuted.
Only the first option is plausible but it fails because then they would remain two.
3
Grasp the conclusion
The passage concludes that hyle is never stripped of form, and that form's subsistence is from a bestower. Understand the implications for causality.
This connects to the broader theological point about dependence on Allah.

From Contingency to Divine Attributes
Imam al-Haramayn's proof from contingency for divine attributes of will, power, knowledge, and life.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how Imam al-Haramayn proves that the preponderator of the contingent world must possess will, power, knowledge, and life.

Core Idea
Contingency demands a preponderator with attributes, not a bare essence.

Imam al-Haramayn presents a rational proof that the world's contingency requires a preponderator that cannot be a bare essence. He then deduces the specific attributes of will, power, knowledge, and life from the nature of specification, effectuation, and perfection. This lesson unpacks his logical steps.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once without analyzing. Then read the literal translation. Get a sense of the overall argument.
Notice the compact style; each sentence carries a logical move.
2
Identify the three possibilities for the preponderator
The passage lists three ways the preponderator can work: by essence alone, by essence with an attribute, or by an attribute beyond the essence. Locate these in the text.
Remember that 'preponderator' (murajjih) means that which tilts the balance from non-existence to existence.
3
Understand why essence alone fails
Imam al-Haramayn argues that the essence alone cannot specify one possible over another because its relation to all possibilities is equal. An attribute is needed to break the symmetry.
This is the key point that forces the preponderator to have an attribute.
4
Follow the deduction of the four attributes
The passage then deduces will as the attribute of specification, power as the attribute of effectuation, knowledge from perfection in the act, and life as necessary for these attributes. Trace each step in the text.
Notice how each attribute is logically connected to the previous one.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وأجاب إمام الحرمين عن سؤال الاستقراء بأن قال: نحن لم نستقر الحال في الشاهد، ولا نحكم على الغائب بحكم الشاهد لكنا نقول: قام الدليل على حدوث العالم واحتياجه من حيث إمكانه إلى مرجح لأحد طرفي الإمكان. والمرجح إما أن يرجح بذاته ولذاته، أو بذاته على صفة، أو بصفة وراء الذات. واستحال الترجيح والتخصيص بالوجود بالذات من حيث هو ذات، بأن الموجب بالذات لا يخصص عن مثل، فإن نسبة الذات إلى هذا المثل كنسبته إلى المثل الثاني، فيجب أن يكون بصفة وراء الذات أو بذات على صفة. وقد ورد التنزيل والشرع بتسمية تلك الصفة التي يتأتى بها التخصيص إرادة، ثم الإرادة تخصص ولا توقع، فالصفة التي يتأتى بها الإيقاع والإيجاد هي القدرة. والفعل إذا اشتمل على إتقان وإحكام وجب بالضرورة كون الصانع عالمًا، والإرادة أيضًا لا تتعلق بشيء إلا بعد أن يكون المريد عالمًا. وهذه الصفات تستدعي في ثبوتها الحياة، فوجب أن يكون الصانع حيًّا.
And Imām al-Ḥaramayn answered the question about induction by saying: We did not induce the state in the seen, nor do we judge the unseen by the judgment of the seen, but we say: Proof has been established for the temporality of the world and its need, in terms of its possibility, for a preponderator (murajjīḥ) toward one of the two sides of possibility. The preponderator is either that it preponderates by its essence and for its essence, or by its essence with an attribute, or by an attribute beyond the essence. Preponderance and specification by existence by the essence, inasmuch as it is essence, is impossible, because that which necessitates by essence does not specify one equal over another; for the relation of the essence to this equal is like its relation to the second equal. So it must be by an attribute beyond the essence or by an essence with an attribute. Revelation and Law have come to name that attribute by which specification is achieved 'will' (irāda). Then, will specifies but does not bring into existence. So the attribute by which bringing into existence and effectuation is achieved is power (qudra). And when an act includes perfection and precision, it is necessarily the case that the Maker is knowing (ʿālim). Also, will does not relate to anything except after the one who wills is knowing. And these attributes, in their establishment, necessitate life (ḥayāt), so it is necessary that the Maker is living (ḥayy).
Source pages p. 172

Commentary
This passage presents Imām al-Ḥaramayn's response to the objection against induction. He begins by clarifying that his method is not based on induction from the seen to the unseen. Instead, he offers a rational proof: the world is temporally originated (ḥādith) and, being contingent (mumkin), requires a preponderator (murajjīḥ) to tip the scales from non-existence to existence. The preponderator must be either: (1) the essence itself by itself, (2) the essence together with an attribute, or (3) an attribute distinct from the essence. He argues that preponderance by the essence alone is impossible because an essence that necessitates equally cannot specify one of two equals; the essence's relation to one possible is identical to its relation to any other possible. Therefore, the preponderator must involve an attribute—either the essence with an attribute or an attribute beyond the essence. Revelation names this attribute of specification 'will' (irāda). Will specifies but does not actually bring into existence; the attribute that effects existence is power (qudra). Furthermore, if the act displays perfection and precision (itqān wa iḥkām), it necessarily requires the Maker to be knowing (ʿālim). Moreover, will cannot relate to anything unless the one who wills already has knowledge (‘ilm). Finally, these attributes (will, power, knowledge) logically entail life (ḥayāt), because an attribute-bearing entity must be living. Thus, the proof culminates in affirming the four attributes: will, power, knowledge, and life. The passage is a tightly reasoned argument from contingency to the necessity of divine attributes.

حدوث
إمكان
مرجح
ذات
صفة
إرادة
قدرة
علم
حياة

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The essence itself can serve as the preponderator for the contingent universe.
Correction: Imam al-Haramayn argues that the essence, by itself, cannot specify one of two equals; the relation of the essence to all possibilities is uniform, so an attribute is needed to break the symmetry.

Checkpoint
Why does Imam al-Haramayn argue that the preponderator (murajjih) cannot be the essence itself?
A) Because the essence is unknown to human reason
B) Because the essence is simple and cannot act
C) Because the essence does not specify one equal over another
D) Because the essence is not a cause

Key Takeaway
The contingency of the world rationally necessitates a preponderator with will, power, knowledge, and life.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
If God were not all‑powerful, all‑knowing, and alive as this proof demonstrates, would your trust in Him during trials be shaken? How does knowing that His attributes are rationally necessary affect the way you turn to Him in your next moment of weakness? Commit to one specific prayer today that acknowledges His power and knowledge.
?
Imam al‑Haramayn argues that God must have will and power — He chooses and then creates. Does your daily life reflect a belief that God actively wills your circumstances? When you face a situation you did not choose, are you able to submit to His will because He is the One who specifies? Write down one area where you struggle to accept His choice and resolve to say 'I accept Your will in this.'
?
The proof shows that every act of perfection points to God’s knowledge. Think of something beautiful or precisely made in your life today — a sunrise, a skill, a relationship. How does this verse come true: 'And you were not able to do it, but Allah did it'? How can you turn that appreciation into an act of worship right now?

The Dilemma of Divine Causality: Per Se or Per Accidens
The author presents a dilemma: God as per se or per accidens cause.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why the author argues that applying 'cause' to God leads to a dilemma: either God is needy or the term is invalid.

Core Idea
Calling God a 'cause' traps you between two impossible alternatives.

The author defines cause and principle, then poses a critical question: Is the Necessary Being a cause of the first intellect per se or per accidens? Each option leads to a contradiction with divine self-sufficiency. This lesson unpacks this dilemma and its consequences.

Guided Path
1
Understand the definition of cause
Read how the author defines 'cause' and 'principle' — something whose existence continues and from which another thing arises.
Note the distinction between per se and per accidens, and proximate vs remote.
2
Examine the dilemma
Identify the two possibilities: God as cause per se or per accidens. Notice the consequences of each: per se implies need; per accidens is logically invalid.
The author argues that a per accidens cause requires a per se cause, so the term fails.
3
Reflect on the conclusion
The author concludes that applying 'cause' or 'principle' to God is invalid. Ask yourself: does this mean God is not a cause at all?
The author's aim is to show that causality language does not apply to God in the usual sense.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فأقول : العلة والمبدأ عندكم يقال على كل ما استمر له وجود في ذاته ثم حصل منه وجود شيء آخر ويقوم به، وقد يكون الشيء علة للشيء بالذات وقد يكون بالعرض، وقد تكون علة قريبة، وقد تكون علة بعيدة، وواجب الوجود لذاته تام الوجود في ذاته وحصل منه العقل الأول أهو علة له بالذات أم بالعرض؟ فإن كان علة له بالذات لا بالعرض فهو مبدأ له بالقصد الأول لا بالقصد الثاني، ولم يكن واجب الوجود غنيًّا مطلقا، بل فقيراً محتاجًا إلى كسب، ولم يكن كاملا مطلقا بذاته لولا معلوله بل كاملا بغيره ناقصاً باعتبار ذاته، كيف وهم يأبون أن يكون المعلول الأول وسائر الموجودات إلا من لوازم تعقله بذاته، فلا يكون توجيه واجب الوجود الأزلي إلا إلى ذاته على سبيل الاستغناء المطلق عن الكل، ووجوه سائر الموجودات إليه على سبيل احتياج الكل إليه، وإن قالوا: هو علة بالعرض لا بالذات مبدأ بالقصد الثاني لا بالقصد الأول، قلنا : فما لم تكن العلة علة لشيء بالذات لا تكون علة لشيء آخر بالعرض، وما لم يكن مبدأ الشيء بالقصد الأول لا يكون مبدأ الشيء بالقصد الثاني، فيلزم ما لزم في الأول فيبطل إطلاق لفظ العلة والمبدأ عليه. ونقول أيضًا كونه مبدأ وعلة لم يدخل في مفهوم كونه واجبًا بذاته، فإن مفهوم أنه علة أمر إضافي ومفهوم أنه واجب بذاته أمر سلبي، وقد تمايز الأمران والمفهومان ويدل كل واحد منهما على غير ما دل عليه الثاني، فمن أين يصح لكم رد المعاني إلى أمر واحد وهو الذات، فتمايز المفهومات والاعتبارات عندكم، وتمايز الأحوال عند أبي هاشم، وتمايز الصفات عند أبي الحسن على وتيرة واحدة، وكلكم يشير إلى مدلولات مختلفة الخواص والحقائق، واعتذاركم أن كثرة اللوازم والسلوب والإضافات لا تقتضي كثرة في صفات الذات، واستشهادكم بالقرب والبعد لا يغني عن هذا الإلزام، فإنا نلزمكم تمايز الوجوه والاعتبارات بين الإضافي والسلبي؛ إذ من المعلوم كونه علة ومبدأ للمعلول الأول أمر أو حال سلبي، وليس يوجب المعلول من حيث إنه انتفى عنه الكثرة، وليس كونه مسلوب الكثرة عنه شيئاً موجباً للمعلول، ولا يلزم وجوده يسلب عنه شيء من حيث يلزم وجوده شيء، ولا من حيث يسلب عنه شيء.
So I say: 'Cause' and 'principle' according to you are said of everything whose existence in itself continues, then from it comes into existence another thing and subsists by it. And a thing may be a cause of a thing per se and may be per accidens, and may be a proximate cause and may be a remote cause. And the Necessary Being in essence, perfect in existence in essence, and from Him came the first intellect — is He a cause of it per se or per accidens? If He is a cause of it per se not per accidens, then He is a principle of it by first intention not by second intention, and the Necessary Being would not be absolutely independent, but poor, needy, seeking gain; and He would not be absolutely perfect in essence without His effect but perfect by another, imperfect in respect of His essence. How, when they refuse that the first effect and all existents be only from the necessities of His intellecting His essence? So the orientation of the eternal Necessary Being is only towards His essence in the way of absolute independence from all, and the faces of all existents towards Him in the way of the need of all for Him. And if they say: He is a cause per accidens, not per se, a principle by second intention not by first intention, we say: If a cause is not a cause of something per se, it is not a cause of another thing per accidens; and if a thing is not a principle of a thing by first intention, it is not a principle of a thing by second intention. So what followed in the first follows, and the application of the term 'cause' and 'principle' to Him is invalid. And we also say: His being a principle and cause is not included in the concept of His being necessary in essence. For the concept of His being a cause is a relative matter, and the concept of His being necessary in essence is a negative matter. The two matters and concepts are distinct; each indicates other than what the second indicates. So how is it valid for you to reduce the meanings to one thing, which is the essence? For the distinctness of concepts and considerations according to you, and the distinctness of states according to Abu Hashim, and the distinctness of attributes according to Abu al-Hasan, are on one pattern. And all of you point to different significations with different properties and realities. And your excuse that the multiplicity of necessities, negations, and relations does not necessitate multiplicity in the attributes of the essence, and your citation of nearness and distance, does not avail against this objection. For we oblige you to distinguish the aspects and considerations between the relative and the negative. For it is known that His being a cause and principle for the first effect is a thing or a negative state, and the effect is not necessitated by the fact that multiplicity is negated of Him, and His being negated of multiplicity is not a thing that necessitates the effect, and His existence does not necessitate that something is negated of Him by virtue of which His existence necessitates something, nor by virtue of which something is negated of Him.
Source pages p. 174

Commentary
This passage is a refutation of an objection concerning God's causality. The author first defines cause and principle, then raises a dilemma: if the Necessary Being is a cause per se of the first intellect, He would not be absolutely self-sufficient; if He is a cause per accidens, the author argues that something not a cause per se cannot be a cause per accidens, so the term 'cause' is invalid. Then he presents an additional argument: the concept of 'cause' is relational, while the concept of 'necessary being' is negative; they are distinct. This distinction, he argues, refutes those who reduce all attributes to the essence, whether they be Mu'tazilites (Abu Hashim) or Ash'arites (Abu al-Hasan). The author insists that the multiplicity of relational and negative aspects does not imply multiplicity in the divine essence itself.

علة بالعرض
مبدأ بالقصد الأول
مبدأ بالقصد الثاني
واجب الوجود
معلول أول

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some think that calling God a 'cause' is harmless and does not imply any dependence.
Correction: The author shows that if 'cause' is taken in its usual sense, it either implies need or is logically invalid. Divine causality is sui generis and not captured by ordinary causal language.

Checkpoint
What does the author argue would happen if God were a cause of the first intellect per se?
A) God would be absolutely independent.
B) God would be imperfect and needy.
C) The first intellect would be eternal.
D) The term 'cause' would be valid.

Key Takeaway
The author argues that God cannot be a cause in the ordinary sense without compromising His self-sufficiency, leading to a rejection of causal language applied to God.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?

?
When you think of God as the 'cause' of your existence, do you imagine Him as a cause that depends on having an effect? How would your relationship with Him change if you truly believed He is perfectly self-sufficient regardless of you?
?
The author argues that calling God a 'cause' in the ordinary sense implies imperfection. Is there any area of your life where you act as if God is incomplete without your contribution? What would it mean to genuinely accept His utter independence?

The Conceptual Distinction: Cause vs. Necessary Being
The author argues for conceptual distinction between causality and necessary existence.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why the author insists that the concept of 'cause' is relational and distinct from the negative concept of 'necessary being', and how this refutes reducing attributes to the essence.

Core Idea
Cause is relational; necessary being is negative — they are irreducible concepts.

After dismissing the causal language dilemma, the author presents a deeper argument: the concepts 'cause' and 'necessary being' are fundamentally different. One is relative, the other negative. This distinction challenges both Mu'tazilite and Ash'arite views on divine attributes. Let's see how.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two concepts
Read the passage and find where the author distinguishes 'being a cause' (relative) from 'being necessary in essence' (negative).
Note that 'cause' is an relative matter (amr idafi) and 'necessary' is a negative matter (amr salbi).
2
Understand the refutation
The author argues that your opponents (Abu Hashim, Abu al-Hasan) cannot reduce these distinct concepts to one essence. See how he links this to their theories (states, attributes).
The multiplicity of concepts does not imply multiplicity in the essence — but the concepts themselves are distinct.
3
Explore the counterexample
The author dismisses the excuse that 'multiplicity of necessities... does not necessitate multiplicity' with a counterexample about near and far.
He insists that the relative and negative are fundamentally different, so they cannot be reduced.

The Philosophers' Argument for the Oneness of the Necessary Being
Philosophers divide existence and prove necessary being's oneness

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the philosophers' rational argument for the absolute oneness of God based on the division of existence and the impossibility of multiplicity in the necessary being.

Core Idea
If there were two necessary beings, each would depend on its parts, so neither would be necessary.

The philosophers of Islam employed rational categories to establish fundamental theological truths. Here they begin with a simple division of existence into necessary and possible, and then use a powerful argument to show that the necessary being must be absolutely one. This passage presents the core of their monotheistic proof, using logical composition to rule out any multiplicity in God.

Guided Path
1
Read the Arabic and translation
Read the Arabic passage once without analysis, then read the English translation. Let the author's own rhythm come through before you interpret.
Notice the concise logical style of the philosophers.
2
Identify the three categories
Find the three divisions of existence: necessary by itself, possible by itself, and necessary by another. Understand how they are defined.
The third category is a subset of possible existence.
3
Follow the reductio argument
Trace the argument: assume two necessary beings → they share necessity → each has a differential → composition → parts are prior → not necessary. See how each step leads to contradiction.
The key move is that sharing and differing implies genus and species.
4
Reflect on the conclusion
The necessary being must be one from every aspect—no numerical parts or conceptual multiplicity. Ask yourself: why is this conclusion necessary for Islamic theology?
This argument is foundational for tawhid.

Source Text

قالت الفلاسفة : القسمة الصحيحة العقلية أن الموجود ينقسم إلى واجب بذاته، وإلى ممكن بذاته وواجب بغيره، فالواجب بذاته ليس يتصور إلا واحدا من كل وجه لا كثرة فيه بوجه من الوجوه ؛ لا كثرة أجزاء عددية، ولا كثرة معان عقلية، وإلا فيلزم أن يشترك في وجوب الوجود، وينفصل كل واحد عن صاحبه بفصل يخصه، فحينئذ تتركب ذاته من جنس وفصل ثم تكون الأجزاء مقومات للجملة لا محالة والمقوم أقدم من المقوم، وما يقوم بالشيء لا يكون واجبا
The philosophers said: The sound rational division is that existent (al-mawjud) divides into necessary by itself (wajib bi-dhatihi) and into possible by itself (mumkin bi-dhatihi) and necessary by another (wajib bi-ghayrihi). So the necessary by itself is not conceived except as one from every aspect, no multiplicity in any respect: no multiplicity of numerical parts, nor multiplicity of intelligible meanings. Otherwise there would follow that they share in the necessity of existence, and each one is distinguished from its companion by a differential (fasl) specific to it. Then its essence would be composed of a genus (jins) and a species (fasl), and then the parts would be constituents (muqawwimat) of the whole inevitably, and the constituent is prior to the constituted. And what subsists by something cannot be necessary.
Source pages p. 199

Commentary
This passage presents the philosophers' argument for the oneness of God based on a division of existence into necessary and possible. The necessary by itself must be one from every aspect, with no multiplicity—neither numerical parts nor conceptual meanings. If there were two necessary beings, they would share the attribute of necessity and differ by a differential, making each composed of genus and species. The parts would then be constituents prior to the whole. What depends on constituents cannot be necessary by itself. Therefore, multiplicity implies contingency, so the necessary being must be absolutely one.

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: One might think that two necessary beings could exist, each being necessary in itself but distinct.
Correction: The argument shows that if there were two, they would share the property of being necessary (a genus) and differ by a differential, leading to composition. Anything composed is dependent on its parts, so it cannot be necessary by itself. Thus, multiplicity is impossible.

Checkpoint
According to the philosophers, why must the necessary by itself be one without any multiplicity?
A) Because multiplicity is impossible in existence
B) Because if there were two, they would share necessity and differ by a differential, leading to composition and dependence on parts
C) Because God is unknowable
D) Because the philosophers only believe in one god

Key Takeaway
The necessary being must be absolutely one because any multiplicity would introduce composition and dependence, contradicting its necessity.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
If God is absolutely one with no parts or multiple meanings, how does that challenge the way you sometimes compartmentalize His attributes—for example, thinking He is merciful in some situations and just in others? How can you cultivate a unified vision of God in your worship and daily life? This week, when you recite 'Allahu Ahad,' pause and consider that His mercy and justice are not separate parts but flow from His simple essence.
?
The argument shows that any dependency would contradict God's necessity. Are there aspects of your life where you still depend on created things as if they are necessary for your happiness or security? Identify one such dependency and make a concrete plan to rely more on the Necessary Being alone.
?
If the necessary being cannot have multiple 'meanings,' then your understanding of God—your concept of Him—must be free from any notion of composition. Does your heart sometimes imagine God as having different 'parts' or roles? How can you train your mind to consistently affirm His absolute simplicity in your prayers and reflection?

The Philosophers' Argument for Divine Simplicity
Philosophers present their division and deduce necessary simplicity.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the philosophers' rational argument that the necessary being must be absolutely simple, without any multiplicity of parts or attributes.

Core Idea
Divine simplicity is a logical consequence of necessary existence.

The philosophers begin by dividing existence into the necessary by itself and the possible by another. From this division, they deduce that the necessary being cannot admit any multiplicity—neither of numerical parts nor of intellectual meanings—because that would introduce composition from genus and differentia, which undermines its necessary existence. They then apply this to the theologians' attributes, arguing they are either multiple necessities or dependent entities.

Guided Path
1
Read the philosophers' passage
Read the Arabic passage through once, then the translation. Let the argument unfold without interruption.
Notice how the argument begins with a division of existence.
2
Identify the central premise
The philosophers state that the necessary by itself must be one from every aspect. Mark this as the core premise.
This premise is not argued; it is presented as a necessary truth.
3
Follow the reasoning on multiplicity
Trace what would happen if multiplicity existed: shared necessity and distinguishing differentiae would lead to composition of genus and species, making the parts prior to the whole.
Prior parts would contradict necessary existence because they would be more fundamental.
4
See the application to attributes
The philosophers offer two options for the attributes: either they are necessary themselves (leading to multiple necessary beings) or they depend on the essence (creating composition). Both are unacceptable.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قالت الفلاسفة : القسمة الصحيحة العقلية أن الموجود ينقسم إلى واجب بذاته، وإلى ممكن بذاته وواجب بغيره، فالواجب بذاته ليس يتصور إلا واحدا من كل وجه لا كثرة فيه بوجه من الوجوه؛ لا كثرة أجزاء عددية، ولا كثرة معان عقلية، وإلا فيلزم أن يشترك في وجوب الوجود، وينفصل كل واحد عن صاحبه بفصل يخصه، فحينئذ تتركب ذاته من جنس وفصل ثم تكون الأجزاء مقومات للجملة لا محالة والمقوم أقدم من المقوم، وما يقوم بالشيء لا يكون واجبا بذاته بل بالمقوم، والواجب بذاته لن يتصور إلا واحدا، والمعاني التي أثبتموها؛ إما أن تكون واجبة بذاتها فيلزم أن يكون اثنان واجبي الوجود وذلك محال كما قدمناه، وإما أن لا تكون واجبة بذاتها بل يكون قوامها بالذات فيلزم ما ذكرناه آنفا. قيل لهم قسمة الوجود إلى واجب بذاته وإلى واجب بغيره دليل قاطع على نقض إلزامكم، فإن الوجود من حيث هو وجود قد عم الواجب والجائز، والوجوب من حيث هو وجوب قد خص الواجب، فاشتركا في الأعم وافترقا في الأخص، وما به عم غير ما به خص فتركبت الذات من وجود عام ووجوب خاص، فهو كتركيب الذات من واجب الذات، قد شملت الواجبين ويفصل لكل واحدهما بفصل عن الواجب الآخر.
The philosophers said: The correct rational division is that existence is divided into necessary by itself, and into possible by itself and necessary by another. The necessary by itself cannot be conceived except as one from every aspect, with no multiplicity in any respect—no multiplicity of numerical parts, nor multiplicity of intellectual meanings. Otherwise, it would follow that they share in the necessity of existence, and each one is distinguished from its companion by a differentia specific to it, and then its essence would be composed of genus and differentia, and then the parts would necessarily be constituents of the whole, and the constituent is prior to the constituted, and that which subsists by something else cannot be necessary by itself but rather by the constituent, and the necessary by itself cannot be conceived except as one. The meanings that you (theologians) affirm: either they are necessary by themselves, which would necessitate two necessary existents, which is impossible as we have stated; or they are not necessary by themselves but their subsistence is by the essence, which necessitates what we mentioned earlier. It was said to them: The division of existence into necessary by itself and necessary by another is a decisive proof of the flaw of your objection. For existence qua existence is common to the necessary and the contingent, and necessity qua necessity is specific to the necessary. So they share in the more general and differ in the more specific, and that by which they are common is other than that by which they are specific. So the essence is composed of general existence and specific necessity, and it is like the composition of the essence from the necessary of the essence, which has included the two necessary things and distinguishes each one of them by a differentia from the other necessary.
Source pages p. 199

Commentary
This passage contains two parts: first, the philosophers' argument for divine simplicity, and second, the theologian's rebuttal. The philosophers begin by dividing existence (al-mawjud) into that which is necessary by itself (wajib bi-dhatihi) and that which is possible by itself but necessary by another (mumkin bi-dhatihi wa wajib bi-ghayrihi). They argue that the necessary by itself must be one from every aspect, without any multiplicity of parts or meanings. If there were multiplicity, then there would be shared necessity and distinguishing differentiae, implying composition from genus and differentia, which would undermine necessary existence because the constituent parts would be prior. They then apply this to the attributes (al-ma'ani) affirmed by the theologians: if the attributes are necessary by themselves, there would be multiple necessary beings; if they are not necessary but subsist by the essence, then the essence is composed of them, which is impossible for the necessary being. The theologian responds by turning the philosophers' own division against them: the division of existence into necessary and possible itself implies composition. Existence (al-wujud) is common to both, while necessity (al-wujub) is specific to the necessary. Thus the very concept of a necessary being is composed of a common element (existence) and a specific element (necessity). This parallels the composition the philosophers criticize, so their objection is self-defeating.

وجود
وجوب
تركيب

مقوم

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some may think the philosophers deny that God has any attributes whatsoever, but they only reject attributes as distinct realities subsisting in the essence.
Correction: The philosophers argue that attributes distinct from essence imply composition, but they may accept attributes in a non-distinct sense (e.g., identity). The passage focuses on the theologians' view that attributes are additional meanings.

Checkpoint
According to the philosophers, what would happen if the necessary being had multiplicity?
A) It would have parts but still be necessary.
B) Its essence would be composed of genus and differentia, making it dependent.
C) It would be possible by itself.
D) It would have multiple necessary beings.

Key Takeaway
The philosophers' argument for divine simplicity rests on the claim that any multiplicity in the necessary being would introduce composition through genus and differentia, undermining its necessity.

The Theologian's Rebuttal: A Self-Defeating Division
Theologian rebuts by pointing out composition in the philosophers' own division.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how the theologian shows that the philosophers' own division of existence into necessary and possible implies composition, thereby refuting their objection against attributes.

Core Idea
The philosophers' division of existence itself involves composition of existence and necessity.

The theologian does not directly defend the attributes but instead points out a logical flaw in the philosophers' argument. By analyzing the very division that the philosophers introduced, the theologian demonstrates that the concept of a necessary being is composed of a common element (existence) and a specific element (necessity). Thus, if composition is a problem, the philosophers' own concept stands condemned.

Guided Path
1
Read the theologian's response
Read the Arabic passage carefully, then the translation. Notice the shift from the philosophers' argument to the rebuttal.
The theologian uses the philosophers' own division against them.
2
Identify the shared and specific elements
The theologian points out that existence (al-wujud) is common to both necessary and contingent, while necessity (al-wujub) is specific to the necessary. Mark these two aspects.
3
Understand the composition claim
Because the necessary being is defined by both existence and necessity, its concept is composed of these two distinct aspects. This mirrors the composition the philosophers objected to.
The composition is conceptual, not necessarily ontological.
4
Grasp the self-defeating nature
The philosophers' argument against attributes assumed composition is impossible; but their own division creates composition. Therefore, their objection is inconsistent.
The theologian's point is a logical counter rather than a positive doctrine.

The Philosophers' Objection on Commonality and the Theologian's Rebuttal
The philosophers object that existence is not common equally, and the theologian retorts that the same applies to necessity.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how the theologian uses symmetry to neutralize the philosophers' argument that existence is not a common genus.

Core Idea
What applies to existence applies equally to necessity — the objection cuts both ways.

The philosophers refine their attack: existence cannot be a genus because it does not apply equally to God and creation. The theologian brilliantly turns the tables by showing that the same logic applies to necessity, which is the foundation of their own argument. This passage demonstrates a classic kalam strategy of symmetrical rebuttal.

Guided Path
1
Read the philosophers' objection
Read the first part of the passage where the philosophers argue that existence does not encompass the two divisions equally, so it cannot be a constituent part or a genus.
Notice the terms 'primary and prior' (بالأولى والأول) and 'without primacy or priority' (بلا أولى ولا أول).
2
Identify the logical structure
The philosophers claim: unequal commonality → not a constituent part → not a genus → no composition. Trace this chain.
3
Read the theologian's response
Read the second part: The theologian applies the exact same reasoning to necessity (الوجوب). If the philosophers' logic holds, then necessity also cannot be a genus, which would undermine the theologians' earlier argument.
The response begins with 'قيل : هذا بعينه في الوجوب جوابنا'.
4
Understand the symmetry
Reflect on why this rebuttal is effective: it shows that the philosophers' objection is self-defeating if applied consistently.
This is a classic argumentative technique in kalam: using the opponent's own logic against them.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قالت الفلاسفة : الوجود ليس يعم القسمين بالسوية، بل هو في أحدهما بالأولى والأول، وفي الثاني بلا أولى ولا أول، فلم يكن جزا مقوما، فلم يصلح أن يكون جنسا، فلم يلزم التركيب منه والتقوم به. قيل : هذا بعينه في الوجوب جوابنا، فإن الوجوب ليس يعم القسمين بالسوية، فلم يكن الوجوب جزا مقوما، فلم يصلح أن يكون جنسا، فلم يلزم التركيب والتقوم به.
The philosophers said: Existence does not encompass the two divisions equally. Rather, in one of them it is primary and prior, and in the second it is without primacy or priority. So it is not a constituent part, so it cannot be a genus, so composition from it and subsistence by it do not follow. It was said: This very same thing applies to wajub in our answer. For wajub does not encompass the two divisions equally, so it is not a constituent part, so it cannot be a genus, so composition and subsistence by it do not follow.
Source pages p. 200

Commentary
This passage is a second objection-and-answer exchange. The philosophers refine their previous argument: they claim that existence (al-wujud) is not a true genus because it does not apply equally to the necessary and the possible. In the necessary being it applies primarily and prior (bi al-ula wa al-awwal), while in the possible it is secondary (bila ula wa la awwal). Since it is not common in the same way, it cannot be a constitutive part (juz' muqawwim) or a genus (jins), so no composition from existence follows. The theologian responds by applying the exact same logic to necessity (al-wujub): necessity is also not common equally between the two divisions; it is only in the necessary being. Thus necessity cannot be a genus or a constitutive part either. The objection is thus neutralized: if the philosophers argue that existence's lack of equal commonality prevents composition, then the same reasoning prevents necessity from implying composition, which is the basis of the theologians' earlier argument. The exchange ends with the theologian's successful rebuttal.

Key Terms
عموم
أولى
أول
جزء مقوم
جنس

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: One might think that existence being primary in God disproves composition from existence.
Correction: This passage shows that the same applies to necessity, so the argument is symmetrical and inconclusive. The theologian's rebuttal preserves the possibility of composition from necessity.

Checkpoint
How does the theologian respond to the philosophers' claim that existence is not common equally between necessary and possible?
A) He accepts the claim and redefines existence.
B) He argues that necessity also is not common equally, so the same logic applies.
C) He denies that existence is primary in the necessary being.
D) He asserts that existence is indeed common equally despite the philosophers' claim.

Key Takeaway
The theologian's rebuttal shows that if unequal commonality invalidates existence as a genus, it equally invalidates necessity, thereby preserving the argument for divine simplicity.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?

?
When someone challenges your beliefs with a criticism, do you respond by mirroring their logic honestly like the theologian, or do you become defensive and overlook flaws in your own position? What will you do the next time you face a challenge?
?
The theologian used the philosophers' own argument against them. How can you adopt this practice of intellectual honesty in your own discussions, especially when defending your faith? What is one relationship where you need to apply this principle starting today?

Distinction Requires an Attribute
Shahrastani argues for the necessity of an attribute to distinguish the Necessary Being.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand Shahrastani's logical argument that the Necessary Being must have a distinguishing attribute because a universal existence cannot become specific without a specific existential attribute.

Core Idea
Distinction demands an attribute; universality cannot specify itself.

Shahrastani presents a logical argument to establish that God must possess attributes distinct from His essence. He begins by noting that both existence and self-subsistent essence are common to the Necessary and possible beings. Therefore, something beyond mere essence must account for God's uniqueness. This passage shows why attributes are not superfluous but rationally necessary.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic and translation to grasp the argument's flow. Notice the conditional structure and the logical steps.
This is a dense logical argument; take your time.
2
Identify the key premises
The argument rests on the commonality of existence and essence. See how Shahrastani uses these to force the need for an attribute.
3
Follow the logical progression
Walk through each conditional statement: if no attribute, then no distinction; if distinction by essence, then why not by existence? The conclusion is that a specifying attribute is necessary.
Pay attention to the 'if...then' structure.
4
Connect to the broader debate
Understand how this supports the Ash'ari view that attributes are real and distinct, against those who deny distinct attributes.
Consider the opponents' position mentioned.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وما ذكرتموه من تفريع مذهبكم على هذه القاعدة نتكلم عليها بعد هذه المسألة إن شاء الله تعالى ونقول: إذا كان الوجود من حيث هو وجود يعم الواجب والممكن فالذات القائم بالنفس من حيث إنه ذات قائم بالنفس يعم الواجب والممكن، فما الذي يخص الواجب من أمر وراء ذاته حتى يتميز عن سائر الذوات، فإن لم يكن له ما يخصه فليس له ما يعمه، وإن كان التخصيص وقع بالذات فهلا قلتم: وقع التخصيص بالوجود دون الوجوب، وإن وقع التخصيص بأمر دون أمر فذلك الأمر الذي خصص وعين هو الصفة عندنا إلا أنا بحكم الشرع أطلقنا اسم العلم والقدرة والإرادة على ذلك، وأنتم ما خالفتمونا في العلم والقدرة، بل قلتم: هو تعالى عالم لذاته، وذاته علم، وإن كان الذات من حيث هو ذات شاملا شمول الوجود والتخصيص إنما يقع بأمر وصفة، وبالجملة كل أمر عام وجودي إذا تخصص فإنما يتخصص بأمر خاص وجودي، فيلزم هناك اثنينية من المعنى عام وخاص وسواء كان العام جنسًا والخاص فصلا، وسواء لم يكن الأمر كذلك.
And what you mentioned of deriving your school from this principle—we will speak on it after this issue, God willing—and we say: If existence as existence is common to the Necessary and the possible, then the self-subsistent essence as a self-subsistent essence is common to the Necessary and the possible. So what is it that specifically characterizes the Necessary from something beyond its essence so that it is distinguished from other essences? If it has nothing that characterizes it, then it has nothing that is common to it. And if characterization occurred by the essence itself, then why did you not say: characterization occurred by existence without necessity? And if characterization occurred by a certain thing rather than another, then that thing which characterized and specified is the attribute according to us, except that by the ruling of the Law we apply the names 'knowledge,' 'power,' and 'will' to that. And you have not opposed us concerning knowledge and power; rather you said: He, exalted, is knowing by His essence, and His essence is knowledge. And if the essence as essence is inclusive of existence, and characterization occurs only by a thing and an attribute, then in summary, every universal existential thing, when it becomes specific, it becomes specific only by a specific existential thing, so there is a duality of meaning: universal and specific, whether the universal is genus and the specific differentia, or whether it is not so.
Source pages p. 207

Commentary
This passage is a proof-based argument. The author addresses opponents who might have derived their position from a principle. He argues: if existence is common to the Necessary and the possible (a shared universal), and self-subsistent essence is also common, then what distinguishes the Necessary Being? If nothing distinguishes it, it has no commonality either. If the distinction is by the essence itself, why not say distinction is by existence without necessity? But if distinction is by one thing rather than another, that distinguishing thing is an attribute. The author notes that even if opponents say God is knowing by His essence, they still admit a distinction; but that distinction must be by an attribute. Since a universal existential thing can only be specified by a specific existential thing, a duality of universal and specific follows. This supports the Ash'ari position that attributes are distinct from essence.

Key Terms
وجود
واجب
ممكن
ذات قائم بالنفس
تخصيص
صفة
علم
قدرة
إرادة

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The argument denies God's simplicity by positing multiple eternal entities.
Correction: The argument does not deny simplicity but shows logical necessity for distinction; attributes are not separate entities but real aspects of God's essence.

Checkpoint
According to the argument, what must be the case for the Necessary Being to be distinguished from possible beings?
A) It must have a negative attribute.
B) It must have a specific existential attribute.
C) It must be identical to existence.
D) It must have no attributes.

Key Takeaway
The Necessary Being requires a specific existential attribute to be distinct from possible beings, and this attribute is what Muslims call knowledge, power, and will.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Do you truly believe that God’s attributes—His knowledge, power, and will—are real and active in your life right now, or do you treat them as abstract concepts? How would your daily choices change if you internalized that His attribute of knowledge sees every thought you have?
?
This argument shows that God must have a distinguishing attribute. Have you ever considered that your own existence is only possible because of His specific attribute of creation? How does that realization make you accountable to Him in a way you have been avoiding?
?
If God’s essence includes a real attribute of power, then He is actively sustaining you at every moment. Are you living as if you are independent of Him, or are you relying on His power in your weakness? What one area of your life do you need to surrender to His power today?

Allah's Existence
intermediate
5 min
Responding to Ontological Order: The Dilemma of Evil
Author challenges opponent's ontology of order, asks why not also admit opposition, then presents dilemma about source of evil.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the author's argument that admitting order in existence logically compels one to also admit opposition (good and evil), and the resulting dilemma about the source of evil.

Core Idea
If order exists, why not opposition? The source of evil traps the opponent in a contradiction.

The author now responds directly to the opponent's ontology. He cleverly turns their own admission of hierarchy against them, asking why they do not also admit a fundamental opposition of good and evil. He then invokes scriptural evidence and presents a devastating dilemma that leaves no consistent answer for the opponent.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and identify the challenge.
Notice how the author starts by accepting the opponent's premise of order, then asks for consistency: why not opposition? Read the Arabic and translation.
The author uses the language of 'order' (ترتيب) and 'opposition' (تضاد).
2
Examine the appeal to revelation and dualists.
The author brings up angels (good) and devils (evil) and notes that dualists (light/darkness) would agree with this. Understand how this forces the opponent to confront religious data.
Dualists are mentioned as an example of those who admit two principles.
3
Analyze the three-pronged dilemma.
The author presents three possibilities for the source of beings: no source (atheism), pure good source, pure evil source. Each leads to a contradiction. Work through each to see why the opponent has no escape.
The dilemma is designed to show that the opponent's system cannot account for evil.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فنقول : أثبتم ترتيبا في الوجود حتى قضيتم بأن الوجود في بعض الموجودات أول وأولى، وفي بعضها لا أول ولا أولى، فهلا أثبتم فيه تضادًا أيضا حتى تحكموا بأن الوجود في بعض الموجودات خير كله وفي بعضها شر كله، وقد سمعتم من أصحاب الشرائع إثبات الملائكة الروحانيين وذلك خير كله، وإثبات الشياطين وهذا شر كله وأصحاب الأصلين النور والظلمة يقرون عليكم السلام للإلزام وأصحاب الكلام يلزمونكم إثبات موجودات العالم غير مستندة إلى الموجد كأنها وقعت اتفاقا لا قصدًا، أو حدثت فلتة لا إرادة واختيارًا، فإن كانت لا مستند لها فهي مخلوقة لا خالق لها، وإن كان مستندها خيرًا محضا، فكيف يصدر الشر من الخير؟ وإن كان مستندها شراً محضاً فالشر المحض لا وجود له عندكم، فما الجواب؟ وكيف الخروج عن عهدة الخطاب؟
So we say: You have established order in existence such that you judged that existence in some beings is first and prior, and in some it is neither first nor prior. So why do you not also establish opposition in it, so that you judge that existence in some beings is entirely good and in some entirely evil? And you have heard from the people of the revealed laws the affirmation of the spiritual angels, and that is entirely good, and the affirmation of the devils, and this is entirely evil. And the proponents of the two principles (light and darkness) acknowledge you in salutation for refutation, and the proponents of kalam (theologians) oblige you to affirm that the beings of the world are not dependent on the Effector, as if they occurred by chance without purpose, or happened spontaneously without will and choice. For if they have no source, then they are created without a creator; and if their source is pure good, how does evil issue from good? And if their source is pure evil, then pure evil has no existence according to you. What is the answer? And how to extricate oneself from the burden of discourse?
Source pages p. 256

Commentary
This passage is a direct response to the opponent's view. The author first points out that the opponent admits a hierarchy (order) in existence, with some beings more prior than others. If they can admit order, why not also admit opposition (تضاد) such that some beings are entirely good and others entirely evil? The author then appeals to revealed religion: angels are entirely good, devils entirely evil. He also mentions the dualist schools (light and darkness) who would accept this. He then uses a dilemma: either the beings of the world have no source (then they are uncaused, which implies atheism), or their source is pure good (then how does evil come from good?) or pure evil (but pure evil does not exist in your system). Thus the opponent is trapped in a contradiction.

Key Terms
ترتيب (order)
تضاد (opposition)
الملائكة الروحانيين (spiritual angels)
الشياطين (devils)
أصلان (two principles)
مستند (source)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The author is arguing that good and evil are two independent principles, like in Zoroastrianism.
Correction: No, the author is not endorsing dualism. He is using the opponent's own admission of order to force them to accept opposition, and then showing the absurd consequences of that position. He is a theist arguing against a particular ontology.

Checkpoint
What dilemma does the author present to the opponent regarding the source of evil?
A) Either evil is self-caused or God creates it directly.
B) Either the world is uncreated, or its source is good (then evil is inexplicable) or evil (then it is nonexistent).
C) Either evil is an illusion or it is necessary.
D) Either God is good but powerless, or evil is impossible.

Key Takeaway
Admitting order in existence leads to the question of opposition, and the author shows that no consistent account of the source of evil can be given by the opponent.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you experience or witness evil in the world, does your heart immediately look for a source? Do you blame yourself, others, or God? How does this lesson's dilemma expose the insufficiency of any answer that does not begin with divine wisdom and decree?
?
The author leaves the opponent with no escape. But you, as a believer, must have an answer. Have you truly settled in your soul that everything—including evil—ultimately comes from Allah with wisdom? Or is there a hidden doubt that conditions your trust in Him?
?
This passage presses you to choose: either the world is meaningless (no source) or the source is pure good but evil is a puzzle. But you know the revealed answer. Are you living as if evil has no purpose, or do you actively seek the wisdom behind trials in your own life? What concrete step will you take today to trust the all-good Source even when you face opposition?
